Return of Authorization After Slave Returns
Clarifies that previous authorization does not automatically restore when slave returns after absconding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the restoration of a slave's trading authorization after absconding and explain why a new permission is required.

Core Idea
A revoked authorization does not revive automatically; new permission is required.

This passage, introduced as a fa'ida (benefit) by the commentator, addresses a scenario not explicitly covered by Imam Muhammad: what happens when a ma'dhun (permitted to trade) slave who absconded later returns? The relied-upon view is clear: the previous authorization is nullified and does not revive. This highlights the Hanafi principle that once a legal permission is voided by an event, its restoration requires explicit action by the authorizing party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
A ma'dhun slave (one permitted to trade by his master) absconds (runs away). He later returns to his master.
2
Recognize the Legal Issue
Does the original permission to trade automatically become valid again upon the slave's return? This is the core question.
Absconding is considered a rebellion and nullifies the permission.
3
Note Imam Muhammad's Silence
Imam Muhammad did not explicitly rule on this point in the Zahir al-Riwayah texts.
This gap necessitates reliance on the later established view.
4
State the Correct View
The correct (sahih) view is that the authorization does NOT return. It remains null and void.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position.
5
Understand the Legal Implication
The master must issue a new explicit permission if he wishes the slave to resume trading. Any transactions conducted by the slave without fresh permission are invalid.
Nullification is not reversed merely by cessation of the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدہ... اگر ما دون غلام بھاگنے کے بعد لوٹ آئے تو اجازت سابقہ لوٹ آئے گی یا نہیں؟ اس کو امام محمد نے ذکر نہیں کیا اور صحیح یہ ہے کہ اجازت عود نہیں کرے گی ۔
Benefit: If a ma'dhun slave returns after absconding, will the previous authorization return or not? Imam Muhammad did not mention it. The correct view is that the authorization will not return.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This is a fa'ida (benefit) added by the commentator as supplementary guidance. The issue is whether the original authorization that was revoked due to absconding becomes effective again when the slave returns. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani did not explicitly rule on this point, but the established correct view (as-sahih) is that the authorization does not return automatically. This means the master must issue a new permission if he wishes the slave to resume trading. The reasoning is that the original permission was nullified by the absconding, and nullification is not reversed merely by the cessation of the cause.

Key Terms
عود (ʿawd): Return; here it refers to the restoration of the previous authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a ma'dhun slave who absconded later returns, what happens to his previous authorization?
A) It automatically revives.
B) It remains invalid; new permission is needed.
C) It revives only if the master explicitly says so.
D) It is up to the slave to decide.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that the authorization does not return; a fresh permission from the master is necessary.

Interdiction of a Ma'dhuna Slave upon Becoming Umm Walad
Ruling that birth of child imposes interdiction and master's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a ma'dhuna slave who becomes umm walad, explain the role of custom ('urf) in imposing interdiction, and compare the Hanafi view with that of Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
Customary seclusion after birth revokes trading permission unless explicitly renewed.

When a female slave authorized to trade gives birth to her master's child, does her trading authorization continue? Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, in the passage from al-Hidayah, presents a dispute with Imam Zufar. The Hanafi school holds that her status changes: the master's protective custom indicates he no longer wishes her to mix with traders. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequences for creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is the act under discussion? A ma'dhuna slave (authorized to trade) gives birth to her master's child (becomes umm walad). The ruling: this birth is a form of interdiction (hijr) upon her, i.e., she can no longer trade without new permission.
The ruling is immediate upon birth, not at the master's death.
2
State the Dispute
Who disagrees and why? Imam Zufar says continuation is based on initiation: since the master could permit her initially, the permission should remain. The Hanafi view disagrees.
Zufar's analogy: al-baqa' bil-ibtida'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Proof
The Hanafi proof rests on custom ('urf). After childbirth, the master will naturally seclude her (yahsinuha). This customary seclusion is a 'dalalat al-hajr' – a strong indication that he intends to revoke permission. Unlike initiation, where explicit permission (sarih) overrides custom, here no explicit renewal is given; custom becomes the default indicator.
The passage says: 'al-sarih qadin 'ala al-dalalah' – explicit overrules indication.
4
Examine Liability for Creditors
If the slave had debts from her trading, and then becomes umm walad (making her unsellable), the master becomes liable for her value to the creditors. Why? He destroyed the nexus (mahall) that was the creditors' recourse, as sale is now prevented by istilad. He must pay her value (qimah) to satisfy them.
This is a compensation for the lost right of creditors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا ولدت الماذون لها من مولاها فذلك حجر عليها خلافا لزفر وهو يعتبر البقاء بالابتداء ولنا ان الظاهر انه يحصنها بعد الولادة فيكون دلالة الحجر عادة بخلاف الابتداء لان الصريح قاض على الدلالة ويضمن المولى قيمتها ان ركبتها ديون لاتلافه محلا تعلق به حق الغرماء اذ به يمتنع البيع وبه يقضى حقهم ترجمہ: اگر مادونہ باندی نے اپنے آقا سے بچہ جنا تو یہ اس پر حجر ہے۔ برخلاف امام زفر کے وہ بقاء کو ابتداء پر قیاس کرتے ہیں۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ آقا اس کو لوگوں کے میل جول سے محفوظ رکھے گا۔ پس یہ از راہ عادت حجر کی دلیل ہے۔ بخلاف ابتداء کے کیونکہ صریح اجازت دلالت پر حاکم ہوتی ہے اور ضامن ہو گا آقا اس کی قیمت کا اگر اس پر قرض چڑھ گئے ہوں اسکے ایسے محل کو تلف کر دینے کی وجہ سے جس کے ساتھ قرض خواہوں کا حق متعلق تھا۔ کیونکہ استمیلاد کی وجہ سے اس کی بیع ممتنع ہو گئی۔ حالانکہ بیع ہی سے ان کا حق پکایا جاتا ہے۔
He said: And when a ma'dhuna female slave gives birth from her master, that is interdiction upon her, contrary to Zufar who analogizes continuation to initiation. And for us, the apparent (custom) is that he will protect her after childbirth, so it is a customary indication of interdiction, unlike initiation, because the explicit (permission) overrules the indication. And the master guarantees her value if debts mount upon her, because he destroyed a nexus to which the creditors' right was attached, since sale is prevented (by her being umm walad) and by sale their right is recovered. Translation: If a ma'dhuna female slave gives birth to her master's child, this is interdiction upon her. Contrary to Imam Zufar, who analogizes continuation to initiation. Our proof is that it is apparent that the master will protect her from mixing with people. So this is a customary indication of interdiction. Unlike initiation, because explicit permission overrules indication. And the master will guarantee her value if debts are incurred upon her, due to his destroying a nexus to which the creditors' right was attached, because istilad prevents her sale, while the creditors' right is recovered through sale.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute with Imam Zufar regarding whether a ma'dhuna slave who gives birth to her master's child becomes interdicted (mahjur). The Hanafi position is that she does become interdicted because the master will customarily (urf) wish to protect her from public dealings after she becomes the mother of his child (umm walad). This customary seclusion is a strong indication (dalalah al-hijr) that the master no longer consents to her trading. Imam Zufar argues that since the master could initially permit such a slave to trade (initiation), the permission should continue by analogy. The Hanafi response is that the custom after childbirth changes: the master will naturally keep her secluded, unlike a slave who is not an umm walad. However, if the master explicitly permits her to trade after childbirth, that explicit permission overrides the customary indication. The passage also addresses the master's liability: if the slave incurred debts while ma'dhuna, and then becomes umm walad (making her unsellable), the master is liable to the creditors for her value, because his act of istilad (proclaiming paternity) destroyed the asset that was the creditors' recourse.

Key Terms
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears her master's child and is thereafter free upon the master's death and cannot be sold.
دلالة الحجر (dalālat al-ḥajr): Customary indication that the master intends to impose interdiction.
مأذونة (ma'dhūna): A female slave who has been given permission (idhn) by her master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi proof that a ma'dhuna slave becomes interdicted upon giving birth to her master's child?
A) Qiyas with a freed slave.
B) Explicit statement from the master.
C) Customary seclusion indicating revocation.
D) The child's right to inherit.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhuna slave becomes interdicted upon becoming umm walad due to customary seclusion, and the master must compensate creditors for her value if she had debts.

Effect of Tadbir on a Ma'dhuna Slave's Authorization
Ruling that tadbir does not impose interdiction on a ma'dhuna slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that tadbir does not revoke a ma'dhuna slave's authorization and explain the reasoning based on custom and absence of contradiction.

Core Idea
Tadbir does not revoke trade authorization because custom does not seclude mudabbara slaves and no contradiction exists between tadbir and permission.

In this passage from al-Hidayah, the author addresses a specific scenario: a ma'dhuna slave incurs debts exceeding her value, then the master makes her mudabbara. The question is whether this new status revokes her authorization to trade. The Hanafi school clarifies that it does not, and provides the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The scenario: a ma'dhuna slave borrows more than her value, then the master makes her mudabbara. The ruling: she remains ma'dhuna as before.
2
Analyze the primary proof
The proof is the absence of any indication of interdiction (dalalat al-hajr). The author states that custom does not require secluding a mudabbara slave, meaning there is no customary implication that tadbir revokes permission.
Focus on the word 'عادة' (custom).
3
Understand the role of custom
Custom is the basis: it is not customary for masters to seclude mudabbara slaves; they are allowed to mix freely. Therefore, tadbir does not signal a desire to restrict dealings.
Contrast with umm walad, where custom of seclusion exists.
4
Recognize the master's liability
The master is guarantor for the slave's value because tadbir prevents sale of the slave to cover debts. This is analogized to the umm walad case.
Liability arises from the master's act that removes the slave from being sold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و اذا استدانت الامة الماذون لها اكثر من قيمتها فدبرها المولى فهى ماذون لها على حالها لانعدام دلالة الحجر اذ العادة ما جرت بتحصين المدبرة ولا منافاة بين حكميهما ايضا والمولى ضامن لقيمتها لما قررناه في ام الولد ترجمہ: اگر ماذونہ باندی نے اپنی قیمت سے زیادہ ادھار لیا پھر آقا نے اس کو مد برہ بنا دیا تو وہ اپنے حال پر ماذونہ رہے گی دلالت حجر معدوم ہونے کی وجہ سے کیونکہ مد برہ کو محفوظ رکھنے کی عادت جاری نہیں۔ اور دونوں کے حکم میں کوئی منافات بھی نہیں ہے اور آقا اس کی قیمت کا ضامن ہو گا اسی دلیل سے جو ہم نے ام ولد میں بیان کی ہے۔
He said: And when a ma'dhuna female slave borrows more than her value, then the master makes her mudabbara, she remains ma'dhuna as she was, due to the absence of any indication of interdiction, because custom does not require secluding a mudabbara. There is also no contradiction between their rulings. And the master is guarantor for her value. Translation: If a ma'dhuna female slave borrows more than her value, then the master makes her mudabbara, she remains ma'dhuna as before, due to the absence of any indication of interdiction, because there is no custom of secluding a mudabbara; they are allowed to mix with people. There is also no contradiction between the rulings of tadbir and permission. And the master is the guarantor for her value, by the same reasoning we stated regarding umm walad.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a ma'dhuna slave incurs debts exceeding her value, and then the master makes her mudabbara (promised freedom after the master's death). The question is whether this tadbir imposes interdiction (hajr) on her. The Hanafi ruling is that it does not; she continues to be ma'dhuna. The reason is that there is no customary indication (dalalah al-hajr) of interdiction for a mudabbara—unlike an umm walad, it is not customary for masters to seclude a mudabbara slave; rather, they are allowed to mix freely and can engage in trade. Additionally, there is no inherent conflict between the ruling of tadbir (which grants future freedom) and the ruling of permission (which allows transactions). However, the master is liable for her value if debts are incurred because his act of tadbir prevents the creditors from selling her to recover their debt (since a mudabbara cannot be sold). This liability is analogous to the case of umm walad explained earlier.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A contract by which a master promises that a slave will be free after the master's death.
مدبرة (mudabbara): A female slave who has been given tadbir; she will be free upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a ma'dhuna slave borrows more than her value and then the master makes her mudabbara, what is the Hanafi ruling on her authorization?
A) It is revoked.
B) It remains valid.
C) It is conditional on the master's permission.
D) It is revoked after the master's death.

Key Takeaway
Tadbir does not impose interdiction on a ma'dhuna slave; her authorization remains valid because no custom of seclusion applies and there is no conflict between the two rulings.

Confession of an Interdicted Ma'dhun Slave
Dispute on validity of confession by a ma'dhun slave after interdiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of a ma'dhun slave's confession after interdiction and explain the legal reasoning (dalil) behind each view.

Core Idea
An interdicted slave's hand may still hold legal weight: Abu Hanifa validates confession for wealth in his possession.

In Hanafi fiqh, a ma'dhun slave is one authorized to trade. When the master places him under interdiction (hajr), a legal question arises: does the slave's confession (iqrar) regarding property still bind? This passage presents a sharp intra-madhhab disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), on whether factual possession (yadd) retains legal effect after interdiction. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi approach to capacity, possession, and the limits of interdiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a confession (iqrar) made by a ma'dhun slave after the master has placed him under interdiction. According to Abu Hanifa, this confession is valid specifically for wealth physically in the slave's hand. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), it is invalid entirely.
The disagreement is over the scope of validity, not the definition of iqrar.
2
Locate the validifier (musahhih) for each view
The Sahibayn argue that the confession's validity depends either on authorization (idhn) or on hand (yadd). Interdiction removes authorization and renders the hand legally irrelevant. Abu Hanifa argues that the validifier is the hand itself, which remains factually present; interdiction only nullifies it if the hand is empty of need.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between factual possession and legal recognition.
3
Trace the dalil for each side
The Sahibayn use a qiyas: the hand of a mahjur is like the hand of a child or insane person—legally disregarded. They also analogize to the case where the master takes the wealth from the slave's hand. Abu Hanifa responds that the slave's hand is not empty; the confession itself proves that the hand contains property, so interdiction does not nullify its legal effect.
The argument hinges on the condition of the hand being empty of need.
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the slave's creditors can benefit from his confession because the confessed property can be taken to satisfy debts. Under the Sahibayn's view, the confession is void, so creditors have no claim via that confession.
The practical consequence affects third-party rights.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text presents Abu Hanifa's argument in detail, suggesting it is the stronger view. Later Hanafi fatwa often prefers Abu Hanifa's position, though the Sahibayn's view is also authoritative.
Both positions are part of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فاذا حجر على الماذون فاقراره جائز فيما في يده من المال عند ابي حنيفة ومعناه ان يقر بما في يده انه امانة لغيره او غصب منه او يقر بدين عليه فيقضى مما في يده وقال ابويوسف ومحمد لا يجوز اقراره لهما ان المصحح لاقراره ان كان هو الاذن فقد زال بالحجر ان كان اليد فالحجر ابطلها لان يد المحجور غير معتبرة وصار كما اذا اخذ المولى كسبه من يده قبل اقراره او ثبت حجره بالبيع من غيره ولهذا لا يصح اقراره في حق الرقبة بعد الحجر وله ان المصحح هو اليد ولهذا لا يصح اقرار الماذون فيما اخذه المولى من يده واليد باقية حقيقة وشرط بطلانها بالحجر حكما فراغها عن حاجته واقراره دلیل تحققها بخلاف ما اذا انتزعه المولى من يده قبل الاقرار لان يد المولى ثابتة حقيقة وحكما فلا تبطل باقراره وكذا ملكه ثابت في رقبته فلا يبطل باقراره من غير رضاه وهذا بخلاف ما اذا باعه لان العبد قد تبدل بتبدل الملك على ما عرف فلا يبقى ما ثبت بحكم الملك ولهذا لم يكن خصما فيما باشره قبل البيع
He said: When interdiction is imposed on a ma'dhun, his confession (iqrar) is valid regarding the wealth that is in his hand according to Abu Hanifa. Meaning, he may confess that what is in his hand is an amanah (trust) for another, or a usurpation (ghasb) from him, or confess a debt upon him, and it will be satisfied from what is in his hand. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: His confession is not valid. For them, the validifier of his confession, if it was the authorization, it has ceased by interdiction; if it was the hand (possession), interdiction nullifies it because the hand of a mahjur is not considered. And it becomes like where the master takes his earnings from his hand before his confession, or his interdiction is established by sale to another, and therefore his confession regarding the slave's body (raqaba) is not valid after interdiction. For him (Abu Hanifa), the validifier is the hand, and therefore the confession of a ma'dhun regarding what the master took from his hand is not valid, while the hand remains in reality. The condition of its nullification by interdiction legally is its being devoid of his need, and his confession is proof of its reality. Contrary to when the master takes it from his hand before confession, because the master's hand is established in reality and legally, so it is not nullified by his confession. Similarly, his ownership of the body is established, so it is not nullified by his confession without his consent. And this is unlike when he sells him, because the slave changes with change of ownership, as known, so what was established by virtue of ownership does not remain, and therefore he is not a contestant regarding what he did before the sale.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa on one side and Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) on the other, regarding the validity of a confession (iqrar) made by a ma'dhun slave after he has been placed under interdiction (hajr). The context is that the slave had been authorized to trade, then the master revoked that authorization by placing him under interdiction. Now, does the slave's confession regarding the wealth that remains in his hand (e.g., acknowledging it belongs to someone else) hold legal weight? According to Abu Hanifa, such a confession is valid and binding for the wealth in his hand, meaning the wealth is subject to the confession (e.g., to be given to the claimant). But if the master had taken the wealth from the slave's hand before the confession, then the confession is not valid. The Sahibayn argue that the confession is not valid at all after interdiction, because either the basis (authorization) is gone, or the hand (possession) is no longer legally recognized due to interdiction. Abu Hanifa explains that the hand remains a factual possession, and the condition for its legal nullification is that it is empty of the slave's need. The slave's confession actually proves that the hand is not empty of need. Furthermore, the master's ownership of the slave's person (raqaba) is not affected by the slave's confession without the master's consent. The Sahibayn's view is that since the slave is under interdiction, his hand is like that of a child or insane person—legally irrelevant. However, Abu Hanifa distinguishes: the fact that the slave still physically holds the property means his hand retains some legal effect. The mufta bihi view is believed to be that of Abu Hanifa, though later authorities may have different preferences.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): Confession or admission; a declaration acknowledging a right of another, e.g., that property is a trust or a debt is owed.
يد (yadd): Hand; legal possession or control over property.
يد المحجور (yadd al-mahjur): The possession of an interdicted person; considered legally deficient but not absolutely void according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what condition must be absent for the hand of an interdicted slave to be legally nullified?
A) The slave must have confessed.
B) The hand must be empty of the slave's need.
C) The master must have consented.
D) The wealth must be in the form of debt.

Key Takeaway
An interdicted slave's confession regarding wealth in his factual possession is valid according to Abu Hanifa because the hand remains legally significant unless empty of need.

Effect of Istilad and Tadbir on the Ma'dhunah's Permission to Trade
Explains the effect of istilad and tadbir on a ma'dhunah's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a ma'dhunah becomes interdicted upon becoming umm walad or mudabbirah, and evaluate the opposing view of Imam Zufar and the Three Imams.

Core Idea
istilad interdicts a ma'dhunah due to custom of seclusion; tadbir does not.

A ma'dhunah is a female slave permitted by her master to trade. Her legal capacity becomes fragile if the master fathers her child (istilad) or promises her freedom after his death (tadbir). The Hanafi school distinguishes between the two: istilad automatically interdicts her from further transactions unless the master explicitly re-permits, while tadbir leaves her permission intact. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this distinction and the master's liability when his action destroys creditors' rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: a ma'dhunah who becomes umm walad (istilad), and a ma'dhunah who becomes mudabbirah (tadbir). For each, note the ruling on her permission (interdicted or not) and the master's liability.
The first scenario involves a debt exceeding the slave's price; the second also involves credit purchases.
2
State the Hanafi position and the opposing view
The Hanafi school holds that istilad interdicts the ma'dhunah (she becomes mahjurah), while tadbir does not. Imam Zufar and the Three Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) disagree on istilad, arguing it does not contradict the initial permission.
The opposing view relies on the fact that the master can explicitly permit even after istilad.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil aqli)
The Hanafi proof for interdiction in istilad is based on custom (urf): umm walad are customarily kept in seclusion and not allowed to mix in markets, indicating the master's implicit withdrawal of permission. For tadbir, there is no such custom; mudabbirah are left to mix, so no interdiction.
Explicit permission after istilad overrides the implied interdiction because explicit text (sarih) is stronger than implication (dalalah).
4
Determine the master's liability
If the master's action (istilad or tadbir) destroys the creditors' recourse to the slave's person (e.g., istilad makes her unsellable), the master guarantees the lower of her price or the debt amount.
The liability is wajib (obligatory compensation) because he destroyed a source of right (itlaf al-mahal).
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the Hanafi view, creditors can claim the price from the master immediately upon istilad; under the opposing view, they cannot unless the master's explicit revocation. In tadbir, all schools agree the ma'dhunah remains permitted, so creditors continue dealing with her.
This affects the risk assessment for creditors when extending credit to a ma'dhunah.

قیمت کا ضامن ہو گا اسی دلیل سے جو ہم نے ام ولد میں بیان کی ہے۔ تشریح قوله واذا ولدت الخ- ایک باندی مازو نہ تھی آقا نے اس سے ولی کی اور اس سے بچہ ہوا آقا نے بچہ کا دعویٰ کیا تو باندی اس کی اتم ولد ہوگئی۔ اب وہ استیلا د کی وجہ سے مجور التصرف ہو جائے گی مگر ولالہ ۔ امام زفر اور ائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک مجور نہ ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ استبیلا دابتداء اذن کے منافی نہیں۔ کیونکہ آقا اپنی ام ولد کو تجارت کی اجازت دے سکتا ہے تو بقاء بطریق اولی منافی نہ ہوگی ہم یہ کہتے ہیں کہ ام ولد عادہ پردہ میں رہتی ہے اور خرید و فروخت کے سلسلہ میں مالک اس کے نکلنے اور لوگوں کے ساتھ اختلاط کرنے سے راضی نہیں ہوتا تو یہ اس کے مجور ہونے کی دلیل ہے ہاں اگر مالک استلاد کے بعد اذن تجارت کی تصریح کر دے تو حجر ثابت نہ ہوگا کیونکہ صراحت دلالت سے قوی تر ہے۔
قوله ويضمن المولى الخ اور مذکورہ ماذونہ باندی کا آقا اس کی قیمت کا تاوان دیگا۔ کیونکہ قرض خواہوں کا حق باندی کی ذات سے متعلق ہو چکا تھا اور وہ اس کو فروخت کرا کر وصول کر سکتے تھے اور جب آقا نے اس کو ام ولد بنالیا تو استیفاء، حق متعد رہو گیا۔ کیونکہ ام ولد کی بیج درست نہیں۔ پس آقا نے استیلاد کے ذریعہ سے ان کا محل حق تلف کر دیا۔ لہذا قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا۔
قوله واذا استدانت .... الخ اگر بازو نہ باندی نے اپنی قیمت سے زیادہ مال ادھار کے طور پر خر یدا پھر آ قا نے اس کو مد برہ کر دیا تو تدبیر سے حجر ثابت نہ ہوگا بلکہ وہ اپنے حال پر ماذونہ رہے گی۔ کیونکہ یہاں مجور ہونے کی کوئی دلالت نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کہ مد برہ کو پردہ میں رکھنے اور اختلاط سے محفوظ رکھنے کی عادت نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ بدستور لوگوں میں خلط ملط کے ساتھ چھوڑ دیتے ہیں تو وہ تجارت کر سکتی ہے۔ نیز اذن وتدبیر کے حکم میں کوئی منافات بھی نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کہ حکم تدبیر یہ ہے کہ فی الحال حق حریت اور فی المال حقیقت حریت ثابت ہوتی ہے اور حکم اذن فک حجر ہے اور حق حریت مانع فکاک حجر نہیں ہے۔ پھر آقا اس مد برہ کی قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا۔ جس کی دلیل اتم ولد والے مسئلہ میں اوپر گذر چکی کہ اس نے ایسے محل کو تلف کر دیا جس کے ساتھ غرماء کا حق وابستہ ہو چکا۔
He will be liable for the price by the same proof we mentioned in the case of umm walad. Explanation: He said: 'And when she gives birth...' A slave-girl was permitted (ma'dhunah); the master had intercourse with her and she gave birth to a child; the master claimed the child, so she became his umm walad. Now due to istilad, she becomes interdicted in transactions – this is our madhhab. According to Imam Zufar and the Three Imams, she will not become interdicted because istilad does not contradict the initial permission, since the master can permit his umm walad to trade, so a fortiori it does not contradict its continuation. We say: The umm walad customarily remains in seclusion, and the master is not pleased with her going out and mixing with people in buying and selling; this is evidence of her interdiction. Yes, if the master explicitly grants permission to trade after istilad, then interdiction does not occur because explicit text is stronger than implication. He said: 'And the master guarantees...' The master of the aforementioned permitted slave-girl pays compensation for her price because the creditors' right was attached to her person, and they could have sold her to recover it. When the master made her umm walad, the recovery of the right became impossible because sale of an umm walad is invalid. Thus, the master destroyed their source of right through istilad, so he is liable for the price. He said: 'And if she purchases on credit...' If a permitted slave-girl purchased goods on credit exceeding her price, then the master made her mudabbirah, then tadbir does not cause interdiction; rather, she remains permitted as before because there is no evidence of interdiction here, since a mudabbirah is not customarily kept in seclusion and protected from mixing; rather, she is left to mix with people, so she can trade. Also, there is no contradiction between permission and tadbir because the ruling of tadbir is that the right to freedom is established immediately while the reality of freedom is established upon death, and the ruling of permission is the removal of interdiction, and the right to freedom does not prevent the removal of interdiction. Then the master will be liable for the price of this mudabbirah, whose proof has already passed in the umm walad case: that he destroyed a source to which the creditors' right was attached.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on two related sub-rulings from the Kitab al-Ma'dhun of Al-Hidayah. The first sub-ruling concerns a ma'dhunah (female slave permitted to trade) who becomes an umm walad (mother of her master's child) through istilad. The author (Marghinani) states that according to the Hanafi school, she becomes interdicted (mahjurah) in transactions because the custom of seclusion for umm walad conflicts with trading activities. Imam Zufar and the Three Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) disagree, arguing that istilad does not contradict the initial permission, since the master can explicitly permit an umm walad to trade later. The Hanafi response is that the default habit of seclusion implies the master's lack of consent to her mixing, so interdiction applies unless the master explicitly re-authorizes trade (which overrides the implication). The second sub-ruling is about a ma'dhunah who purchases goods on credit exceeding her value, then the master makes her mudabbirah (promises freedom after his death). Unlike the umm walad case, tadbir does not cause interdiction because a mudabbirah is not customarily secluded; she continues to mix and trade. Also, the ruling of tadbir (right to freedom) does not contradict the ruling of permission (removal of interdiction). In both cases, if the master's action (istilad or tadbir) destroys the creditors' recourse to the slave's person, the master becomes liable for the slave's price (or the debt amount) because he has destroyed a source to which their right was attached.

Key Terms
مأذونة (ma'dhunah): A female slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade (اذن في التجارة).
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, and the master has acknowledged the child, thus she becomes free upon his death.
مدبرة (mudabbirah): A female slave whom the master has promised freedom after his death (تدبير).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school in this passage, what happens to a ma'dhunah (permitted female slave) when she becomes an umm walad?
A) She remains fully permitted to trade as before.
B) She becomes interdicted (mahjurah) in transactions.
C) She is automatically freed.
D) Her master must sell her immediately.

Key Takeaway
The master's istilad of a ma'dhunah creates interdiction by default due to custom of seclusion; explicit permission overrides it. Tadbir does not cause interdiction because the mudabbirah customarily mixes. The master is liable for the price in both cases if his action destroys creditors' recourse.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Validity of Iqrar by an Interdicted Permitted Slave
Intra-Hanafi dispute on iqrar of a mahjur slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on the validity of an interdicted permitted slave's confession and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Intra-madhhab ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa validates a mahjur slave's confession by istihsan; the Sahibayn reject it by qiyas.

The issue of a slave's capacity after interdiction involves a conflict between strict analogy (qiyas) and juristic preference (istihsan) based on need. The passage examines the iqrar (confession) of a mahjur 'abd ma'dhun regarding property in his hand. We follow the arguments of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, concluding with the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the Hanafi position
The ruling is about the validity of a confession by a permitted slave after his master has placed him under interdiction (hajr). Imam Abu Hanifa holds it valid by istihsan; the Sahibayn hold it invalid by qiyas.
The core question: which factor validates the confession? Permission (idhn) or possession (yad)?
2
Understand the Sahibayn's qiyas argument
The Sahibayn argue that either the permission is gone due to hajr, or the possession is legally nullified because the possession of a mahjur is not considered. They analogize to cases where the master takes the property before confession or sells the slave, where confession is invalid by consensus.
This is the qiyas (analogy) position.
3
Parse Abu Hanifa's istihsan reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the validating factor is actual possession (yad haqiqiyyah), which remains despite hajr. The legal nullification of possession is conditional on the property being surplus to the slave's need; the confession proves need, so the possession remains valid for the purpose of confession.
Istihsan overrides qiyas due to practical necessity.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the property in the slave's hand can be taken to pay the confessed debt. Under the Sahibayn, creditors have no recourse against that property unless the master voluntarily pays.
This practical difference affects the rights of creditors.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's ruling, as it is based on istihsan and gives effect to the slave's need and creditors' rights.
The text states 'Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that... it is istihsan'.

عبد ماذون کو مجبور کیا گیا تو اس کا اقرار جائز ہے اس مال میں جو ا سکے قبضہ میں ہے
قال فاذا حجر على الماذون فاقراره جائز فيما في يده من المال عند ابي حنيفة ومعناه ان يقر بما في يده انه امانة لغيره او غصب منه او يقر بدين عليه فيقضى مما في يده وقال ابويوسف ومحمد لا يجوز اقراره لهما ان المصحح لاقراره ان كان هو الاذن فقد زال بالحجر ان كان اليد فالحجر ابطلها لان يد المحجور غير معتبرة وصار كما اذا اخذ المولى كسبه من يده قبل اقراره او ثبت حجره بالبيع من غيره ولهذا لا يصح اقراره في حق الرقبة بعد الحجر وله ان المصحح هو اليد ولهذا لا يصح اقرار الماذون فيما اخذه المولى من يده واليد باقية حقيقة وشرط بطلانها بالحجر حكما فراغها عن حاجته واقراره دلیل تحققها بخلاف ما اذا انتزعه المولى من يده قبل الاقرار لان يد المولى ثابتة حقيقة وحكما فلا تبطل باقراره وكذا ملكه ثابت في رقبته فلا يبطل باقراره من غير رضاه وهذا بخلاف ما اذا باعه لان العبد قد تبدل بتبدل الملك على ما عرف فلا يبقى ما ثبت بحكم الملك ولهذا لم يكن خصما فيما باشره قبل البيع
ترجمہ جب آقا نے عبد مازون کو مجبور کر دیا تو اس کا اقرار جائز ہے اس مال میں جو اس کے قبضہ میں ہے امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اس کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ اقرار کرے اس مال کی نسبت جو اس کے پاس ہے کہ یہ غیر کی امانت ہے یا اس سے غصب ہے یا خود پر قرضہ کا اقرار کرے کہ اس کے مقبوضہ مال سے ادا کیا جائے ۔ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ اس کا اقرار جائز نہیں۔ ان کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس کے اقرار کو صحیح کرنے والا امراگر ہی اجازت ہے تو حجر کی وجہ سے وہ زائل ہو چکی اور اگر قرضہ ہو تو حجر نے اس کو بھی مٹادیا کیونکہ مجور کا قبضہ معتبر نہیں ہوتا اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا ۔ جیسے آقا نے اس کے اقرار سے پہلے اس کی کمائی اس کے قبضہ سے لے لی یا ایسا ہو گیا۔ جیسے اس کا حجر ثابت ہو گیا ہو دوسرے کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دینے کی وجہ سے اسی لئے حجر کے بعد رقبہ کے حق میں اس کا اقر صحیح نہیں ہوتا۔ امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اقرار کوصحیح کرنے والا قبضہ ہی ہے۔ اسی لئے ماذون کا اقرار صحیح نہیں ہوتا اس مال میں جس کو آقا نے لے لیا ہو اس کے قبضہ سے اور قبضہ حقیقہ باقی ہے اور حجر کی وجہ سے حکما قبضہ باطل ہونے کی شرط اس کی ضرورت سے فارغ ہوتا ہے اور اس کا اقرار کرنا تحقق حاجت کی دلیل ہے بخلاف اس کے جب اقرار سے پہلے آقا نے اس کے قبضہ سے مال نکال لیا کیونکہ آقا کا قبضہ ثابت ہے حقیقہ بھی اور حکما بھی تو یہ قبضہ غلام کے اقرار کی وجہ سے باطل نہ ہوگا۔ ایسے ہی آقا کی ملک بھی اس کے رقبہ میں ثابت ہے تو اس کی رضاء کے بغیر غلام کے اقرار سے باطل نہ ہوگی اور یہ اس کے خلاف ہے۔ جب آقا نے غلام کو بیچ دیا کیونکہ ملک بدل جانے سے غلام بھی بدل گیا پس جو چیز بحکم ملک ثابت تھی وہ باقی نہ رہے گی۔ اسی لئے غلام مدعی علیہ نہیں ہوتا اس تصرف میں جو اس نے بیج سے پہلے کیا ہو۔
تشریح ... قوله فاذا حجر .... الخ عبد مازون نے مجور ہونے کے بعد اقرار کیا کہ میرے پاس جو کچھ ہے۔ وہ فلاں کی امانت ہے یا مغصوب ہے یا میرے ذمہ قرضہ ہے تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس کا اقرار استحسانا صحیح ہے۔ پس وہ اپنے مقبوضہ مال سے دین وغیرہ ادا کرے گا۔ صاحبین اور ائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک اقرار صحیح نہیں ہے۔ مقتضائے قیاس بھی یہی ہے۔ کیونکہ اقرار کا صحیح ہوتا اگر اجازت تجارت کی وجہ سے ہو تو حجر کی وجہ سے اجازت زائل ہو چکی اور اگر قبضہ کی وجہ سے ہو تو اپنی کمائی پر غلام کا جو قبضہ تھا وہ بھی حجر کی وجہ سے باطل ہو چکا۔ کیونکہ مجور کا قبضہ شرع غیر معتبر ہے۔ پس اقرار صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ اور یہ ایسا ہوگیا جیسے مجور مذکور کے اقرار سے پہلے آقا نے اس کے مقبوضہ مال کو اس کے قبضہ سے لے لیا ہو یا ایسا ہو گیا جیسے اجازت کی حالت نہیں آقا نے اس کو کسی دوسرے کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دیا کہ وہ مجور ہو گیا۔ ان دونوں صورتوں میں بالا تفاق اس کا اقرار صحیح نہیں ہوتا۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر وہ مجور ہونے کے بعد مال کا اقرار کرے تو یہ اس کی گردن کے حق میں صحیح نہیں ہوتا۔ یعنی وہ بالا تفاق اس مال کے لئے فروخت نہیں ہو سکتا۔
قوله وله ان المصح .... الخ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل ( اور وجہ استحسان ) یہ ہے کہ صحت اقرار کا مدار حقیقت قبضہ پر ہے۔ اس لئے جو مال کہ آقا نے اس کے اقرار سے پہلے اس کے قبضہ سے نکال لیا ہو اس مال کی نسبت اس کا اقرار صحیح نہیں ہوتا۔ اور بالفعل اس قبضہ در حقیقت باقی ہے۔ اور حجر کی وجہ سے حکما اس قبضہ کے باطل ہونے کی شرط یہ ہے کہ مال اس کی ضرورت سے فارغ ہو اور اس کا اقرار کرنا اس امر کی دلیل ہے کہ ابھی اس کی ضرورت باقی ہے۔
قوله بخلاف ما اذا انتزعه الخ صاحبین کے استشہاد " وصار كما اذا اخذ المولى كسبه من يده قبل اقراره " کا جواب ہے کہ بخلاف اس کے جب آقا نے اس کے اقرار سے پہلے مال اس کے قبضہ سے نکال لیا ہو کہ اس کا اقرار اس لئے صحیح نہیں ہوتا کہ آقا قبضہ موجود ہے۔ حقیقہ بھی اور حکما بھی تو آقا کا یہ قبضہ غلام کے اقرار کی وجہ سے باطل نہ ہوگا۔ اسی طرح غلام کے رقبہ میں آقا کی ملک باقی ہے پس غلام کا اقرار رقبہ کے حق میں صحیح نہیں۔
When the master interdicted the permitted slave, his confession (iqrar) is valid regarding the property in his possession according to Abu Hanifa. Its meaning is that he confesses that what is in his possession is a trust for someone else, or is usurped from him, or he confesses a debt upon himself, and it will be paid from what is in his possession. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: His confession is not valid. For them, the validating factor for his confession: if it is the permission, then it has ceased by interdiction; if it is the possession (yad), then interdiction has nullified it because the possession of an interdicted person is not considered, and it becomes like when the master took his earnings from his hand before his confession, or his interdiction was established by sale to another. For this reason, his confession is not valid regarding the person (raqabah) after interdiction. And for him (Abu Hanifa), the validating factor is the possession (yad), and for this reason the confession of the permitted slave is not valid regarding property which the master took from his hand. And the possession remains in reality (haqiqatan), but the condition for its legal (hukman) nullification by interdiction is its being free from his need, and his confession is evidence of its being needed. Contrary to when the master seized it from his hand before the confession, because the master's possession is established both in reality and legally, so it will not be nullified by the slave's confession. Similarly, the master's ownership (milk) is established over his person (raqabah), so it will not be nullified by the slave's confession without his consent. And this is different from when the master sold him, because the slave has changed with the change of ownership, as is known, so what was established by virtue of ownership does not remain. For this reason, he is not a defendant regarding transactions he conducted before the sale. Translation: When the master interdicted the permitted slave, his confession is valid regarding the property in his possession according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Its meaning is that he confesses regarding the property in his possession that it is a trust for someone else or usurped from him, or he confesses a debt upon himself, and it will be paid from the property in his possession. The Sahibayn say: His confession is not valid. Their proof is that the factor that validates his confession: if it is the permission, then it has ceased by interdiction; if it is the possession, then interdiction has nullified it because the possession of an interdicted person is not considered, and it becomes like when the master took his earnings from his hand before his confession, or like when his interdiction was established by selling him to another. For this reason, after interdiction his confession is not valid regarding the person (raqabah). Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the validating factor is the possession (yad). For this reason, the confession of the permitted slave is not valid regarding property that the master took from his hand. And the possession remains in reality, and the condition for its legal nullification by interdiction is that it is free from his need, and his confession is evidence of its being needed. Contrary to when the master took the property from his hand before the confession, because the master's possession is established both in reality and legally, so this possession will not be nullified by the slave's confession. Similarly, the master's ownership is established over his person (raqabah), so it will not be nullified by the slave's confession without his consent. And this is different from when the master sold him, because with the change of ownership, the slave also changed, so what was established by virtue of ownership does not remain. For this reason, the slave is not a defendant regarding transactions he conducted before the sale. Explanation: He said: When the master interdicted the permitted slave, his confession is valid... If a permitted slave, after being interdicted, confesses that what he has is a trust for someone or usurped or a debt upon him, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, his confession is valid by istihsan. So he will pay the debt etc. from the property in his possession. According to the Sahibayn and the Three Imams, the confession is not valid, and this is also the requirement of qiyas (analogy). Because if the validation of the confession is due to the permission to trade, then the permission has ceased by interdiction; if due to possession, then the slave's possession over his earnings has been nullified by interdiction because the possession of an interdicted person is legally not considered. So the confession will not be valid. And it becomes like when the master took the property from his hand before the confession, or like when the master sold him to someone else, making him interdicted. In both cases, by consensus, his confession is not valid. For this reason, if he confesses regarding property after interdiction, it is not valid regarding his person (raqabah), i.e., by consensus he cannot be sold for that property. He said: And for him the validating factor... Imam Abu Hanifa's proof (and the reason for istihsan) is that the validity of confession depends on actual possession (haqiqat al-qabd). Therefore, regarding property that the master took from his hand before the confession, his confession is not valid. And in reality, this possession actually remains. And the condition for its legal nullification due to interdiction is that the property is free from his need, and his confession is evidence that his need still exists. He said: Contrary to when he seized it... The response to the Sahibayn's analogy "it becomes like when the master took his earnings from his hand before his confession" is that it is different from when the master took the property from his hand before the confession, because his confession is invalid because the master's possession exists both in reality and legally, so the master's possession will not be nullified by the slave's confession. Similarly, the master's ownership over the slave's person remains, so the slave's confession is not valid regarding the person.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling regarding the validity of the iqrar (confession) of a 'abd ma'dhun (permitted slave) after he has been placed under hajr (interdiction) by his master. The original matn of Al-Hidayah states the ruling: "When the master interdicted the permitted slave, his confession is valid regarding the property in his possession according to Abu Hanifa." The commentator then explains the dispute. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the slave confesses that the property in his possession is a trust, usurped, or that he owes a debt, the confession is valid by istihsan, and the property will be used to discharge it. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the confession is invalid, based on qiyas: either the validating factor is the permission (which is gone) or the possession (which is legally nullified by hajr). The Sahibayn compare this to cases where the master takes the property before confession or sells the slave, where confession is invalid by consensus. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the actual possession (yad haqiqiyyah) remains intact, and the legal nullification of possession due to hajr is conditioned on the property being surplus to the slave's need. The slave's confession itself proves that he still needs the property (e.g., to repay debts), so the possession remains valid for the purpose of confession. The commentator then refutes the Sahibayn's analogy: when the master physically takes the property, the slave has no actual possession, so confession is invalid. But if the property remains in the slave's hand, his actual possession supports the confession. Furthermore, the master's ownership of the slave's person (raqabah) remains unaffected by the slave's confession regarding his own debt; the confession cannot make the slave liable for sale to pay the debt because that would infringe on the master's ownership without his consent. However, if the master sells the slave, the new owner may have different rights. This passage illustrates the Hanafi principle of istihsan overriding qiyas due to practical necessity (need of the slave to settle debts).

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment of a right or liability, here specifically by a slave regarding property in his possession.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction, a legal restriction on a person's capacity to dispose of property or make binding statements.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from strict qiyas due to a stronger consideration, here the need of the slave to settle debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the basis for Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that the confession of an interdicted permitted slave regarding property in his hand is valid?
A) The permission to trade remains despite interdiction.
B) The slave's actual possession (yad haqiqiyyah) remains, and his need is proven by his confession.
C) The slave's confession is valid only if the master consents.
D) The slave is no longer under the master's ownership.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's istihsan validates the confession of an interdicted slave based on actual possession and need, overriding the qiyas of the Sahibayn.

Validity of a Ma'dhun Slave's Confession After Hajr: The Istihsan vs Qiyas Dispute
Dispute on a ma'dhun's iqrar after hajr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a ma'dhun slave's confession after interdiction, explain the proofs of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Actual possession and need preserve a ma'dhun's confession after hajr, overriding qiyas by istihsan.

In Hanafi fiqh, a ma'dhun slave is allowed to trade. But what if the master revokes that permission (hajr)? Can the slave still confess that property in his hands belongs to someone else, or that he owes debts? The schools differ sharply. This lesson unpacks the debate between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), showing how istihsan can override strict qiyas when need and reality persist.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Identify the key facts: a ma'dhun slave is interdicted (hajr), then confesses that property in his possession is a trust, usurped, or that he owes a debt. The question: is the confession valid?
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its basis
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the confession is valid (sahih) by istihsan. The basis is that actual possession (haqiqat al-qabd) remains, and the condition for it being legally voided (that property exceeds need) is not met—the confession proves need.
Note the use of istihsan to override qiyas.
3
Identify the opposing position and its qiyas
The Sahibayn (and the three other imams) hold the confession is invalid. Their proof: validity depends either on permission (idhn) or possession (qabd); both are nullified by hajr. They analogize to two cases: (a) master takes earnings before confession, and (b) master sells the slave to another—both where confession is invalid universally.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response to the analogies
Abu Hanifa distinguishes each analogy. In case (a), the master's possession is both actual and legal, so it overrides the slave's. In case (b), ownership changes entirely, so the slave's prior rights vanish. Here, actual possession remains and need is demonstrated by the confession itself.
This is the heart of the istihsan argument.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference: according to Abu Hanifa, the slave can pay debts from his held property; according to the Sahibayn, the property belongs to the master and debts are not paid. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position, supported by later Hanafi authorities.
This shows how ikhtilaf affects real outcomes.

Translation: When the master has interdicted the ma'dhun slave, his confession is valid regarding the property in his possession. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this means he acknowledges that what he has is another's trust or usurped, or he confesses a debt to be paid from his possessed property. The Sahibayn say his confession is not valid. Their proof: If the factor validating confession is permission (idhn), it is removed by hajr; if it is possession (qabd), hajr has nullified it because the mahjur's possession is not legally valid. It becomes like the master taking his earnings before confession, or like his hajr being established by sale to another. Therefore, after hajr, his confession regarding himself (raqabah) is not valid. Abu Hanifa's proof: The validating factor is actual possession. Therefore, the ma'dhun's confession is not valid for property the master has taken from his possession, but actual possession remains. The condition for possession being voided legally due to hajr is that the property exceeds his need; his confession indicates need remains. Unlike when the master took property before confession, because the master's possession is established both actually and legally, so it won't be voided by the slave's confession. Similarly, the master's ownership in the slave's person is established, so without his consent it won't be voided. And it is contrary to when the master sold the slave, because ownership changed and the slave changed; what was established by ruling of ownership does not remain. Hence the slave is not liable for transactions done before the sale. Commentary: ... When a ma'dhun after being interdicted confesses that what he has is someone's trust or usurped or a debt on him, according to Abu Hanifa his confession is valid by istihsan. So he will pay debts etc. from his possessed property. According to the Sahibayn and the three imams, the confession is not valid; this is also what qiyas requires. Because if validity was due to trading permission, hajr removed it; if due to possession, his possession over earnings is voided by hajr because the mahjur's possession is legally invalid. So it becomes like the master taking his earnings before his confession, or like the master selling him to another. In both cases, universally, his confession is not valid. This is why if he confesses after hajr regarding property, it is not valid regarding his own liability; i.e., he cannot be sold for that property universally. His statement: 'and he has that the corrector...' The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa (and the reason for istihsan) is that the validity of confession depends on actual possession. Therefore, for property that the master took before his confession, his confession is not valid. And his actual possession in reality remains. The condition for voiding this possession legally due to hajr is that the property exceeds his need, and his confession indicates that need persists. His statement: 'unlike when he took it...' Response to Sahibayn's analogy 'and it becomes like when the master took his earnings from his hand before his confession': It is unlike when the master took property before confession, because in that case the master's possession exists both actually and legally, so the master's possession will not be voided by the slave's confession. Similarly, the master's ownership in the slave's person is established, so ownership will not be voided by the slave's confession without his consent. His statement: 'and this is unlike when...' Response to Sahibayn's second analogy 'and his hajr is established by sale to another': It is unlike the case where the master sold this slave to another, because the slave's confession regarding that property is not valid because due to the change in ownership, the slave also changed; thus what was established for the slave by ruling of ownership does not remain. That is, the possession over property he had due to the master's permission does not remain due to change of ownership. Hence his confession is not valid. This is why if the slave made a transaction (buy/sell) before being sold, after the sale he cannot be a defendant regarding it. For example, if he sold something, then the master sold him, one cannot demand delivery of the sold item from this slave, because due to the sale he became like another slave.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of a ma'dhun slave's confession (iqrar) after his master has interdicted him (hajr). The issue is whether the slave's acknowledgment that property in his possession belongs to another (as trust, usurpation) or that he owes a debt is legally effective. Imam Abu Hanifa holds it valid by istihsan, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the three other imams hold it invalid by qiyas. The passage first presents the ruling and then gives the proofs and responses. The Sahibayn argue that the basis for valid confession is either the permission to trade (idhn) or possession (qabd), both of which are nullified by hajr. They analogize to cases where the master takes the earnings before confession or sells the slave to another—both cases where confession is invalid. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the actual possession (haqiqat al-qabd) remains; hajr only cancels the legal effect of possession if the property exceeds need. The slave's confession itself demonstrates that need persists. The responses distinguish the Sahibayn's analogies: in the case of the master taking property, the master's actual and legal possession is superior; in the case of sale, ownership changes entirely, so the slave's prior rights disappear. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's, based on istihsan.

Key Terms
ماذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission (idhn) by his master to engage in trade.
حجر (hajr): The act of the master interdicting the slave, revoking his permission to trade, thus restricting his legal capacity.
اقرار (iqrar): A legal confession or acknowledgment by a person regarding a right of another (e.g., debt, trust, usurpation).
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference in which a ruling is adopted due to its appropriateness, overriding qiyas in exceptional circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the basis for the validity of a ma'dhun slave's confession after hajr?
A) The master's permission for trade
B) The slave's actual possession and ongoing need
C) The slave's ownership of the property
D) The approval of the Sahibayn

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a ma'dhun slave's confession after hajr is valid by istihsan because actual possession and need persist, overriding the strict qiyas that would invalidate it.

Master's Ownership of Slave's Earnings When Debts Encompass: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on master's ownership of slave's earnings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the master's ownership of a ma'dhun slave's earnings when debts encompass the slave's property and person, and explain the reasoning of both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Master's ownership of slave earnings is a succession conditional on the slave's need being met.

In Hanafi law, a ma'dhun slave (authorized to trade) earns property, but the master's claim to those earnings is not absolute. This lesson explores a key dispute within the school: what happens when the slave's debts exceed all his assets and even his value? Both Abu Hanifa and his two students agree on the general rule, but differ on its application when debts encompass. By understanding their proofs, you will grasp a foundational principle about the nature of the master's ownership and its limits.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa)
Identify the ruling that the master does not own the slave's earnings when debts encompass both property and person. The master cannot validly free another slave from those earnings.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent's view (Sahibayn)
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold the opposite: the master owns the earnings, so the freed slave becomes free, and the master must pay the value to creditors.
They base this on full ownership of the slave's person (raqabah).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Sahibayn argue: Master owns the slave's person (milk al-raqabah), which is the cause (sabab) for owning earnings. This is proven by the master's right to free the slave and have intercourse with a slave-girl. Since he owns the source, he owns the proceeds.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds: Ownership of earnings is a succession (khilafah) that only activates when the slave is free from need (gharaghan min hajatih). Debts encompassing the property show the slave's need is unmet, so succession cannot occur. The right to free and intercourse are incidents of ownership of the person, not earnings.
Compare to heir's ownership, which also succeeds only after debts are paid.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, creditors take the earnings directly; the freed slave is not free. Under Sahibayn, the master takes the earnings but must compensate; the freed slave is free.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

He said: And when debts that encompass his property and person become binding on him, the master does not own what is in his hand. And if the master frees a slave from his earnings, it is not freed according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Sahibayn) say: He owns what is in his hand and it is freed, and he must pay its value, because he found the cause of ownership in his earnings, which is ownership of the person (raqabah). For this reason, the master has the right to free him and to have intercourse with the ma'dhun slave-girl, and this is a sign of complete ownership. Unlike the heir, because ownership is established for him for the benefit of the testator, and the benefit is opposite when debts encompass the estate. As for the master's ownership, it is not established for the benefit of the slave. And he has that the master's ownership is only established as a successor (khalifat) of the slave when free from his need, like the ownership of the heir as we established. And what is encompassed by debt is occupied by need, so he does not succeed him in it. And when the existence or non-existence of ownership is known, then freeing is its branch. And if it is effective according to the two (Sahibayn), he guarantees its value to the creditors due to the attachment of their right. He said: And if the debt does not encompass his property, his freeing is permissible in the opinion of all of them. As for the two (Sahibayn), it is apparent. Likewise according to him (Abu Hanifa), because he is not free from a little of it. If he made it a preventive, the door of benefiting from his earnings would be closed, and what is intended by the permission would be corrupted. For this reason, it does not prevent the ownership of the heir and the encompassing... Translation: When such debts that encompass his property and person become binding on him, the master will not own what is in his possession, and if the master freed a slave from his earnings, it will not be freed according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn say: The master will own his possessed property and the freed slave will be freed, and the master must pay its value. Because the cause of ownership is found in the ma'dhun's earnings, and that is ownership of the person. For this reason, the master has the right to free the ma'dhun slave and to have intercourse with the ma'dhun slave-girl, and this is a sign of complete ownership. Unlike the heir, because for him ownership is established for the benefit of the testator, and the benefit is opposite when debts encompass the estate. As for the master's ownership, it is not established for the benefit of the slave. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the master's ownership is established as a substitute for the slave only when free from his need. Like the ownership of the heir as we established. And the property encompassed by debt is occupied by need, so the master does not substitute for him in it. So the master does not own it.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the master's ownership of a ma'dhun slave's earnings when the slave's debts encompass all his property and even his person (i.e., the debts exceed his assets and his value). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the master does not own what is in the slave's hand, and if the master frees a slave from those earnings, the freed slave is not free. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the master does own the earnings, so if he frees a slave from them, the freed slave becomes free, and the master must pay the slave's value to the creditors. The Sahibayn argue that the master already owns the slave's person (raqabah), which is the cause of ownership in earnings, as evidenced by his right to free the slave and have intercourse with the slave-girl. Abu Hanifa responds that the master's ownership of earnings is only derivative (khalifat 'an al-'abd) and is conditional on the slave being free from need. Debts occupying the property indicate the slave's need, so the master cannot take it. The passage then notes that if debts do not encompass all property, the slave's freeing by the master is valid by consensus, because a partial debt should not block all benefit; otherwise, the purpose of permission (idhn) would be defeated. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's, based on the principle that the master's ownership is a succession only when the slave's need is fulfilled.

Key Terms
ملك الرقبة (milk al-raqabah): Ownership of the slave's person (the physical being), which gives the master certain rights like freeing and intercourse.
خلافة (khilafah): Successorship; the master owns the slave's earnings as a substitute (khalifah) for the slave, only when the slave's needs are met.
ديون محيطة (duyun muhitah): Debts that encompass (surround) all of the slave's property and even his person, meaning the debts exceed both his assets and his market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why does the master not own a ma'dhun slave's earnings when debts encompass the property?
A) Because the master's permission is revoked
B) Because the master only owns the person, not the earnings
C) Because the master's ownership is a succession that requires the slave to be free from need
D) Because the slave owns his own earnings in this case

Key Takeaway
The master's ownership of a ma'dhun slave's earnings is not absolute; it is a conditional succession that fails when debts encompass the slave's property and person.

Master's Ownership of Ma'dhun Property When Debts Encompass
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership and emancipation of ma'dhun slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) on whether the master owns the ma'dhun's property when debts encompass it, and derive the practical consequence for emancipation and creditor rights.

Core Idea
When debts encompass the ma'dhun's property, Abu Hanifa denies master's ownership; Sahibayn affirm it but require compensation to creditors.

This passage from the Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of the master's ownership over the ma'dhun slave's earnings when the slave is deeply in debt. The core question: does the master's milk al-raqaba (ownership of the slave's person) grant him full control over the slave's property even when creditors' claims overwhelm it? The answer leads to opposite rulings on whether the master can effectively emancipate a slave purchased with those earnings. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the Hanafi distinction between ownership of the person and ownership of property, and to see how legal maxims about debt and proxy authority interact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the issue
Note that the dispute is within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa versus Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn). The issue is whether the master owns the ma'dhun's property when debts are muḥīṭ (encompassing both property and person).
The ma'dhun is a slave permitted to trade; his capital comes from the master.
2
State each position clearly
Abu Hanifa: master does not own, so emancipation is invalid. Sahibayn: master does own, so emancipation is valid, but he must pay the slave's value to creditors.
The difference hinges on the nature of the master's ownership.
3
Analyze the proofs (dalil) for each side
Sahibayn argue from milk al-raqaba: ownership of the slave's person is the cause of ownership of earnings, evidenced by the master's right to free and to have intercourse with a ma'dhuna. Abu Hanifa counters that the master's ownership is proxy (khilafa) and only functions when the slave is free from need; debt occupies the need, so proxy fails.
The analogy with an heir inheriting an estate engulfed by debt is used to illustrate the principle.
4
Examine the analogy (qiyas) with inheritance
Abu Hanifa compares the master to an heir: just as an heir cannot free a slave from an estate engulfed by debt (because ownership is for the testator's benefit), the master cannot act as proxy when the slave's entire value is needed for debts. The Sahibayn reject this analogy, arguing that the master's ownership is for his own benefit, not the slave's.
This is a key point of talil (ratiocination).
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If debts encompass, Abu Hanifa says emancipation is void; if already done, the slave remains in the master's ownership but the creditors can still pursue the property. Sahibayn say emancipation is valid but the master owes the value to creditors, protecting their rights.
The mufta bihi position is often that of the Sahibayn for practicality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و اذا لزمته ديون تحيط بماله ورقبته لم يملك المولى ما فى يده ولو اعتق من كسبه عبدا لم يعتق عند ابي حنيفة وقالا يملك ما في يده ويعتق وعليه قيمته لانه وجد سبب الملك فى كسبه وهو ملك الرقبة ولهذا يملك اعتاقه ووطى الجارية الماذون لها وهذا آية كماله بخلاف الوارث لانه يثبت الملك له نظرا للمورث والـنـظـر فـي ضده عند احاطة الدين بتركته اما ملک المولى ما ثبت نظرا للعبد وله ان الملك للمولى انما يثبت خلافة عن العبد عند فراغه عن حاجته کملک الوارث على ما قررناه والمحيط به الدين مشغول بها فلا يخلفه فيه واذا عرف ثبوت الملك و عدمه فالعتق فريعته واذا نفذ عندهما يضمن قيمته للغرماء لتعلق حقهم .به قال وان لم يكن الدين محيطا بماله جاز عتقه فى قولهم جميعا اما عندهما فظاهر وكذا عنده لانه لا يعرى عن قليله فلو جعل مانعا لانسد باب الانتفاع بكسبه فيختل ما هو المقصود من الاذن ولهذا لا يـ نع ملك الوارث والمستغرق يـ
He said: And when debts that encompass his property and his neck become binding on him, the master does not own what is in his hand. If he frees a slave from his earnings, it is not freed according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He owns what is in his hand, and it is freed, and he owes its value. Because the cause of ownership is found in his earnings, which is ownership of the neck (the slave himself). Therefore he has the right to free him and to have intercourse with the female slave who is given permission (ma'dhuna), and this is a sign of complete ownership. Contrary to the heir, because ownership is established for him for the benefit of the testator, and the benefit is opposed when the debt encompasses the estate. As for the master's ownership, it is not established for the benefit of the slave. And he has (the argument) that the master's ownership is established as a substitute for the slave when he is free from his need, like the ownership of the heir as we have established. But what is encompassed by debt is occupied with it, so he does not act as a substitute in it. When the establishment or non-establishment of ownership is known, then emancipation is its branch. And when it is effective according to the two, he guarantees its value for the creditors because their right is attached to it. He said: And if the debt does not encompass his property, his emancipation is valid in all of their statements. As for the two, it is apparent, and similarly according to him (Abu Hanifa) because it is not free from a small amount. If it were made a preventative, the door of benefiting from his earnings would be closed, and the purpose of permission would be disrupted. For this reason, a small debt does not prevent the ownership of the heir, but an encompassing debt prevents it.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah commentary presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ma'dhun slave (a slave permitted to trade). The core issue: when the ma'dhun's debts completely cover his property and his person (i.e., he is insolvent), does the master still own the property in the slave's hands, and can the master effectively emancipate a slave purchased with those earnings?

Abu Hanifa's position: The master does not own that property, because his ownership is derivative (by proxy/khilafa) and only arises when the slave's needs are satisfied. Since the debts consume the entire value, the slave's need is paramount, so the master cannot act as a substitute. Hence, if the master frees a slave bought with those earnings, the emancipation is invalid.

Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn): The master does own the property because he owns the slave himself (ownership of the neck). This gives him full authority, including the right to free the slave and to have intercourse with a ma'dhuna female slave (which is a sign of complete ownership). Therefore, emancipation is valid, but the master must pay the value to the creditors because their rights are attached to that property.

The passage then distinguishes this from inheritance: if a testator's estate is engulfed by debt, the heir cannot free a slave from the estate because ownership is for the benefit of the testator (i.e., to settle debts); here, freeing would harm the creditors. In contrast, the master's ownership is for his own benefit, not the slave's, so the analogy with inheritance does not apply directly.

Finally, all agree that if the debts do not encompass the property (i.e., there is surplus), emancipation is valid, because a small debt should not block benefit. Otherwise, the very purpose of granting the slave permission to trade would be defeated.

Key Terms
عبد ماذون (‘abd ma’dhūn): A slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade (madhun lahu) with the condition that the slave's capital comes from the master and profits are shared or belong to the master.
محیط (muḥīṭ): Encompassing; here referring to debts that equal or exceed the total property and value of the slave's person, leaving no surplus.
ملک الرقبہ (milk al-raqaba): Ownership of the slave's very person (the neck), which gives the master absolute authority over the slave including his earnings and physical acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the master's ownership from an heir's ownership in the context of encompassing debts?
A) The master's ownership is for the benefit of the slave, while the heir's is for the testator.
B) The master's ownership is for his own benefit, while the heir's is for the testator's benefit.
C) Both are for the benefit of the debtor.
D) The master's ownership is abolished by debt entirely.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when a ma'dhun slave's debts encompass his property, the master's right to own and dispose of that property is disputed: Abu Hanifa denies ownership (invalidating emancipation), while the Sahibayn affirm ownership (allowing emancipation with creditor compensation).

Freeing a Slave from a Heavily Indebted Madhun: Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on freeing a slave owned by a madhun with encompassing debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and contrast the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the validity of a master freeing a slave from a madhun's assets when the madhun's debt is encompassing, and evaluate the rational proofs (dalil aqli) each side uses.

Core Idea
Encompassing debt suspends the master's ownership in the madhun's earnings – but the Sahibayn still allow freeing with guarantee.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute rooted in the nature of the master's ownership over his madhun slave's earnings. When debts overwhelm the madhun's assets, does the master still own those assets? The answer determines whether he can validly free a slave purchased by the madhun. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that ownership is suspended, while the Sahibayn (and the majority of scholars) maintain that ownership persists but is encumbered. We will trace the reasoning behind each view, analyze the crucial distinction from inheritance law, and see why the Sahibayn's position is preferred for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and the two Hanafi positions
The issue: can a master free a slave owned by his madhun when debt covers all assets and the madhun's person? Imam Abu Hanifa says no; Sahibayn say yes, but master guarantees price.
2
Trace the Sahibayn's proof: master's ownership of the madhun's person
The Sahibayn argue that the master owns the madhun's person (raqaba), which is the cause (sabab) for owning the madhun's acquisitions. Proof: master can free the madhun himself and have intercourse with a madhun slave-girl – both require complete ownership.
This is based on the idea that ownership of the person gives ownership of acquisitions.
3
Analyze the analogy with inheritance and its rebuttal
An objection: in inheritance, an heir cannot free a slave from an estate encompassed by debt. Why should the master be different? The Hidayah responds: the heir's ownership is contingent on the testator's benefit, while the master's ownership is direct and not contingent on the madhun's benefit.
The key distinction is the basis of ownership: derivative vs. direct.
4
Examine Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: ownership by deputyship and the need for surplus
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the master's ownership over the madhun's earnings is by deputyship (niyaba), effective only after the madhun's needs are met. Encompassing debt means the wealth is needed for the madhun's creditors, so the master is not a deputy.
This view treats the madhun's need as a priority over the master's ownership.
5
Determine the practical consequence and the relied-upon ruling
Under the Sahibayn's view, the slave is freed and the master must pay the price; under Imam Abu Hanifa's view, the slave is not freed. The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's position, as it facilitates trade and aligns with the majority.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the slave becomes free or remains a slave.
