تشریح قوله واذا الزمته الخ عبد ماذون پر اس قدرقرضے چڑھ گئے جو اس کے مال اور اس کی گردن کو محیط ہیں تو ایسی صورت میں جو مال اس کے پاس ہو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک آقا اس کا مالک نہیں ہو سکتا۔ پس اگر ماذون کی کمائی میں کوئی غلام ہو اور آقا اس کو آزاد کر دے تو آزاد نہ ہوگا۔ صاحبین اور ائمہ ثلاثہ کے نزدیک آقاعبد مازون کے مال کا مالک ہوتا ہے تو اس کے آزاد کرنے سے غلام مذکور آزاد ہو جائے گا اور آقا پر اس کی قیمت لازم ہوگی اگر وہ مالدار ہو اگر تنگدست ہو تو ماذون کے قرض خواہ آزاد غلام سے تاوان لے سکتے ہیں وہ پھر آقا سے لے لے گا۔ قوله لانه وجد الخ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ عبد مازون کی کمائی میں ملک آقا کا سبب پایا گیا اور وہ یہ کہ آقا اس کے رقبہ کا مالک ہے۔ اسی لئے آقا کو یہ اختیار ہوتا ہے کہ اپنے عبد ماذون کو آزاد کر دے۔ اور اپنی مازو نہ باندی کے ساتھ وطی کرنے جو کمال ملک کی دلیل ہے۔ کیونکہ ولی اور محقق ملک کامل ہی میں ہوتے ہیں۔ قوله بخلاف الوارث ..... الخ سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ عبد ماذون کی کمائی میں سب ملک کا پایا جانا تو تسلیم ہے لیکن مانع تو موجود ہے یعنی دین کا محیط ہونا کہ یہ اعتاق سے مانع ہے جیسے ترکہ میں ہوتا ہے کہ اگر تر کہ دین محیط ہو تو وارث کا اعتاق صحیح نہیں ہوتا۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ اگر مورث کا قرضہ محیط ہو تو وراث کو ترکہ کا غلام آزاد کرنے کا اختیار اس لئے نہیں ہوتا کہ وارث کی ملک تو مورث کی بہتری کے پیش نظر ہے اور ترکہ پر قرضہ محیط ہونے کی صورت میں مورث کی بہتری اسی میں ہے کہ غلام آزاد ہو۔ رہی ملک آقا سودہ عید ماذون کی بہتری کے پیش نظر نہیں ہے یہاں تک کہ یہ لازم آئے کہ اس کی بہتری اسی میں ہے کہ اداء قرض تک اعتناق جائز نہ ہو بلکہ آقا تو ملک بذات خود حاصل ہے۔ قوله وله ان الملك...... الخ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ آقا اپنے تاجر غلام کی کمائی کا مالک بطریق نیابت اس وقت ہوتا ہے جب وہ کمائی غلام کی ضرورت سے فارغ ہو اور جس مال کو دین محیط ہے وہ اس کی ضرورت میں مشغول ہے تو آقا اس مال میں خلیفہ نہ ہوگا ۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جب غلام کی کمائی میں آقا کی ملک کا ثابت ہونا ( صاحبین کے نزدیک ) اور اس کا ثابت نہ ہونا (امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ) معلوم ہو گیا تو آزاد کرنا اس کی فرع ہے یعنی امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک جب ملک ثابت نہیں تو آزادی بھی نہ ہوگی اور صاحبین کے نزدیک جب ملک ثابت ہے تو عبد ماذون کی کمائی کا غلام آزاد کرنا بھی صحیح ہوگا اور جب صاحبین کے نزدیک آقا کا اعتاق نافذ ہوا تو آقا قرض خواہوں کے لئے اس کی قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس سے قرض خواہوں کا حق متعلق ہو چکا ہے۔ قوله وان لم يكن الدين ... الخ اور اگر عبد ماذون کا دین اس کے مال اور اس کی ذات کو محیط نہ ہو تو آقا کا عبد ماذون کے غلام کو آزاد کرنا بالاتفاق صحیح ہے۔ صاحبین کے نزدیک تو ظاہر ہے اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس لئے جائز ہے کہ قلیل قرضہ سے مال خالی نہیں ہوتا۔ اگر ایسا قرضہ بھی مانع سمجھا جائے تو عبد مازون کی کمائی سے آقا کے نفع اٹھانے کا دروازہ ہی بند ہو جائے گا اور اجازت دینے کا جو مقصد ہے وہ حاصل نہ ہوگا ۔ اسی لئے قلیل قرضہ ملک وارث سے مانع نہیں ہوتا اور دین محیط مانع ملک ہوتا ہے۔
Commentary on his statement: 'And when it is required, etc.' A madhun slave accumulates debts that encompass his wealth and his neck. In such a case, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the master cannot own the wealth he has. So if there is a slave among the madhun's earnings and the master frees him, he will not be free. According to the Sahibayn and the three Imams, the master owns the madhun's wealth, so by his freeing, that slave becomes free and the master becomes liable for his price. If the master is wealthy, he pays; if he is insolvent, the madhun's creditors may take compensation from the freed slave, who then recovers from the master. His statement: 'Because he found, etc.' The Sahibayn's proof is that the cause of the master's ownership in the madhun's earnings is found, namely that the master owns his person. Hence the master has the authority to free his madhun slave and to have intercourse with his madhun slave-girl, which is evidence of complete ownership, because guardianship and authority are only in complete ownership. His statement: 'Unlike the heir, etc.' This is an answer to a question. The question is: It is conceded that the master's ownership of the madhun's earnings is found, but there is a preventing factor, namely encompassing debt, which prevents manumission, as in inheritance: if debt encompasses the estate, the heir's manumission is not valid. The answer is: If the testator's debt is encompassing, the heirs do not have authority to free a slave of the estate because the heir's ownership is contingent on the testator's benefit, and when the estate is encompassed by debt, the testator's benefit lies in the slave being freed. However, the master's ownership of the madhun's earnings is not contingent on the madhun's benefit, so it does not follow that his benefit lies in prohibiting manumission until the debt is paid; rather, the master's ownership is acquired by himself. His statement: 'And he has that ownership...' Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the master owns his merchant slave's earnings by way of deputyship only when the earnings are surplus to the slave's need. Wealth encompassed by debt is occupied for his need, so the master is not a deputy in that wealth. The author of the Hidayah says: When it is known that the master's ownership of the slave's earnings is established (according to the Sahibayn) and not established (according to Imam Abu Hanifa), then manumission is a branch of that. That is, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, when ownership is not established, manumission does not occur; according to the Sahibayn, when ownership is established, freeing a slave from the madhun's earnings is valid. And when the Sahibayn hold that the master's manumission is effective, the master becomes guarantor of its price for the creditors, because the creditors' right has become attached thereby. His statement: 'And if the debt does not encompass...' If the madhun's debt does not encompass his wealth and his person, then the master's freeing of the madhun's slave is valid by consensus. It is obvious according to the Sahibayn, and according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is permissible because wealth is not free from a small debt. If such a debt were also considered a preventer, then the door for the master to benefit from the madhun's earnings would be closed, and the purpose of granting permission would be defeated. Therefore, a small debt does not prevent the ownership of the heir, and encompassing debt prevents ownership.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of a master freeing a slave owned by his madhun (a slave permitted to trade) when the madhun is heavily in debt. The core issue is the nature of the master's ownership over the madhun's earnings when those earnings are entirely consumed by debt. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that when the madhun's debts cover his assets and his person, the master does not actually own those assets; they are effectively owned by the creditors for their claims. Consequently, the master cannot validly free a slave that is part of those assets, because he lacks ownership over it. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and the three Imams (the other major Sunni schools) argue that the master remains the owner of the madhun's earnings, as the master owns the madhun himself, which is the basis for ownership of the madhun's acquisitions. Therefore, the master can free such a slave, but he becomes liable for the slave's value to the creditors if he is wealthy; if insolvent, the creditors can claim compensation from the freed slave, who then recovers from the master. The passage then addresses a potential objection drawn from inheritance: just as an heir cannot free a slave from an estate encompassed by debt, why should the master be different? The answer distinguishes the two cases. In inheritance, the heir's ownership is contingent on the testator's benefit; when debt covers the estate, the testator's benefit lies in freeing the slave to earn reward, so the heir's ownership is subordinated. In contrast, the master's ownership of the madhun's earnings is absolute, not contingent on the madhun's benefit, so no such subordination applies. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the master's ownership is by deputyship (niyaba), effective only when earnings are surplus to the madhun's need. Debt-encumbered wealth is tied to the madhun's need, so the master is not a deputy. The Hidayah author summarizes: establishing ownership determines the ruling on manumission—where ownership is absent (Imam Abu Hanifa's view), manumission fails; where ownership is present (Sahibayn), manumission is valid and the master guarantees the value. Finally, if the madhun's debt is not encompassing (i.e., there is some surplus), all agree the master can free the slave, because small debts are considered unavoidable and do not impede the purpose of the trading permission.

Key Terms
عبد ماذون (abd ma'dhun): A slave whom his master has permitted to engage in trade; the slave's acquisitions become part of the master's ownership, but the slave can be liable for debts from the trade.
دین محیط (dayn muhit): Debt that entirely covers the madhun's assets and his personal value (his 'neck'), leaving no surplus for the master.
عتق (itq): Manumission; freeing a slave. The validity depends on the owner having valid ownership at the time of freeing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view (mufta bihi), what happens when a master frees a slave owned by his heavily indebted madhun (debt encompassing all assets and the madhun's person)?
A) The manumission is invalid because the master lacks ownership.
B) The manumission is valid, and the master must pay the slave's price to the creditors.
C) The manumission is valid only if the creditors consent.
D) The manumission is valid, but the slave remains liable for the debt.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a master can free a slave from a heavily indebted madhun's assets, but he must compensate the creditors for the slave's value.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Madhun Selling to Master: Valid at Fair Price, Invalid at a Loss
Ruling on madhun's sale to master with comparisons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on a madhun's sale to his master at fair price or at a loss, distinguish it from similar cases involving strangers and heirs, and explain the legal basis for each distinction using the concepts of tuhmah and attachment of rights.

Core Idea
A madhun's sale to his master at a loss is invalid due to tuhmah; selling to a stranger at a loss is valid because no tuhmah.

This passage from al-Hidayah explores the validity of transactions by a madhun (a slave permitted to trade) when selling to his master. It establishes a baseline ruling: sale at fair value is valid when the madhun is indebted, but sale at a loss is invalid due to suspicion of favoritism. The text then contrasts this with two other scenarios—showing favor to a stranger, and a sick person selling to an heir—to highlight the underlying legal principles of tuhmah and the attachment of rights ('ayn vs. maliyah). These distinctions are essential for understanding Hanafi contract law and the protection of creditors' and heirs' rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary case
The case is a madhun (a slave permitted to trade) selling an item to his own master. Determine whether the sale is at fair market value or at a loss, and whether the madhun is indebted.
The ruling differs based on these factors.
2
Apply the ruling for sale at fair value when indebted
If the madhun is indebted with debt covering his earnings, and he sells at fair value, the sale is valid. The master is considered a stranger regarding the madhun's earnings because creditors have claims.
If the madhun is not indebted, he and his property belong to the master, so no sale between them occurs.
3
Apply the ruling for sale at a loss
If the madhun sells at a loss to his master, the sale is invalid due to tuhmah (suspicion that he is favoring his master to the detriment of creditors).
A slight loss within the range of different valuations might be tolerated, but excessive loss is clear tuhmah.
4
Contrast with muhabat to a stranger
If the madhun shows favor (sells at a loss) to a stranger, it is valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa because there is no tuhmah—the stranger is not the master.
This contrast underscores that tuhmah is specific to the master due to the master's authority.
5
Contrast with sale by a sick person to an heir at fair value
If a sick person sells to his heir at fair value, it is invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The right of other heirs attaches to the very substance ('ayn) of the property, so any sale that may favor one heir is suspect, even at fair price.
This differs from the madhun case where creditors' right attaches only to financial value (maliyah), not to the specific item.

قال وان باع من المولى شيئا بمثل قيمته جاز لانه کالاجنبي عن كسبه اذا كان عليه دين يحيط بكسبه وان باعه بنقصان لم يجز لانه متهم فى حقه بخلاف ما اذا حابى الاجنبى عند ابي حنيفة لانه لا تهمة فيه وبخلاف ما اذا باع المريض من الوارث بمثل قيمته حيث لا يجوز عنده لان حق بقية الورثة تعلق بعينه حتى كان لاحدهم الاستخلاص باداء قيمته اما حق الغرماء تعلق بالمالية لا غير فافترقا
ترجمہ اگر فروخت کی مازون نے آقا کے ہاتھ کوئی چیز اس کے برابر قیمت پر تو جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ آقا اس کی کمائی سے مثل اجنبی کے ہے جب کہ ماذون پر اتنا قرض ہو جو اسکی کمائی پر محیط ہو ۔ اور اگر نقصان سے بیچی تو جائز نہیں۔ کیونکہ وہ آقا کے حق میں متہم ہے بخلاف اس کے جب اس نے اجنبی کے ساتھ محابات کی امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک کیونکہ اس میں کوئی تہمت نہیں ہے۔ اور بخلاف اس کے جب مریض نے وارث کے ہاتھ برابر قیمت پر بیچی کہ یہ جائز نہیں امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک کیونکہ باقی ورثہ کا حق عین شئی سے متعلق ہو چکا ہے یہاں تک کہ ہر وارث کو حق ہے اس کی قیمت دے کر چھڑا لینے کا۔ رہے قرض خواہ سو ان کا حق تو صرف مالیت سے متعلق ہے۔
تشریح قوله وان باع من المولى الخ اگر عبد مازون اپنے آقا کے ہاتھ برابر قیمت سے کوئی چیز فروخت کرے تو جائز ہے مگر یہ اس وقت ہے جب غلام مقروض ہو کہ اس وقت اس کا آقا اس کی کمائی میں مثل اجنبی کے ہوتا ہے اور اگر وہ مقروض نہ ہو تو پھر ان دونوں میں خرید و فروخت نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ غلام اور جو کچھ اس کے پاس وہ سب قاکی ملک ہے اور اگر عید ماذون اپنے آقا کے ہاتھ نقصان سے فروخت کرے تو یہ جائز نہیں ، کیونکہ اس کے حق میں تہمت کا امکان ہے۔ فائدہ جب قیمت میں اس قدر کمی ہو جو جملہ مقومین کے اندازہ سے کم ہے مثلاً ایک چیز کی قیمت بعض دس روپیہ اور بعض نو روپیہ اندازہ کرتے ہیں تو نو روپیہ تک خسارہ خفیف ہے اور نو سے کم خسارہ فاحش۔
قوله بخلاف ما اذا حاب ..... الخ قیمت میں کمی کے ساتھ فروخت کرنا محابات ہوتی ہے۔ پس اگر آقا کے ساتھ محابات کی تو یہ جائز نہیں ۔ جیسا کہ او پر مذکور ہوا اس کے برخلاف اگر اس نے کسی اجنبی کے ساتھ محابات کی تو یہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک جائز ہے کیونکہ اس میں کوئی تہمت نہیں ہے اور برخلاف اس کے جب مریض نے اپنے وارث کے ہاتھ کوئی چیز برابر قیمت سے فروخت کی تو یہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک جائز نہیں۔ کیونکہ باقی ورثہ کا حق اس چیز کی ذات سے متعلق ہو چکا ہے۔ حتی کہ اگر مریض پر قرضہ ہو تو ہر وارث کو یہ اختیار ہے کہ اس کی قیمت ادا کر کے اس کو چھڑا لے۔ بخلاف ماذون کے کہ ماذون کی صورت میں قرض خواہوں کا حق صرف مالیت سے
He said: If the madhun sold something to the master at its fair value, it is valid, because he is like a stranger regarding his earnings, when there is debt that encompasses his earnings. If he sold it at a loss, it is not valid because he is suspected in his right. Unlike if he showed favor to a stranger, according to Abu Hanifa, because there is no suspicion there. And unlike if a sick person sold to an heir at fair value, which is not valid according to him, because the right of the remaining heirs attaches to the very substance of the item, such that each of them may redeem it by paying its price. As for the creditors, their right attaches only to the financial value, not the substance, so they differ. Translation: If the madhun sold something to the master at its fair price, it is valid, because the master is like a stranger regarding his earnings, when the madhun has debt that encompasses his earnings. And if he sold at a loss, it is not valid, because he is suspected regarding his right. Unlike if he showed favor to a stranger, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because there is no suspicion there. And unlike if a sick person sold to an heir at fair price, which is not valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the right of the remaining heirs has attached to the very substance of the item, to the extent that each heir has the right to redeem it by paying its price. As for the creditors, their right attaches only to the financial value. Commentary on his statement: 'And if he sold from the master, etc.' If a madhun sold something to his master at fair price, it is valid, but only if the slave is indebted, because then the master is like a stranger regarding his earnings. If he is not indebted, then sale between them is not valid, because the slave and everything he has is the master's property. And if the madhun sold to his master at a loss, it is not valid, because there is possibility of suspicion regarding his right. Benefit: When the reduction in price is less than the estimation of all valuators, e.g., some estimate an item at ten rupees and some at nine, then a loss up to nine is slight, and less than nine is excessive. His statement: 'Unlike if he showed favor...' Selling with a reduction in price is showing favor (muhabat). So if he showed favor to the master, it is not valid, as mentioned above. In contrast, if he showed favor to a stranger, it is valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because there is no suspicion. And in contrast, if a sick person sold something to his heir at fair price, it is not valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the right of the remaining heirs has attached to the very substance of the item, to the extent that if the deceased had debt, each heir has the right to redeem it by paying its price. Unlike the madhun, in the madhun's case, the creditors' right attaches only to the financial value...
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage deals with the rulings on a madhun (a slave permitted to trade) selling items to his master, both at fair price and at a loss, and draws comparisons with two other scenarios: a madhun showing favor to a stranger, and a sick person selling to his heir. The core principle is the presence or absence of suspicion (tuhmah) and the nature of the rights attached. If the madhun sells to his master at fair market value, the sale is valid when the madhun is indebted, because in that state the master is considered a stranger regarding the madhun's earnings—the master does not directly own the items due to the creditors' claims. If the madhun is not indebted, he and his property belong to the master, so a sale between them is meaningless (the master would be selling to himself). If the madhun sells to his master at a loss (muhabat), the sale is invalid due to suspicion that the madhun is favoring his master at the expense of creditors. This contrasts with a sale to a stranger at a loss, which is valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa because there is no suspicion that the madhun is favoring a stranger over creditors (the stranger is not the master). The passage then compares with the case of a sick person (marid) selling to his heir at fair price: Imam Abu Hanifa rules this invalid even at fair price, because the right of other heirs attaches to the very substance ('ayn) of the property, not just its financial value. Each heir can redeem the item by paying its price. In contrast, the creditors of a madhun only have a right to the financial value (maliyah), not to the specific items, so the analogy does not apply. The text also includes a practical note on what constitutes a slight versus excessive loss. The passage ends abruptly, likely concluding that in the madhun's case, the creditors' right is only to the value, not the substance, so the sale to the master at a loss is invalid due to suspicion, not due to attachment of rights to the substance.

Key Terms
محابات (muhabat): Selling at a price lower than the fair market value, showing favor to the buyer. The degree of loss may be slight (khafif) or excessive (fahish) depending on the valuers' estimates.
تہمت (tuhmah): Suspicion of wrongdoing; here, the possibility that the madhun is favoring the master to the detriment of creditors, which renders the sale at a loss invalid.
عین ('ayn): The specific substance or entity of an item, as opposed to its financial value (maliyah). In inheritance, the right of other heirs attaches to the 'ayn itself, giving them a right to redeem the specific item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a madhun's sale to his master at fair price valid when the madhun is indebted?
A) Because the master is the owner of the madhun and thus owns the item already.
B) Because the madhun needs the master's permission for any sale.
C) Because the master is considered a stranger regarding the madhun's earnings due to the creditors' claims.
D) Because the sale at fair price does not involve tuhmah.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a madhun's sale to his master hinges on the presence of tuhmah and the nature of rights attached ('ayn vs. maliyah).

The Master's Right to Free the Madhun's Slave When Debt Is Small
Ruling statement with talil reasoning and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a master freeing the madhun's slave when the debt does not encompass the slave's property and person, and explain the reasoning (talil) behind the consensus.

Core Idea
A small debt does not block the master's ownership rights; only an encompassing debt (dayn muhit) prevents freeing.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the fiqh of the madhun (trading slave). When the madhun owes a debt that does not cover all his assets, the master retains the right to free the madhun's own slave. This ruling is unanimous among the Hanafi imams, but the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa relies on a practical consideration: if a small debt were an obstacle, the purpose of granting the madhun permission to trade would be defeated. The passage also draws an analogy to inheritance, where a small debt does not prevent ownership transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the ruling
The subject is the master freeing the slave of a madhun (a slave permitted to trade). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) when the madhun's debt does not encompass his property and person.
Note the condition: the debt must not be muhit.
2
Recognize the consensus (ittifaq)
Both Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) agree on the validity. This is stated as 'bil-ittifaq' (by consensus).
This is unusual because usually there is ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning (talil)
Abu Hanifa argues that a small debt does not completely occupy the property; if it were a barrier, the master could never benefit from the madhun's earnings, nullifying the purpose of granting trade permission.
This is a teleological argument (maqsad-based).
4
Examine the analogy to inheritance
The passage compares to inheritance: a small debt does not prevent ownership (the heir inherits despite debts), but an encompassing debt (dayn muhit) does prevent it. This strengthens the principle that the obstacle is only when debt surrounds all assets.
This is a qiyas (analogy) from the chapter of inheritance.
5
Apply to distinguish dayn muhit vs non-muhit
The key distinction: if the debt equals or exceeds the madhun's total property and his personal value (dayn muhit), the master cannot free the slave. If the debt is less, freeing is valid.
The term 'dayn muhit' is defined here: it encompasses both wealth and person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وان لم يكن الدين ... الخ اور اگر عبد ماذون کا دین اس کے مال اور اس کی ذات کو محیط نہ ہو تو آقا کا عبد ماذون کے غلام کو آزاد کرنا بالاتفاق صحیح ہے۔ صاحبین کے نزدیک تو ظاہر ہے اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس لئے جائز ہے کہ قلیل قرضہ سے مال خالی نہیں ہوتا۔ اگر ایسا قرضہ بھی مانع سمجھا جائے تو عبد مازون کی کمائی سے آقا کے نفع اٹھانے کا دروازہ ہی بند ہو جائے گا اور اجازت دینے کا جو مقصد ہے وہ حاصل نہ ہوگا ۔ اسی لئے قلیل قرضہ ملک وارث سے مانع نہیں ہوتا اور دین محیط مانع ملک ہوتا ہے۔
His statement: 'And if the debt does not encompass...' etc. And if the debt of the madhun does not encompass his property and his person, then the master's freeing of the madhun's slave is valid by consensus. As for the Sahibayn, it is apparent. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is permissible because a small debt does not leave the property free. If such a debt were also considered an obstacle, then the door for the master to benefit from the madhun's earnings would be closed, and the purpose of granting permission would not be achieved. Therefore, a small debt does not prevent ownership of inheritance, and an encompassing debt prevents ownership.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with legal reasoning. It discusses the scenario where a madhun (a slave permitted to trade) has a debt that does not cover his entire property and person. In such a case, the master may free the slave (the madhun's own slave) and it is unanimously valid. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) obviously agree, and Imam Abu Hanifa justifies it by reasoning that a small debt does not completely encumber the property; if it did, the master would never benefit from the madhun's earnings, defeating the purpose of granting the madhun permission to trade. The passage analogizes to inheritance, where a small debt does not prevent ownership transfer, but a debt that encompasses the property does.

Key Terms
ماذون (madhun): A slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade; the slave can contract debts and transactions within the scope of permission.
دین محیط (dayn muhit): A debt that encompasses (surrounds) the slave's property and personal value, i.e., the total debt equals or exceeds the slave's assets and himself.
قلیل قرضہ (qalil qarza): A small debt that does not encompass the slave's property; it leaves some property free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Permissible only according to Imam Abu Hanifa, not the Sahibayn.
D) Permissible only if the master compensates the creditors.

Key Takeaway
A master may free the madhun's slave only if the madhun's debt does not encompass his property and person; this is a unanimous Hanafi ruling.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

The Madhun's Sale at a Loss to His Master: Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Inter-madhhab dispute on muhabah by a madhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi internal ikhtilaf regarding a madhun selling to his master below market price and apply the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) to a given scenario.

Core Idea
When a madhun sells to his master at a loss, suspicion (tuhmah) triggers a remedy; the Sahibayn's valid sale with option is mufta bihi.

A madhun (slave permitted to trade) who is in debt faces restrictions on his transactions to protect creditors. If he sells to his master at a loss (muhabah), the master's self-interest creates suspicion (tuhmah) of collusion. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa invalidates the sale outright, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) validate it but give the master an option to remedy the loss. This lesson unpacks their reasoning, the definition of muhabah, and the practical consequences for creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario and key actors
Recognize the parties: a madhun (slave permitted to trade) with debt, his master, and creditors. The act is a sale at a loss (muhabah).
The madhun's debts must exceed his earnings for the master to be treated like a stranger.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds the sale is invalid (batil) due to tuhmah (suspicion) that the master is favoring himself at the expense of creditors. He analogizes to a sick person selling to an heir.
The comparison with the sick person shows attachment of rights to the very thing ('ayn) vs. mere value (maliyyah).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
The Sahibayn hold the sale is valid (sahih) but the master has an option: either remove the muhabah by paying the market price difference, or annul the sale. Their concern is removing harm from creditors.
They treat slight (khafif) and excessive (fahish) muhabah equally in this ruling, unlike sales to strangers.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Under Abu Hanifa, the sale is null and the property returns; under the Sahibayn, it depends on the master's choice. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the Sahibayn's view because it balances validity with creditor protection.
Later Hanafi scholars (e.g., in al-Hidayah) adopt the Sahibayn's view as the operative ruling.

ترجمه صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر نقصان سے بیچی جب بھی بیع جائز ہے اور آقا کو اختیار دیا جائیگا ۔ چاہے محابات زائل کر لے چاہے بیع کو توڑ دے اور دونوں مذہبوں پر کم و بیش محابات برا بر ہے اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ بیع کا امتناع غرماء سے ضرر دور کرنے کے لئے ہے اور ان سے ضرر اسی طرح دور ہوگا اور یہ اجنبی کے ہاتھ محابات خفیفہ پر بیچنے کے خلاف ہے۔ کہ یہ جائز ہے اور اجنبی کو محابات دور کرنے کا حکم نہیں دیا جاتا اور آقا کو اسکا حکم دیا جاتا ہے اس لئے کہ محابات خفیفہ کے ساتھ بیچنا متردد ہے تبرع اور بیچ ہونے میں اس کمی کے داخل ہونے کی وجہ سے مقومین کے اندازہ کے تحت میں پس ہم نے اس کو آقا کے ہاتھ فروخت کرنے میں تبرع کا اعتبار کیا تہمت کی وجہ سے اور اجنبی کے حق میں غیر تبرع تہمت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے اور بخلاف اس کے جب اجنبی کے ہاتھ پر محابات فاحش پر فروخت کی کہ یہ صاحبین کے نزد یک بالکل جائز نہیں اور آقا کے ہاتھ جائز ہے مگر محابات کے ازالہ کا حکم کیا جائیگا۔ اس لئے کہ صاحبین کے اصول پر عبد ماذون کی طرف سے محابات کرنا جائز نہیں مگر آقا کی اجازت سے اور اجنبی کے ساتھ بیع کرنے میں اجازت نہیں ہے اورآ قا خود بیع کرنے کے ذریعہ سے اجازت دینے والا ہے صرف اتنی بات ہے کہ محابات کا ازالہ حق غرماء کی وجہ سے ہے۔ یہ دونوں فرق صاحبین کے اصول پر ہیں۔
Issues of Muhabah. He said: And if he (the madhun) sells something to the master for equivalent value, it is permissible because he (the master) is like a stranger regarding his earnings when there is a debt that encompasses his earnings. And if he sells at a loss, it is not permissible because he is suspected in his right. Unlike when he favors a stranger according to Abu Hanifa because there is no suspicion. And unlike when a sick person sells to an heir for equivalent value: it is not permissible according to him because the right of the remaining heirs pertains to the very thing, so each has the right to redeem it by paying its value. As for the creditors, their right pertains only to the value, so they differ. Translation: If the madhun sold something to the master for its equivalent value, it is permissible because the master is like a stranger regarding his earnings when the madhun has a debt that encompasses his earnings. And if he sold at a loss, it is not permissible because he is suspected regarding the master. Unlike when he favored a stranger according to Imam Abu Hanifa because there is no suspicion. And unlike when a sick person sold to an heir for equivalent value: it is not permissible according to him because the right of the remaining heirs has attached to the very thing, so each heir has the right to redeem it by paying its value. As for the creditors, their right only pertains to the value. Commentary on his statement: 'And if he sold from the master...' etc. If the madhun sold something to his master for equivalent value, it is permissible, but only when the slave is in debt, for then the master is like a stranger regarding his earnings. If he is not in debt, then the sale between them does not occur because the slave and what he owns all belong to the master. And if the madhun sold to his master at a loss, it is not permissible because of the possibility of suspicion. Benefit: When the reduction in price is less than the estimation of all valuators, e.g., some estimate an item at ten rupees and others at nine, then a loss up to nine is slight, and less than nine is excessive. His statement: 'Unlike when he favored...' etc. Selling with a reduction in price is muhabah. So if he favored the master, it is not permissible as mentioned above. Contrary to if he favored a stranger: according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is permissible because there is no suspicion. And contrary to when a sick person sold something to his heir for equivalent value: according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is not permissible because the right of the remaining heirs has attached to the very thing, even if the patient has debt, each heir has the option to pay its value and redeem it. Unlike the madhun's case: the creditors' right is only to the value, not to the thing itself. Thus the difference between the sale by a sick debtor to an heir and the sale by a debtor madhun to the master is clear. The view of the Sahibayn: And they said: If he sells at a loss, the sale is valid, and the master has the option: if he wishes, he may remove the muhabah, and if he wishes, he may annul the sale. And according to both schools, slight and excessive muhabah are equal. The reason is that the prohibition is to prevent harm to the creditors, and by this, harm is removed. This is contrary to selling to a stranger with slight muhabah: it is permissible and he is not ordered to remove the muhabah, while the master is ordered to do so because selling with slight muhabah is ambiguous between gift and sale because it falls under the valuation of the valuators. So we considered it a gift in the sale to the master due to suspicion, and not a gift regarding the stranger due to absence of suspicion. And contrary to when he sells to a stranger with excessive muhabah: it is absolutely not permissible according to them, while to the master it is permissible and he is ordered to remove the muhabah. Because according to their principle, muhabah by the madhun is not permissible without the master's permission, and there is no permission in the sale to a stranger, while the master gives permission by his very sale. The only issue is that removal of muhabah is due to the right of the creditors. These are the two differences according to their principle. Translation: The Sahibayn say: If he sells at a loss, the sale is still valid, and the master will be given an option: either he removes the muhabah or he annuls the sale. And according to both schools, slight and excessive muhabah are equal. The reason is that the forbiddance of the sale is to remove harm from the creditors, and harm is thus removed. This is contrary to selling to a stranger with slight muhabah: it is permissible and the stranger is not ordered to remove muhabah, while the master is ordered to do so because selling with slight muhabah is ambiguous between gift and sale due to the entry of the reduction under the estimation of the valuators. So we considered it a gift in the sale to the master due to suspicion, and not a gift regarding the stranger due to absence of suspicion. And contrary to when he sells to a stranger with excessive muhabah: it is absolutely not permissible according to them, while to the master it is permissible but he is ordered to remove the muhabah. Because according to their principle, muhabah by the madhun is not permissible without the master's permission, and there is no permission in the sale to a stranger, while the master himself is the one giving permission through his own sale. The only point is that the removal of muhabah is due to the right of the creditors. These are the two differences according to their principle.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute (between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) on the rulings of muhabah (selling below market price) by a madhun (slave permitted to trade). The passage first states the basic ruling: if the madhun sells to his master at the fair price, it is valid; but if at a loss, it is invalid because of suspicion (tuhmah). However, if the madhun sells at a loss to a stranger, according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is valid because no suspicion exists. The passage then compares this to a sick person selling to an heir at fair price: it is invalid because the heir has a right in the very thing. The creditors in the madhun's case only have a right to the value. The commentary clarifies the conditions and defines slight (khafif) and excessive (fahish) muhabah based on the variation among valuators. Then the Sahibayn's view is presented: they say the sale at a loss to the master is valid, but the master has an option to either remove the muhabah (by paying the difference) or annul the sale. Both slight and excessive muhabah are treated equally. Their reasoning is to prevent harm to creditors. They distinguish between slight muhabah to a stranger (valid) and slight muhabah to the master (option given due to suspicion). They also distinguish excessive muhabah: to a stranger it is absolutely invalid, but to the master it is valid with the option because the master's own permission in the sale is implied. The passage concludes by stating that these two differences are based on the Sahibayn's principle. This is a detailed example of how suspicion (tuhmah) and the protection of creditors' rights shape Hanafi commercial law.

Key Terms
محابات (muhabah): Selling an item at a price lower than its market value; a form of favoritism that results in a loss to the seller's creditors.
ماذون (madhun): A slave whom the master has given permission to engage in trade; his transactions are valid within the scope of permission, but he cannot make gifts or do muhabah without the master's consent.
تہمت (tuhmah): Suspicion of partiality or bias; a legal cause to invalidate or restrict a transaction to protect the rights of others (creditors).
مقومین (muqawwimin): Experts who appraise the market value of items; their estimation is used to determine whether a reduction is slight or excessive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what is the ruling when a madhun in debt sells an item to his master at a loss?
A) The sale is invalid and must be annulled.
B) The sale is valid and the master has no further obligation.
C) The sale is valid but the master may either remove the muhabah by paying the difference or annul the sale.
D) The sale is valid only if the master pays the fair price immediately.

Key Takeaway
For a madhun's sale at a loss to his master, the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view: the sale is valid but the master has an option to pay the difference or annul the sale.

Muhabah Sale by Authorized Slave to Master: Dispute of Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on muhabah sale to master vs. stranger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of a muhabah sale by a debt-ridden authorized slave to his master, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two rulings.

Core Idea
A muhabah sale to the master is valid with option according to Sahibayn, but void according to Abu Hanifa – the creditors' rights are decisive.

In this passage, we examine a key intra-Hanafi dispute: a debt-ridden authorized slave (ma'dhun madin) sells property to his own master at a price below market value. Does the suspicion of favoritism invalidate the sale outright, or can it be saved with an option for the master? Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn) differ sharply, and their reasoning reveals deep principles about the interplay of trade authorization, debts, and suspicion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and context
Recognize that the seller is a ma'dhun slave who owes debts (ghuramā have claims). The buyer is his master. The price is below market (muhabah). This triggers suspicion of favoritism.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and its basis
Abu Hanifa rules the sale void due to tuhmah (suspicion) and harm to creditors. His reasoning: the slave may favor his master, undermining creditors' rights.
His position is not elaborated in this passage, but the contrast is clear.
3
State Sahibayn's ruling and its basis
Sahibayn rule the sale valid but give the master an option (takhyir): either remove the muhabah (pay the deficiency) or annul the sale. They argue the master's personal involvement implies permission, but creditors' rights necessitate the option.
This option protects creditors: if the master pays the full value, no harm; if he annuls, the property returns and creditors can access it.
4
Analyze the distinction between slight and excessive muhabah, and sale to stranger vs master
Sahibayn treat slight muhabah to a stranger as valid without option, but excessive muhabah to a stranger as void. In contrast, any muhabah to the master gives an option. The difference is the presence of suspicion (tuhmah) in the master case.
The master is ordered to remove muhabah because suspicion exists, whereas a stranger is not ordered because suspicion is absent.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, the sale fails; property stays with slave, creditors can claim it. Under Sahibayn, the sale stands unless the master chooses to annul; if he pays deficiency, creditors are satisfied.
The passage does not give tarjih, but later Hanafi practice often adopts Sahibayn's view.

The book of the authorized slave. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah vol. 12. It relates to [the thing] not to the essence. So the difference between the sale of a debt-ridden sick person to an heir and the sale of a debt-ridden authorized slave to his master became clear. The view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): They said: If he sells it at a deficiency, the sale is valid, and the master is given an option: if he wishes he may remove the favoritism (muhabah), and if he wishes he may annul the sale. And according to both schools, the small amount of favoritism and the excessive amount are the same. The reason is that refraining [from the sale] is for repelling harm from the creditors, and with this their harm is repelled. This is unlike the sale to a stranger with slight favoritism, for it is valid and he is not ordered to remove the favoritism, whereas the master is ordered to do so. Because the sale with slight [favoritism] is ambiguous between donation and sale, due to its inclusion under the valuation of the valuators. So we considered it a donation in the sale with the master due to suspicion, and not a donation in the case of the stranger due to its absence. And unlike when he sells to a stranger with a lot [of favoritism], for it is absolutely not valid according to them, but [when selling] to the master it is valid and he is ordered to remove the favoritism, because according to their principle favoritism from the authorized slave is not valid except with the permission of the master, and there is no permission in the sale with the stranger, whereas he [the master] is one who permits by personally undertaking it, except that the removal of it is due to the right of the creditors. This is the two distinctions according to their principle.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of a debt-ridden authorized slave (madhun made dayn) to his master at a price lower than market value (muhabah). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that such a sale is invalid altogether due to suspicion (tuhmah) and harm to creditors. The two companions (Sahibayn — Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ: they rule that the sale is valid but the master has an option either to make up the deficiency (remove the muhabah) or to annul the sale. They argue that since the master is personally undertaking the transaction, his involvement implies implicit permission, but the right of the creditors necessitates the option. They also distinguish between slight and excessive muhabah: when selling to a stranger, slight muhabah is valid without option, but excessive muhabah is invalid. The reasoning is based on the presence or absence of suspicion (tuhmah) and the dual nature of the contract as potentially donation or sale. The passage concludes that these two distinctions are according to Sahibayn's principle (usul), not Abu Hanifa's.

Key Terms
ماذون (ma'dhun): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade; the master is liable for debts incurred.
محاباتہ (muhabah): Selling at a price lower than market value; favoritism or undervaluation.
غرماء (ghuramā): Creditors who have a claim on the slave's earnings or the master's liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The sale is valid, and the master must pay the full market price.
C) The sale is valid, and the master has an option to either make up the deficiency or annul the sale.
D) The sale is valid only if the master pays the exact market value immediately.

Key Takeaway
In the sale of a debt-ridden authorized slave to his master with muhabah, Abu Hanifa deems it void, while Sahibayn uphold it with an option for the master to restore the deficiency or annul.

Master Selling to Slave: Ruling on Price and Delivery
States the ruling when a master sells something to his slave at market value or less

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master sells an item to his indebted slave at market value or less, and explain the condition under which the price becomes void.

Core Idea
A master's sale to his slave is valid at market price; delivery before payment voids the price unless it is a commodity.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from al-Hidayah regarding transactions between a master and his authorized slave. The key issue is whether such a sale is valid given the potential conflict of interest. The Hanafi school answers with a nuanced ruling that balances the master's benefit with the slave's limited liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the transaction
The master sells something to his own slave. The price is set at the market value or less. Understand the context: the slave is authorized to trade and has debts.
The master is considered a 'stranger' regarding the slave's earnings when the slave is indebted, eliminating suspicion of favoritism.
2
State the default ruling
The sale is valid (ja'iz). The justification is twofold: the master is a stranger to the slave's earnings (no conflict of interest), and the sale is beneficial to both parties.
Benefit: the item enters the slave's capital, and the master gains the ability to collect the price.
3
Analyze the effect of delivery before payment
If the master delivers the sold item to the slave before receiving the price, the price becomes void (bāṭil). This is because the master's right over the item was one of retention (ḥabs). Upon delivery, that right falls and cannot transform into a debt owed by the slave.
The price cannot be claimed later because the slave's liability is limited; the master loses both item and price.
4
Recognize the exception
If the price is a specific commodity ('araḍ) rather than money, the master's right can remain attached to that commodity. Therefore, delivery before payment does not void the price; the master can still claim the commodity.
The rule differs because a commodity is specific (mu'ayyan) and can be subject to retention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وان باعه المولى شيئا بمثل القيمة او اقل جاز البيع لان المولى اجنبى عن كسبه اذا كان عليه دين على ما بيناه ولا تهمة فى هذا البيع ولانه مفيد فانه يدخل في كسب العبد مالم يكن فيه ويتمكن المولى من اخذ الثمن بعد ان لم يكن له هذا التمكن وصحة التصرف تتبع الفائدة فان سلم اليه قبل قبض الثمن بطل الثمن لان حق المولى فى العين من حيث الحبس فلو بقى بعد سقوطه يبقى فى الدين ولا يستوجبه المولى على عبده بخلاف ما اذا كان الثمن عرضا لانه يتعين وجاز ان يبقى خقه متعلقا بالعين. قال وان امسكه في يده
He said: And if the master sells something to him at the equivalent of the value or less, the sale is valid, because the master is a stranger to his earnings when there is a debt upon him as we have explained, and there is no suspicion in this sale. And because it is beneficial, for it enters into the slave's earnings that which was not in it, and the master becomes able to take the price after not having that ability, and the validity of the transaction follows the benefit. If he hands [the item] over to him before receiving the price, the price becomes void, because the master's right in the item is in terms of retention (habs). If it remains after its fall, it remains as a debt, and the master does not owe it to his slave. Unlike if the price is a commodity ('arad), because it becomes specified and it is possible that his right remains attached to the item. He said: And if he keeps it in his hand...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling by the author (al-Quduri or al-Marghinani) on the scenario where a master sells an item to his own authorized slave. The ruling: If the master sells at market price or less, the sale is valid. The reason is that the master is considered a stranger concerning the slave's earnings when the slave is indebted; there is no suspicion of favoritism in this sale. Moreover, the sale is beneficial because the item enters the slave's capital and the master gains the ability to collect the price. The principle that validity of a transaction follows its benefit is applied. However, a special condition arises: if the master delivers the sold item to the slave before receiving the price, the price becomes void (bâtil). This is because the master's right over the item was a right of retention (habs) until payment. Once delivery occurs, that right falls, and if the price remains unpaid, it becomes a debt that the master cannot claim from the slave (since the slave's liability is limited). The exception is if the price is a specific commodity ('arad), not money; then the master's right can remain attached to that commodity, so delivery before payment does not void the price. This ruling protects the master from loss while respecting the limits of the slave's liability.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale; here specifically money (naqd) as opposed to a commodity ('arad).
حبس (habs): Retention; the right of the seller to keep the sold item until payment is received.
عرض ('arad): A commodity or goods, as opposed to money (thaman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale becomes void along with the price.
B) The price remains payable as a debt owed by the slave.
C) The price becomes void (bâtil) and the master cannot claim it.
D) The master can demand the price at any time.

Key Takeaway
A master's sale to his indebted slave at market price or less is valid, but early delivery voids the price unless the price is a commodity.

Ma'dhun's Undervalued Sale: Master vs Stranger
Ruling on ma'dhun's sale with undervaluation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling for an indebted ma'dhun's sale with undervaluation and explain the distinction between master and stranger in both slight and excessive undervaluation.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun's undervalued sale to his master triggers suspicion of favoritism, while a sale to a stranger does not.

In this lesson, we examine the rules governing a ma'dhun slave who sells goods to his own master or to a stranger at an undervalued price. The Hanafi school must balance the slave's trading capacity against the rights of creditors and the suspicion that arises when the slave deals with his master. We will trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and degree of undervaluation
Determine whether the sale involves slight or excessive undervaluation (muhabah khafifah/fahishah) and whether the buyer is the master or a stranger.
Slight undervaluation: a small deviation from market price; excessive: a large deviation that would be considered fraud (ghabn fahish).
2
State the ruling for slight undervaluation according to the Sahibayn
For slight undervaluation, the sale is valid whether with the master or a stranger, but the master is ordered to remove the undervaluation (compensate the deficiency to protect creditors). The stranger is not ordered to do so.
Reason: ambiguity between gift and sale; with master, suspicion of favoritism tilts towards gift; with stranger, no suspicion.
3
State the ruling for excessive undervaluation according to the Sahibayn
For excessive undervaluation, the sale to the master is valid but he must remove the undervaluation; the sale to a stranger is absolutely invalid.
Reason: the ma'dhun cannot make excessive undervaluation without the master's permission; the master's direct transaction implies permission, but with a stranger permission is lacking.
4
Contrast with Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that any undervaluation (slight or excessive) with the master invalidates the sale due to strong suspicion. For excessive undervaluation with a stranger, he considers the sale valid but the slave ordered to remove the undervaluation.
This creates only one distinction: master (invalid) vs stranger (valid but ordered to remove) in excessive undervaluation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the reasoning
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn, as preferred by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah ('ala aslihima'). The key principles are suspicion (tuhma) for slight undervaluation with master and implied permission for excessive undervaluation with master.
The mufta bihi balances the rights of creditors with the validity of transactions, requiring compensation where suspicion exists but not invalidating outright.

Source Text

قوله وهذان الفرقان .... الخ بعض نسخوں میں ” وهذا الفرقان بلفظ افراد ہے پہلی صورت میں لفظ فرقان نون مکسورہ کے ساتھ ہے کیونکہ یہ تثنیہ کا نون ہے اور دوسری صورت میں نون مرفوع ہے اور فرقان بضم فاء مثل غفران بمعنی فرق ہے صاحب نہا یہ فرماتے ہیں ۔ کہ هذان الفرقان اصح ہے کیونکہ صاحبین کے قول پر یہ دونوں فرق موجود ہیں اور صحیح نسخوں میں یہی ثابت ہے پھر صاحب ہدایہ نے علی اصلهما‘ اس لئے کہا کہ جب امام ابو حنیفہ نے آقا کے ہاتھ بیع کو جائز ہی نہیں رکھا نہ نین بیر کے ساتھ اور نہ ہی غبن فاحش کے ساتھ تو ان کے قول کے لئے ان دو فرقوں کی احتیاج نہیں ہے بلکہ صرف ایک ہی فرق کی ضرورت ہے اور وہ نین فاحش کے ساتھ اجنبی کے ہاتھ بیع کا جائز ہونا اور آقا کے ہاتھ جائز نہ ہونا ہے۔

Book of the Ma'dhun. Explanation of Al-Hidayah in Urdu volume 12: A sale by the slave of his master with slight undervaluation: this sale is valid, and the stranger is not ordered to remove the undervaluation while the master is ordered to remove it. The reason is that the contract made with the master with slight undervaluation is ambiguous between gift (tabarru') and sale (bay'). It is a gift because in the amount of undervaluation the contract of sale is devoid of price, and it is a sale because that deficiency is included in the valuation of the assessors. So we considered both aspects. Thus when a indebted ma'dhun slave made such a sale to his master, due to suspicion we considered it a gift, and when he made such a sale to a stranger, due to absence of suspicion we considered it a sale. His saying: 'from the stranger with much (excessive) ... etc.' The mentioned ruling is for slight undervaluation. But if the indebted ma'dhun slave sold to a stranger with excessive undervaluation, according to the Sahibayn it is absolutely invalid, and with the master it is valid, but the master will be ordered to remove the undervaluation because the principle of the Sahibayn is that the ma'dhun is not permitted to make undervaluation except with the master's permission. With the stranger this permission is lacking, while with the master it is present because the master's direct transaction itself constitutes permission. The only thing is that due to the right of the creditors the master will be ordered to remove the undervaluation. His saying: 'And these two distinctions ... etc.' In some copies it is 'hādha al-furqān' (singular). In the first form the word 'furqān' has a kasra on the nun because it is the nun of dual, and in the second form the nun is raised, and 'furqān' with damma on the fa like 'ghufrān' meaning distinction. The author of Nihāyah says that 'hādhāni al-furqān' is correct because there are these two distinctions in the statement of the Sahibayn, and this is established in correct copies. Then the author of Hidayah said 'alā aṣlihimā' (according to their principle) because when Imam Abu Hanifa did not consider the sale to the master valid at all, neither with slight nor with excessive undervaluation, then for his statement there is no need for these two distinctions; rather only one distinction is needed, and that is: the validity of the sale to a stranger with excessive undervaluation and its invalidity to the master. The meaning of his statement is that in the case of slight undervaluation the distinction between master and stranger is that the master will be ordered to remove the undervaluation, while the stranger will not be ordered. In the case of excessive undervaluation, the master will be ordered to remove the undervaluation, and if it occurs with a stranger such a sale is absolutely invalid. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the sale to the master with slight undervaluation is invalid, and excessive undervaluation: if with the master it is also invalid, and if with the stranger it is valid but he will be ordered to remove the undervaluation.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya deals with the rules of a ma'dhun slave selling goods to his own master or to a stranger when the sale involves undervaluation (muhabah). The discussion is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The passage first explains the ruling for slight undervaluation: the sale is valid in both cases, but the master is ordered to remove the undervaluation out of suspicion (tuhma) that the slave is favoring his master at the expense of creditors, while the stranger is not so ordered. The reason is that such a sale is ambiguous between gift and sale; when the slave deals with his master, the element of gift is dominant due to suspicion, so it is treated as a gift requiring restitution. When dealing with a stranger, there is no suspicion, so it is treated as a valid sale. Then the passage discusses excessive undervaluation. According to the Sahibayn, the ma'dhun cannot make an excessive undervaluation unless the master explicitly permits it. Since the master's direct transaction with the slave constitutes implicit permission, the sale to the master is valid but the master must remove the excess to protect creditors; the sale to a stranger is absolutely invalid because permission is absent. Imam Abu Hanifa differs: he considers any sale to the master with undervaluation (slight or excessive) invalid due to the strong suspicion, but a sale to a stranger with excessive undervaluation is valid, though the slave is ordered to remove it. The author of al-Hidayah, al-Marghinani, adopts the view of the Sahibayn ('alā aṣlihimā), meaning the relied-upon position is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The passage also clarifies a textual issue: the phrase 'hādhāni al-furqān' (these two distinctions) is correct because for the Sahibayn there are two distinctions—first between master and stranger in slight undervaluation (master ordered to remove, stranger not), and second between master and stranger in excessive undervaluation (master: valid but ordered to remove; stranger: invalid). For Abu Hanifa, only one distinction is needed: between master (invalid) and stranger (valid but ordered to remove).

Key Terms
مَأْذُون (ma'dhūn): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade, and whose transactions are valid as long as they do not harm the creditors if the slave is indebted.
مُحَابَاة (muḥābāh): Undervaluation in a sale, i.e., selling for less than the fair market value. It is divided into slight (khafīf) and excessive (fāḥish).
صَاحِبَان (Ṣāḥibān): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Their positions are often contrasted with Abu Hanifa's.
غَبْن فَاحِش (ghabn fāḥish): Excessive mispricing or fraud, often used synonymously with 'muhabah fahishah' in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Sahibayn), what is the ruling if an indebted ma'dhun slave sells goods to a stranger with excessive undervaluation?
A) The sale is valid, and the slave is not ordered to remove the undervaluation.
B) The sale is absolutely invalid.
C) The sale is valid, but the stranger is ordered to remove the undervaluation.
D) The sale is only valid if the master explicitly permits it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school balances the ma'dhun's trading capacity with creditor rights, distinguishing between slight and excessive undervaluation and between master and stranger based on suspicion and permission.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Master Selling to Ma'dhun at Fair Value and the Right of Retention
Ruling on master selling to ma'dhun at fair value, delivery, and retention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master sells to his indebted ma'dhun slave at or below market value and explain the legal effect of delivery before payment and the seller's right of retention.

Core Idea
A master may sell to his ma'dhun slave at fair value without suspicion, but the right of retention is crucial for the master's protection.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: a master selling an item to his own ma'dhun (permitted) slave who is in debt. At first glance, one might suspect the master could favor himself at the expense of the slave's creditors. However, the Hanafi school explains why such a sale is not only valid but beneficial, and then details the consequences of delivery and the master's right to retain the item until payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their status
The master is the seller; the ma'dhun slave is the buyer who has existing debts. The master is considered a 'stranger to the slave's earnings' because the slave's earnings go to creditors.
This principle establishes that the master has no conflict of interest.
2
Determine the price condition
The sale is valid when the price is at the equivalent value (miṯl al-qīmah) or less. There is no taṯammah (suspicion) because the master is not favoring himself.
The sale is beneficial: the slave acquires an asset, increasing his earnings for the creditors.
3
Analyze the effect of delivery before payment
If the master delivers the item before receiving the price, the price debt becomes void. This is because the master's right of retention (ḥaqq al-ḥabs) falls, and he cannot claim a debt from his own slave.
Exception: if the price is a specific commodity, the master's right remains attached to that commodity.
4
Examine the right of retention
The master has the right to retain the sold item until the price is paid, just like any seller. He is even more entitled than other creditors regarding that specific property.
This right exists only when the price is a specific property (e.g., money or goods).

If the master sells something to the ma'dhun at the equivalent value or less, it is valid. He said: And if the master sells something to him at the equivalent value or less, the sale is valid because the master is a stranger to his earnings when he has a debt, as we explained, and there is no suspicion in this sale, and it is beneficial because something enters the slave's earnings that was not there, and the master becomes able to take the price after not having such ability. The validity of a transaction follows benefit. If he (the master) delivers it to him before receiving the price, the price becomes void because the master's right in the specific property is by way of retention. If after it falls, it remains as a debt, and the master does not become entitled to it from his slave, unlike if the price is a commodity because it is specific, and it is permissible for his right to remain attached to the specific property. He said: And if he holds it in his hand until he receives the price, it is valid because the seller has the right of retention in the sold item, and for this reason he is more entitled to it than other creditors, and it is permissible for the master to have a right in a debt if it is attached to a specific property. And if he sells it to him for more than its value, he will be ordered to remove the undervaluation or annul the sale, as we explained regarding the slave, because the increase is attached to the right of the creditors. He said: And if the master frees the ma'dhun while he has debts, his emancipation is valid because his ownership over him remains, and the master is liable for his value to the creditors; he destroyed what their right was attached to, as they could have sold him and recovered from his price. What remains of the debts is demanded after emancipation because the debt is in his (the slave's) liability, and nothing became obligatory on the master except as a guarantee for what he destroyed. So the remainder remains on him as it was. If it (the debt) is less than his value, he guarantees only the debt, because their right is only to that extent. Unlike if he frees a mudabbar or umm walad who are ma'dhun and debts have ridden them, because the right of the creditors was not attached to their persons by way of sale, so the master did not destroy their right, so he does not guarantee anything. Translation: And if the master sold something to the ma'dhun at equal value or less, it is valid because his earnings are like a stranger's when the ma'dhun has a debt, and there is no suspicion in this sale, and it is beneficial because something enters the slave's earnings that was not there, and the master becomes able to take the price whereas before he did not have that ability. The validity of a transaction follows benefit. And if the master sold it to him for more than its value, he will be ordered to remove the undervaluation or annul the sale, as we explained regarding the slave, because the increase is connected to the right of the creditors. If the master freed the ma'dhun while he has debts, his freeing is valid because his ownership over him remains, and the master will be the guarantor of his value to the creditors because he destroyed a thing to which their right was attached, in that they could have sold him and collected from his price. Whatever remains of the debts will be demanded after emancipation because the debt is in his liability, and only as much became incumbent on the master as he destroyed as a guarantee, so the remaining debt remains on the ma'dhun as it was. Then if the debt is less than the slave's value, the master only guarantees the debt because the creditors' right is only to that extent. Unlike if he freed a mudabbar ma'dhun or umm walad ma'dhunah while debts had accumulated on them, because the creditors' right was not attached to their persons in a way that they could be sold and collected from, so the master did not destroy their right and will not guarantee anything. Explanation: If the master sells something to his ma'dhun slave at the full price or at a loss, this is unanimously valid because when the slave has a debt, the master is a stranger to his earnings, and there is no suspicion in this sale. Also it is beneficial because something enters the slave's earnings that was not there, so the creditors suffer no loss. Then the master...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings concerning transactions between a master and his ma'dhun slave who is indebted. The key principles are: (1) When a master sells something to his ma'dhun slave at the fair market value or less, the sale is valid and beneficial. The master is considered a stranger to the slave's earnings because the slave's earnings are for the creditors. There is no suspicion here because the slave is not favoring the master at the creditors' expense; rather, the slave acquires an asset that increases his earnings. (2) If the master delivers the item before receiving payment, the price debt is void because the master's right of retention in the specific item falls, and he cannot have a debt claim against his own slave. However, if the price is a specific commodity, the master's right can remain attached to that commodity. (3) The master has the right to retain the sold item until payment is made, as he has a superior right of retention (haqq al-habs) like any seller. (4) If the master sells at more than the value, the excess is considered an undervaluation (muhabah) because it harms the creditors, so the master is ordered to remove it or the sale is annulled. (5) If the master frees the ma'dhun while he is in debt, the emancipation is valid but the master becomes liable for the slave's value (or the amount of the debt, whichever is less) as a guarantee to the creditors, because the master's act of emancipation destroys the creditors' right to sell the slave to recover their debts. The freed slave remains liable for any remaining debt beyond the value. (6) Special cases: For a mudabbar (deferred emancipation) or umm walad (mother of the master's child) who are also ma'dhun, if the master frees them while they are in debt, he is not liable because their persons were not subject to sale for debt recovery; thus the creditors' right was not attached to their persons in the same way. The passage ends with a clarification that the ruling on the master selling to the slave is unanimous.

Key Terms
حَقّ الحَبْس (ḥaqq al-ḥabs): The right of retention that a seller has over the sold item until the price is paid. It gives the seller priority over other creditors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The price remains a debt enforceable against the slave.
B) The price becomes void.
C) The master can reclaim the item.
D) The sale is void.

Key Takeaway
A master's sale to his ma'dhun slave at fair value is valid and beneficial, but delivering without payment voids the price due to the nature of the retention right.

Overpricing by Master and Emancipation of Indebted Ma'dhun
Rulings on overpricing and emancipation with debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling when a master sells above market value to his ma'dhun slave and the consequences of freeing an indebted ma'dhun, including the distinction for mudabbar and umm walad.

Core Idea
Selling above value harms creditors; freeing an indebted ma'dhun makes the master liable for the price, but not for protected slaves like mudabbar or umm walad.

This lesson continues from the previous, now considering two scenarios: (1) the master sells at an inflated price, which may harm the slave's creditors, and (2) the master frees the indebted ma'dhun, which destroys the creditors' right to sell the slave. The passage provides remedies and clarifies situations where the master is not liable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overpricing ruling
If the master sells at more than the value, the excess is considered muhabah (undervaluation) that harms creditors. The master is commanded to remove the excess or annul the sale.
This mirrors the rule when the slave himself sells above value, as previously discussed.
2
Analyze emancipation of indebted ma'dhun
The master's emancipation is valid because his ownership remains. However, he is liable for the slave's value to the creditors, because he destroyed their right to sell the slave to recover debts.
The slave remains liable for any debt exceeding the value.
3
Distinguish cases: mudabbar and umm walad
If the freed slave is a mudabbar or umm walad (who are also ma'dhun), the master is not liable. Their persons are protected from sale for debt, so the creditors' right was not attached to their bodies.
The creditors cannot sell these slaves, so the master's freeing does not destroy their right.
4
Examine the liability cap
If the debt is less than the slave's value, the master guarantees only the debt amount, up to the value.
He does not guarantee more than the creditors' loss.
