Validity of a Slave's Trade by Istihsan
Ruling with proof on slave's trade validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a slave's trade is valid when he claims or appears authorized, and explain how istihsan overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
A slave's claim of authorization in trade is accepted by istihsan to avoid hardship.

In this passage, al-Hidayah presents a ruling that seems to go against strict analogy: a slave who comes to a city and says 'I am a slave of so-and-so' and then trades is considered authorized. The text explains that even if he does not mention permission, his transactions are valid because people's affairs are presumed sound. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of istihsan that overrides qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (a slave buying and selling) and the hukm (valid and binding).
The ruling is that everything from trade is binding on him.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is istihsan, based on the principle of acting on appearances in transactions.
The text states: 'العمل بالظاهر هو الاصل في المعاملات'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the principle of acting on appearances leads to the ruling: since the slave's actions (buying/selling) appear to be that of an authorized person, we presume he is authorized.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) require proof of authorization (qiyas) and do not accept the slave's claim without evidence.
The text mentions qiyas would require proof, but istihsan is preferred.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school adopts istihsan over qiyas to avoid hardship and because acting on appearances is the foundation of transactions.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص نے شہر میں آ کر کہا کہ میں فلاں کا غلام ہوں پھر اس نے خرید وفروخت کی ہر وہ چیز جو از قسم تجارت ہے لازم ہوگی
قال ومن قدم مصرا فقال انا عبد لفلان فاشترى و باع لزمه كل شيء من التجارة لانه ان اخبر بالاذن فالاخبار دليل عليه وان لم يخبر فتصرفه جائز اذ الظاهر ان المحجور على موجب حجره والعمل بالظاهر هو الاصل في المعاملات كيلا يضيق الأمر على الناس الا انه لا يباع حتى يحضر مولاه لانه لا يقبل قوله في الرقبة لانها خالص حق المولى بخلاف الكسب لانه حق العبد على العبد ما بيناه فان حضر وقال هو ماذون بيع في الدين لانه ظهر الدين فى حق المولى وان قال هو محجور فالقول قوله لانه متمسک بالاصل
ترجمہ: ایک شخص نے شہر میں اگر کہا کہ میں فلاں کا غلام ہوں پھر اس نے خرید و فروخت کی تو اس پر لازم ہوگی ہر وہ چیز جواز قسم تجارت ہو اس لئے کہ اگر اس نے اجازت پانے کی خبر دی ہے تو اس کا خبر دینا خود اس پر دلیل ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے یہ خبر نہیں دی تو اس کا تصرف جائز ہے کیونکہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ مجو شخص اپنے حجر کے مطابق ہی عمل کریگا۔ اور ظاہر پر عمل کرنا ہی اصل ہے معاملات میں تا کہ لوگوں پر معاملہ تنگ نہ ہو جائے ۔لیکن غلام فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ یہاں تک کہ اس کا آقا حاضر ہو کیونکہ اپنے رقبہ کی بابت اس کا قول قبول نہیں کیا جا سکتا اس لئے کہ رتبہ تو خالص آقا کا حق ہے بخلاف کمائی کے کہ وہ غلام کا حق ہے۔ پس اگر آقا آ کر کہے کہ وہ ماذون ہے تو قرضہ میں فروخت کر دیا جائے گا کیونکہ یہ قرضہ آقا کے حق میں بھی ظاہر ہو گیا اور اگر آقا نے کہا کہ وہ مجور ہے تو اس کا قول مقبول ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ وہ اصل سے حجت پکڑ رہا ہے۔
تشریح قوله ومن قدم مصراً ... الخ - ایک شخص نے شہر میں آکر کہا کہ میں فلاں ( مثلا زید ) کا غلام ہوں پھر اس نے خریدو فروخت کی تو جو چیز بھی از قسم تجارت ہو وہ اس پر لازم ہوگی اب یہ مسئلہ دو وجہ پر ہے ایک یہ کہ اس نے اجازت پانے کی خبر دی۔ یعنی اپنا مازون ہونا بیان کیا تو استحسانا اس کی تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ خواہ وہ عادل ہو یا غیر عادل اور مقتضائے قیاس یہ ہے کہ اس کی تصدیق نہ کی جائے ۔ چنانچہ ائمہ ثلاثہ اسی کے قائل ہیں ۔ کیونکہ یہ اس کی طرف سے ایک دعوی ہے تو بلا حجت تصدیق نہیں ہو سکتی۔
ولما فرغ من بيان احكام اذن العبد في التجارة شرع في بيان احكام اذن الصبى و المعتوه و قدم الاول لكثر قوعه ۱۲ نتائج -
A person entered a city and said, 'I am the slave of so-and-so,' then he bought and sold. Everything that is of the type of trade is binding on him. He said: And whoever enters a city and says, 'I am the slave of so-and-so,' then buys and sells, everything pertaining to trade is binding upon him. Because if he informed of permission, the information is evidence for it. If he did not inform, his transaction is valid, since the apparent situation is that a person under interdiction acts according to his interdiction, and acting according to appearances is the foundation in transactions so that matters are not made difficult for people. However, he is not sold until his master presents himself, because his statement is not accepted regarding his person, for it is the pure right of the master, unlike earnings which are the right of the slave. As we have explained. Then if the master comes and says, 'He is authorized,' he is sold for the debt because the debt became apparent in relation to the master. If he says, 'He is interdicted,' then his statement is accepted because he clings to the original. Translation: If a person in the city said, 'I am the slave of so-and-so,' then bought and sold, then everything that is of the type of trade is binding on him. Because if he informed of having permission, his informing is evidence of it. And if he did not inform, his transaction is valid because the apparent situation is that the interdicted person acts according to his interdiction. And acting on the apparent is the foundation in transactions so that matters are not made difficult for people. But the slave will not be sold until his master appears, because his statement regarding his person is not accepted, for that is the pure right of the master, unlike earnings which are the right of the slave. So if the master comes and says he is authorized, he will be sold for the debt because the debt became apparent in the right of the master. And if the master says he is interdicted, his statement is accepted because he clings to the original. Explanation of his statement 'And whoever enters a city...' - A person entered a city and said, 'I am the slave of so-and-so (e.g., Zaid),' then he bought and sold. So whatever is of the type of trade is binding on him. Now this issue has two aspects: one is that he informed of having permission, i.e., he stated that he is ma'dhun. Then by istihsan, his statement is believed, whether he is just or unjust. And the requirement of qiyas is that his statement should not be believed. Indeed, the three Imams (Imam Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) hold this view, because it is a claim from him, and without evidence, belief cannot be given. And he said, 'Indeed, the basis in this is that when something becomes difficult for people, His judgment becomes lenient, and when its affliction becomes widespread, its ruling falls away.' The second aspect of the issue is that he only buys and sells and does not inform of anything, i.e., he does not tell that his master has authorized him. In this case, the requirement of qiyas is that authorization is not proven. Indeed, in one opinion, Imam Shafi'i holds this view, because silence admits probability. But by istihsan, authorization is proven because the apparent situation is that he is authorized, because the affairs of Muslims are to be construed as sound as far as possible, and the validity of his transaction cannot be proven without authorization, so it will be construed as authorization. And acting on the apparent situation is the foundation in transactions so that matters are not made difficult for people. The conclusion is that if he informed of having permission, his informing is evidence of it. And if he did not inform of being ma'dhun, still his transaction is valid. In any case, he is considered ma'dhun, and any debt incurred in trade matters will be binding on him. His statement 'except that he is not sold' is an exception from the previous statement 'everything is binding on him.' The meaning is that the aforementioned slave will not be sold for the debt that is binding on him until his master appears, because his statement regarding his person is not accepted, for that is the pure right of the master, as has passed earlier on page 308, where he said: 'And his debt is attached to his earnings... until he said, 'Because the master only succeeds him in ownership after his freedom from the need of the slave.' Then if after appearing the master says he is ma'dhun, he will be sold for the debt (if the master does not pay his debt), because by the master's saying that he is ma'dhun, this debt becomes apparent in the right of the master. And if the master says that he is mahjur, then his statement with oath is respected and accepted, because the absence of authorization (i.e., being mahjur) is the original state, and he is clinging to the original. However, if the creditors establish evidence against him that the master authorized him, then the slave will be sold, because the slave's claim of authorization is like a claim of emancipation or kitabah; upon the master's denial, his statement is not accepted without evidence.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the validity of transactions made by a person who enters a city claiming to be a slave and engages in trade. The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and addresses two scenarios. First, if the slave explicitly states that he has been authorized by his master to trade, his statement is accepted by istihsan, even if he is not a just witness, contrary to qiyas which would require proof. Second, if he remains silent about authorization, his transactions are still considered valid because the default assumption is that he is acting properly, and transactions should not be hindered. The text then addresses the sale of the slave for debt: he cannot be sold until the master appears because his claim regarding his own person (raqabah) is not accepted, as that is the master's exclusive right. If the master confirms authorization, the slave is sold for debt. If the master denies, his statement is accepted because the original state is lack of authorization. Only if creditors provide evidence is the slave sold. The passage demonstrates the application of istihsan to override strict qiyas in commercial transactions to prevent hardship.

Key Terms
اذن (idhn): Permission given by the master to the slave to engage in trade.
محجور (mahjur): One under interdiction; here, a slave who is not permitted to trade.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas; here used to accept the slave's claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept the slave's trade as valid even if he does not inform of permission?
A) Because qiyas requires it
B) Because acting on apparent circumstances avoids hardship and is the foundation of transactions
C) Because the slave is always authorized
D) Because the master's permission is presumed

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a slave's trade by istihsan, overriding qiyas, to maintain ease in commercial dealings.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Sale of the Slave for Debt: When the Master Confirms or Denies Authorization
Procedural ruling on selling the slave for debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a slave can be sold for debt according to Hanafi law, based on the master's statement or evidence.

Core Idea
A slave is not sold for debt until the master appears; his confirmation or denial of authorization decides the outcome.

After establishing that a slave's trade is valid, the text addresses enforcement: can the slave be sold for his debts? The ruling introduces procedural rules: the slave's word about his own person (raqabah) is not accepted, so he cannot be sold until the master appears. Then the master's statement is key: if he confirms authorization, the slave is sold for debt; if he denies, his word is accepted unless creditors provide evidence. This lesson focuses on these evidentiary rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second issue
After the validity of the slave's trade, the text discusses sale of the slave for debt. Understand the distinction between debt liability and sale of the person.
The slave's earnings (kash) are his right, but the person (raqabah) belongs to the master.
2
Analyze the rule: slave not sold until master appears
The slave's claim about being authorized or having debts is not accepted regarding the sale of his person because that is the master's exclusive right.
The text: 'لا يقبل قوله في الرقبة'.
3
Master's confirmation: slave sold for debt
If the master appears and says the slave was ma'dhun (authorized), the slave is sold for the debt because the debt becomes apparent in the master's right.
The master's confirmation makes the debt binding on the master's property (the slave).
4
Master's denial: his statement accepted
If the master says the slave was mahjur (interdicted), his statement is accepted because the original state is lack of authorization.
The master is 'clinging to the original' (mutamassik bi al-asl).
5
Exception: creditors can provide evidence
Even if the master denies, if the creditors bring evidence that the slave was authorized, the slave is sold.
Evidence overcomes the master's denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص نے شہر میں آ کر کہا کہ میں فلاں کا غلام ہوں پھر اس نے خرید وفروخت کی ہر وہ چیز جو از قسم تجارت ہے لازم ہوگی
قال ومن قدم مصرا فقال انا عبد لفلان فاشترى و باع لزمه كل شيء من التجارة لانه ان اخبر بالاذن فالاخبار دليل عليه وان لم يخبر فتصرفه جائز اذ الظاهر ان المحجور على موجب حجره والعمل بالظاهر هو الاصل في المعاملات كيلا يضيق الأمر على الناس الا انه لا يباع حتى يحضر مولاه لانه لا يقبل قوله في الرقبة لانها خالص حق المولى بخلاف الكسب لانه حق العبد على العبد ما بيناه فان حضر وقال هو ماذون بيع في الدين لانه ظهر الدين فى حق المولى وان قال هو محجور فالقول قوله لانه متمسک بالاصل
ترجمہ: ایک شخص نے شہر میں اگر کہا کہ میں فلاں کا غلام ہوں پھر اس نے خرید و فروخت کی تو اس پر لازم ہوگی ہر وہ چیز جواز قسم تجارت ہو اس لئے کہ اگر اس نے اجازت پانے کی خبر دی ہے تو اس کا خبر دینا خود اس پر دلیل ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے یہ خبر نہیں دی تو اس کا تصرف جائز ہے کیونکہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ مجو شخص اپنے حجر کے مطابق ہی عمل کریگا۔ اور ظاہر پر عمل کرنا ہی اصل ہے معاملات میں تا کہ لوگوں پر معاملہ تنگ نہ ہو جائے ۔لیکن غلام فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ یہاں تک کہ اس کا آقا حاضر ہو کیونکہ اپنے رقبہ کی بابت اس کا قول قبول نہیں کیا جا سکتا اس لئے کہ رتبہ تو خالص آقا کا حق ہے بخلاف کمائی کے کہ وہ غلام کا حق ہے۔ پس اگر آقا آ کر کہے کہ وہ ماذون ہے تو قرضہ میں فروخت کر دیا جائے گا کیونکہ یہ قرضہ آقا کے حق میں بھی ظاہر ہو گیا اور اگر آقا نے کہا کہ وہ مجور ہے تو اس کا قول مقبول ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ وہ اصل سے حجت پکڑ رہا ہے۔

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the validity of transactions made by a person who enters a city claiming to be a slave and engages in trade. The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and addresses two scenarios. First, if the slave explicitly states that he has been authorized by his master to trade, his statement is accepted by istihsan, even if he is not a just witness, contrary to qiyas which would require proof. Key Terms
رقبة (raqabah): The person/slave's body, which is the property of the master.
كسب (kash): Earnings acquired by the slave through his own labor or trade.
التمسك بالأصل (al-tamassuk bi al-asl): Clinging to the original state or presumption; here, the presumption that the slave is interdicted unless proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The slave's person is the master's right; sale for debt requires the master's confirmation or creditors' evidence.

Minor Permitted to Trade: Analogy to the Authorized Slave
Ruling with proof by qiyas to authorized slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply qiyas by analogy to the authorized slave to determine the validity of a minor's trade, and state the condition that the minor must understand trade.

Core Idea
A minor permitted by his guardian trades like an authorized slave — valid if he understands.

This passage extends the ruling of the authorized slave (al-'abd al-ma'dhun) to a minor who has been given permission to trade by his guardian. Building on the earlier discussion of the slave's capacity, the author now addresses a common scenario: a minor who understands buying and selling. This is a clear application of qiyas jaliy (manifest analogy) where the common cause (illa) is the permission from the person in authority combined with the ability to transact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the hukm: the minor's transactions are valid and binding (sahih).
This is with the condition stated next.
2
Identify the proof structure
The ruling is derived by qiyas to the authorized slave. Note the illa: permission + understanding.
Qiyas jaliy is used here.
3
State the condition
The minor must understand buying and selling (ya'qilu al-bay' wa al-shira').
Without understanding, the transaction is not effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل واذا اذن ولى الصبى للصبى فى التجارة فهو فى البيع والشراء كالعبد الماذون اذا كان يعقل البيع والشراء حتى ينفذ تصرفه
ترجمہ فصل کا اگر بچہ کے ولی نے بچہ کو تجارت کی اجازت دی تو وہ خرید و فروخت میں عبد ماذون کے مثل ہے جب کہ خرید و
ولما فرغ من بيان احكام اذن العبد في التجارة شرع في بيان احكام اذن الصبى و المعتوه و قدم الاول لكثر قوعه ۱۲ نتائج -
Section: If the guardian of a minor permits the minor to engage in trade, then in buying and selling he is like the authorized slave, if he understands buying and selling, so that his transaction is effective. Translation of the section: If the guardian of a minor permits the minor to trade, then he is like the authorized slave in buying and selling, provided he understands buying and selling. And when he finished explaining the rulings of permission of a slave in trade, he began explaining the rulings of permission for a minor and an insane person, and he presented the first due to its frequent occurrence. 12 outcomes.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This section establishes that a minor who has been given permission to trade by his guardian is treated like the authorized slave (al-'abd al-ma'dhun) in all matters of buying and selling, as long as the minor understands the nature of trade (ya'qilu al-bay' wa al-shira'). This analogy (qiyas) is based on the common element of permission from the person in authority (guardian/master) and the capacity to transact. The commentary notes that the author chose to treat the slave's case first because it occurs more frequently. The minor's transactions are thus effective and binding.

Key Terms
ولی (wali): Guardian of a minor, usually father or grandfather.
عبذ ماذون (abd ma'dhun): Slave given permission to trade.
يعقل البيع والشراء (ya'qilu al-bay' wa al-shira'): He understands buying and selling; i.e., the minor comprehends the nature and consequences of trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is a minor's trade valid when permitted by the guardian?
A) The minor must be an orphan
B) The minor must understand buying and selling
C) The guardian must be present
D) The minor must be a male

Key Takeaway
A minor's trade is valid if his guardian permits him and he understands trade.

intermediate

Proof of a Slave's Permission to Trade by Istihsan
Explains istihsan basis for slave's permission and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school establishes a slave's permission to trade by istihsan and distinguish the ruling from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides analogy: a slave's permission to trade is presumed due to general need and practice.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses how a slave's permission to trade (idhn) is established. The Hanafi school relies on istihsan (juristic preference) rather than strict analogy (qiyas), because requiring proof at every transaction would cause hardship. The ruling is based on the hadith 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i' and the consensus of Muslims. The passage also clarifies liability: the slave's debts attach to his earnings and he can be sold for them only in the presence of his master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is whether a slave who acts as a merchant is presumed to have his master's permission to trade. The Hanafi school says yes, based on istihsan.
2
Examine the proof for istihsan
The proof is the hadith 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i' (the burden of proof is on the claimant), combined with widespread practice and ijma' (consensus). These justify departing from qiyas.
3
Understand the Hanafi vs Shafi'i difference
Imam al-Shafi'i in one view holds that silence does not establish permission because it is ambiguous. The Hanafi response is that acts of Muslims are interpreted in the best manner to avoid hardship.
4
Apply the ruling to liability
If the slave incurs debts from trading, they are binding on him. He cannot be sold for those debts unless his master is present. The master's statement about permission is accepted with an oath unless creditors prove otherwise.

والا صل في ذلك ان ماضاق على الناس امرها تسع حكمه وما عمت بلیته سقطت قضیه مسئلہ کی دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ وہ صرف خرید و فروخت کرے اور کسی چیز کی خبر نہ دے یعنی یہ نہ بتائے کہ آقا نے مجھے ماذون کیا ہے اس صورت میں مقتضائے قیاس تو یہی ہے کہ اجازت ثابت نہ ہو ۔ چنانچہ ایک وجہ میں امام شافعی اسی کے قائل ہیں کیونکہ سکوت میں احتمال ہے لیکن استحسانا اجازت ثابت ہو جائے گی کیونکہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ وہ ماذون ہے اس لئے کہ امور مسلمین جہاں تک ممکن ہو صلاح پر محمول کرنا ضروری ہے اور اس کے تصرف کا جواز بلا اجازت ثابت نہیں ہو سکتا تو اس کو اجازت پر ہی محمول کیا جائے گا اور ظاہر حال پر عمل کرنا یہی معاملات میں اصل ہے تاکہ لوگوں پر کام میں تنگی نہ ہو جائے ۔
حاصل یہ کہ اگر اس نے اجازت پانے کی خبر دی تو اس کا خبر دینا خود اس پر دلیل ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے ماذون ہونے کی خبر نہیں دی تب بھی اس کا تصرف جائز ہے بہر حال وہ ماذون سمجھا جائے اور تجارتی معاملات میں جو قرضہ عائد ہو وہ اس پر لازم ہوگا۔ قوله الاانه لا يباع الخ - قول سابق الزمه كل شی سے استثناء ہے مطلب یہ ہے کہ غلام مذکور پر جو قرضہ لازم ہو اس میں اس کو فروخت نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ جب تک کہ اس کا آقا حاضر نہ ہو کیونکہ اپنے رقبہ کے بارے میں اس کا قول مقبول نہیں اس لئے کہ وہ تو خالص آقا کا حق ہے جیسا کہ سابق میں ص ۳۰۸ پر گذر چکا۔ حيث قال ويتعلق دينه بكسبه الى ان قال لان المولى انما يخلفه فى الملك بعد فراغه عن حاجة العبد. پھر اگر آقا نے حاضر ہونے کے بعد کہا کہ یہ ماذون ہے تو وہ قرضہ میں فروخت کر دیا جائے گا۔ (اگر آقا اس کا قرضہ نہ چکائے ) کیونکہ آقا کے یہ کہنے سے کہ وہ ماذون ہے یہ قرضہ آقا کے حق میں بھی ظاہر ہو گیا۔ اور اگر آقا نے یہ کہا کہ وہ مجبور ہے تو قسم کے ساتھ اس کا قول معتبر و مقبول ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عدم اذن یعنی مجور ہونا اصل ہے اور وہ اصل ہی کے ساتھ تمسک کر رہا ہے ہاں اگر غر ماء نے اس پر بلینہ قائم کر دیا کہ آقا نے اس کو اجازت دی ہے تو پھر غلام کو فروخت کر دیا جائے گا کیونکہ غلام کا اس پر اجازت کا دعوی کرنا ایسا ہی ہے جیسے دعوی عشق و کتابت تو آقا کے انکار کے وقت بینہ کے بغیر اس کا قول مقبول نہ ہو گا۔
Page 325. Book of the Permitted Slave. Ashraf al-Hidaya, Urdu commentary of Hidaya, Volume 12. [Based on] the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant.' The basis of istihsan is that people act upon this, and the consensus of the Muslims is a proof by which even an athar (tradition) can be specified and analogy can be abandoned. Moreover, there is general need and common occurrence because for the validity of his transaction, the existence of permission is necessary, and establishing proof at every contract is impossible. Al-asl fi dhalika: anma daqa 'ala al-nas amruha tusi' hukmahu wa ma 'ammat baliyyatuhu saqatat qadiyyatahu. The second aspect of the issue is that he merely buys and sells and does not inform of anything, i.e., he does not say that his master has permitted him. In this case, the requirement of analogy is that permission is not established. Thus, in one aspect, Imam al-Shafi'i holds this view because silence is ambiguous. But, by istihsan, permission will be established because the apparent state is that he is permitted. Indeed, the affairs of Muslims are to be interpreted in the best possible manner, and the validity of his transaction cannot be established without permission. So it will be interpreted as permission, and acting upon the apparent state is the principle in transactions to avoid hardship for people. The conclusion: if he informs of having obtained permission, his informing itself is evidence for it. And if he does not inform of being permitted, even then his transaction is valid; he is considered permitted in any case, and any debt incurred in commercial transactions will be binding upon him. His saying: 'but he will not be sold...' - the previous statement 'everything that binds him' is an exception. The meaning is that the mentioned slave will not be sold for a debt that is binding upon him until his master is present, because his statement regarding his own person is not acceptable, as it is solely the master's right, as previously mentioned on page 308. Where he said: 'And his debt is attached to his earnings... until he said: because the master only succeeds him in ownership after his need is fulfilled.' Then if the master, after being present, says that he is permitted, then he will be sold for the debt (if the master does not pay his debt), because by the master's saying that he is permitted, the debt becomes evident against the master. And if the master says that he is compelled (i.e., not permitted), then his statement with an oath is credible and accepted, because the absence of permission (i.e., being compelled) is the original state, and he adheres to the original. However, if the creditors establish proof that the master has given him permission, then the slave will be sold, because the slave's claim of permission is like a claim of having been married or having a kitabah contract; at the time of the master's denial, his statement without proof is not accepted.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade). The commentator explains that the Hanafi school establishes the slave's permission to trade on the basis of istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas (analogy). The key proof is the hadith 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i' (the burden of proof is on the claimant) combined with public practice and consensus (ijma'). The commentator states that since people commonly deal with such slaves and proof is not required at every transaction, the slave's permission is presumed based on his apparent conduct. If the slave does not explicitly announce permission, his transactions are still valid due to istihsan, as his silence is interpreted in the best possible manner. The passage further discusses liability: the slave can be sold to pay his debts only in the presence of his master. If the master confirms permission, the debt is established against the master; if the master denies permission, his statement with an oath is accepted, unless the creditors provide proof of permission. The slave's own claim of permission is not accepted without proof.

Key Terms
ماذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference to abandon analogy (qiyas) for a ruling that better serves public interest.
اجماع مسلمین (ijma' al-muslimin): Consensus of the Muslim community, here cited as a proof for the widespread practice.
البيئة على المدعی (al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i): The burden of proof is on the claimant. A hadith used to place the onus on one who asserts a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the Hanafi ruling that a slave is presumed permitted to trade without explicit declaration?
A) The hadith 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i' requires the master to prove lack of permission.
B) The slave's earnings belong to the master anyway, so permission is irrelevant.
C) The master's silence is considered explicit consent.
D) The slave is always permitted by default.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to presume a slave's permission to trade, overriding analogy to avoid hardship, based on hadith, ijma', and public need.

Minor's Trade Permission: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on minor's trade permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a minor's trade after guardian's permission and evaluate the Shafi'i objection with the Hanafi response, identifying the core difference in the cause of interdiction.

Core Idea
A minor's trade is valid after permission because interdiction is due to lack of guidance, not minority itself.

A guardian permits a minor to trade—are the minor's purchases and sales valid? The Hanafi school says yes, if the minor understands trade. The Shafi'i school disagrees, arguing the minor's incapacity remains. This lesson unpacks the debated cause of interdiction (hajr) and the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf). You will see how each school's underlying principle leads to a different ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the Hanafi position: with guardian's permission and understanding, the minor's transactions are effective (nāfidh).
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
State the Shafi'i counter-position: transactions are not effective because the minor remains under interdiction.
Also attributed to Malik and Ahmad in one narration.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
Analyze their analogical reasoning: hajr continues due to minority; the minor is under a guardian like a slave; buying and selling are not necessities.
They compare trade to divorce/manumission (pure harm) and distinguish from worship/will (necessity).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi counter-argument: the cause (sabab) of hajr is lack of guidance, not minority itself; guardian's permission establishes guidance, so the cause is removed.
Trade is between benefit and harm, unlike pure harm, so the minor can be eligible after permission.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Explain the practical difference: under Hanafi, minor's trades are immediately valid; under Shafi'i, they are suspended until guardian approves.
The Hanafi view facilitates commerce and necessity.

Section: And if the guardian of a minor gives permission to the minor for trade, then he is like the permitted slave in buying and selling if he understands buying and selling, so that his transaction is effective. Translation of the section: If the guardian of a minor gives permission to the minor for trade, then he is like the permitted slave in buying and selling, provided he understands buying and selling, and his transaction will be effective. Explanation of his saying 'and if...' etc.: If the guardian of a minor gives permission to the minor for trade, then that minor is like a permitted slave in buying and selling, provided he understands buying and selling. (The purchase is a means of acquiring ownership and the sale is...) and his transaction will be effective. In one narration, Imam Ahmad and some followers of Imam al-Shafi'i also hold this view. Imam al-Shafi'i's perspective: Al-Shafi'i said: It is not effective because his interdiction is due to his minority, so it remains as long as minority remains, and because he is under a guardian; the guardian has the authority to transact on his behalf and has his interdiction, so he cannot be a guardian [of himself] due to contradiction. Thus it becomes like divorce and manumission, unlike fasting and prayer because they are not performed by the guardian, and similarly a will according to his principle, so necessity for its execution from him is realized. As for buying and selling, the guardian undertakes it, so there is no necessity here. Our [Hanafi] proof: The legitimate transaction originated from its proper subject in its proper place by a legal guardianship, so it must be executed, as established in the disputations. And childhood is a cause for interdiction due to lack of guidance, not due to its essence; and guidance has been established by considering the guardian's permission, and the continuation of his guardianship is for the benefit of the minor, for the fulfillment of interest in two ways and the possibility of change of circumstance. Unlike divorce and manumission, they are pure harm, so he is not eligible for them. And pure benefit like accepting a gift or charity: he is eligible for it even before permission. And buying and selling lie between benefit and harm, so he is considered eligible after permission, not before. But before permission, they are suspended upon the guardian's approval due to the possibility of being in the minor's interest and the transaction's validity in itself. Translation: Imam al-Shafi'i says: It will not be effective because his interdiction is due to his minority, so as long as minority remains, interdiction remains; and because the minor is under a guardian, the guardian has authority over him and has his interdiction, so the minor cannot be a guardian [for himself] due to contradiction. Thus, it becomes like divorce and manumission, unlike fasting and prayer because they are not established by a guardian, and similarly a will according to their principle; therefore necessity for its execution from the minor is realized. As for buying and selling, the guardian undertakes it, so there is no necessity. Our proof: The legitimate transaction originated from its proper subject in its proper place by legal guardianship, so it must be executed, as established in the disputations. And childhood is a cause for interdiction due to lack of guidance, not due to its essence, and guidance has been established by considering the guardian's permission; and the continuation of the guardian's authority is for the minor's benefit, for the fulfillment of interest in two ways and the possibility of change of circumstance. Unlike divorce and manumission, which are pure harm, so he is not made eligible for them. And pure benefit like accepting a gift or charity: he is considered eligible even before permission. And buying and selling are between benefit and harm, so he is made eligible after permission, not before. But before permission, they are suspended upon the guardian's approval due to the possibility of being in his interest and the validity of the transaction in itself. Explanation of his saying 'and al-Shafi'i said...' — According to Imam al-Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and in another narration Imam Ahmad, his transaction will not be effective because his interdiction is due to his minority, so as long as minority remains, interdiction remains (and the continuation of the cause necessitates the effect).
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the validity of a minor's transactions after his guardian grants him permission to trade. The Hanafi position, stated first, is that the minor is like a ma'dhun slave: if he understands buying and selling, his transactions are effective (nāfidh). The Shafi'i view (also attributed to Malik and Ahmad in one narration) is that the transactions are not effective because the minor's interdiction (hajr) continues as long as he is a minor; he is under a guardian and cannot act for himself, analogizing it to divorce and manumission which are purely harmful. The Hanafi response is that the cause of interdiction is not minority itself but lack of guidance (hidāyah); permission from the guardian establishes guidance, so the minor becomes eligible. The Hanafi school distinguishes acts of pure harm (like divorce) from those that can be beneficial or harmful (like trade); the latter are permitted after the guardian's permission. Pure benefit (like accepting a gift) is allowed even without permission. Thus, the minor's trade is valid after permission, ensuring practical necessity.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Interdiction; legal restriction on a person's capacity to act due to minority or other cause.
ولاية (wilayah): Guardianship; legal authority over a minor or dependent.
نفع محض (naf' mahd): Pure benefit; acts that are entirely beneficial, such as accepting a gift or charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effective cause (sabab) of interdiction (hajr) for a minor?
A) Minority itself
B) Lack of guidance (hidāyah)
C) Guardian's absence
D) Financial incapacity

Key Takeaway
A minor's trade after guardian's permission is valid and effective because the cause of interdiction—lack of guidance—is removed, not because minority ends.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Dispute on Child's Trade with Guardian's Permission
Dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i on child's trade validity after guardian's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a child's trade with guardian's permission, explain the Shafi'i objection, and apply the Hanafi response using the three-category classification of dispositions.

Core Idea
A child's trade is valid with permission because the cause of restriction is lack of guidance, not minority itself.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the legal capacity of a minor who has been authorized by his guardian to trade. The Hanafis argue that the child’s transactions are valid and enforceable, while Imam al-Shafi'i holds them ineffective. Understanding this dispute requires analyzing the nature of legal restriction (hajr), the role of guardianship (wilayah), and the categorization of dispositions along a spectrum of benefit and harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling and its basis
The passage first states that if a guardian permits a discerning child to trade, the child is like a permitted slave; his sale and purchase are valid and enforceable. The basis is that the child has been given guidance (hidayah) through permission, removing the cause of restriction.
The child must understand buying and selling.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the child's legal restriction (hajr) is due to minority itself, which persists as long as minority continues. Moreover, the child is under guardianship and cannot act as his own guardian; thus his dispositions are like divorce or manumission by a minor, which are invalid. Sale can be done by the guardian, so no necessity for the child.
Shafi'i distinguishes worship and bequest as acts that cannot be performed by the guardian.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response refutes that the cause of hajr is not minority per se but lack of guidance; permission establishes guidance. The guardian's authority remains for additional benefit and caution. The analogy to divorce/manumission fails because those are pure harm, while trade is mixed benefit and harm. The Hanafis categorize dispositions into pure harm, pure benefit, and mixed, and only mixed becomes valid with permission.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In Hanafi practice, a child authorized by his guardian can buy and sell, and the transactions are binding once executed. In Shafi'i practice, such transactions are void unless ratified by the guardian, or considered null.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred position)
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) because it considers the permission as removing the cause of restriction and allows the child to engage in beneficial trade, supported by istihsan.

Book of the Permitted [Slave]. 326. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary of Hidaya Volume 12. He understands sale, so his disposition will be effective. Explanation of his saying 'and when he permits etc.': If the guardian of a child gives him permission to trade, then that child in buying and selling is like a permitted slave (abd ma'dhun) provided he understands buying and selling (indeed sale is for ownership... [unclear]) so that his disposition will be effective. In one narration, Imam Ahmad and some companions of Imam al-Shafi'i also hold this view. Imam al-Shafi'i's viewpoint: He said: 'It is not effective because his restriction (hajr) is due to his minority, so it remains as long as minority remains, and because he is under guardianship: the guardian possesses disposition over him and possesses his restriction, so he cannot be a guardian due to contradiction, thus it becomes like divorce and manumission. Unlike fasting and prayer because they are not performed by the guardian, and similarly bequest according to his principle, so necessity for its execution from him is realized. As for sale and purchase, the guardian undertakes it, so there is no necessity here.' And our [Hanafi] proof: The legislated disposition has emanated from its proper source in its proper place with legal authority, so its execution is obligatory as known from the books of disagreements. Minority is a cause for restriction due to lack of guidance, not inherently, and guidance has been established in consideration of the guardian's permission. And the guardian's authority remains for the child's benefit to fulfill the maslaha through two ways and possibility of change of condition. Unlike divorce and manumission because they are pure harm, so he is not qualified for them. And pure benefit like accepting a gift or charity, he is qualified before permission. Sale and purchase are between benefit and harm, so he is made qualified after permission, not before. But before permission, it is suspended (mawquf) from him upon the guardian's ratification due to possibility of it being beneficial and the validity of the disposition itself. Translation: Imam al-Shafi'i says it will be effective? (Actually commentary says he says not effective). [Then Urdu summary of both positions and Hanafi response.] Explanation of his saying 'and when he permits...': If the child's guardian grants him permission to trade, then that child in buying and selling is like a permitted slave, provided he understands buying and selling... so that his disposition will be effective. (In one narration Imam Ahmad and some Shafi'i companions also hold this view.) Imam al-Shafi'i's viewpoint: He said: 'It is not effective...' [as above]. Then Hanafi proof: The legislated disposition... [as above]. Then Urdu commentary: Imam al-Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and in another narration Imam Ahmad hold that his disposition is not effective... because his restriction is due to his minority... and because the child is under guardianship... so it is like divorce and manumission... Unlike fasting and prayer because they are not performed by the guardian... Similarly bequest according to his principle... As for sale and purchase, the guardian is the one who handles it, so there is no necessity. Our proof: The child's sale and purchase is a legislated disposition that has emanated with legal authority from someone capable (he is sane and discerning) and in its proper place because the property is valuable, so its execution is necessary. Explanation of his saying 'minority is a cause for restriction...': This is a response to al-Shafi'i's statement: minority as a cause for restriction is not inherent but because the child lacks the method of disposition. But the guardian's permission indicates that he has acquired it, otherwise the guardian would not permit. So when the child's opinion is joined with the guardian's, the child and the seller become equal? Then the benefit side is given preference over the harm side. Explanation of his saying 'and the guardian's authority remains...': This is a response to a question: When the child has obtained guidance of disposition through the guardian's permission, why does the guardian's authority remain? Answer: The guardian's authority remains so that the child's interests are fulfilled in two ways: one by the child's own guidance, and second by the guardian's oversight, and also due to possibility of change in condition. Explanation of his saying 'unlike divorce...': This is a response to al-Shafi'i's statement 'it becomes like divorce and manumission'. The explanation: The child is deserving of consideration and protection from harm. Dispositions are of three types: (1) pure harm like divorce, manumission, gift, charity – the child is not entitled to these even if the guardian permits; (2) pure benefit like accepting a gift or charity – the child is entitled even without permission; (3) between benefit and harm like sale, purchase, lease, marriage – the child is entitled to these with the guardian's permission. And without [permission is not valid? The text cuts off].
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the validity of a minor's commercial transactions after being granted permission (idhn) by his guardian. The Hanafi position, supported by the author of the Hidayah, is that if a discerning child (sabi mumayyiz) is authorized by his guardian to trade, his sales and purchases are valid and enforceable, provided he understands the nature of trade. This is likened to a permitted slave (abd ma'dhun). The passage first presents the ruling and notes that Imam Ahmad and some Shafi'i scholars also agree in one narration. Then the Shafi'i position is given: Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the child's legal restriction (hajr) is due to minority, and as long as minority remains, the restriction continues. Moreover, the child is under guardianship; the guardian has authority over him and can restrict him, so the child cannot act as a guardian for himself due to contradiction. Therefore, any disposition by the child is like divorce or manumission by a minor, which are invalid. Shafi'i distinguishes pure worship (salat, sawm) and bequest (wasiyyah) because these cannot be performed by a guardian, so necessity requires their validity from the child. However, sale and purchase can be done by the guardian, so no necessity arises for the child to do them. The Hanafi response follows, structured as a refutation: The child's transaction is a legislated disposition emanating from a qualified person (the child is sane and discerning) in its proper place (valuable property) with legal authority (the guardian's permission), so it must be enforceable. The cause of restriction (hajr) is minority, but the cause is not the child's essence; it is the lack of guidance (tariq al-tasarruf). With the guardian's permission, guidance is established, so the cause is removed. The guardian's authority remains for the child's benefit: to secure maslaha through both the child's own judgment and the guardian's oversight, and to account for possible changes in the child's condition. The Hanafi then refutes the analogy to divorce and manumission by categorizing dispositions: (1) pure harm (divorce, manumission, giving gifts) – the child is not qualified even with permission; (2) pure benefit (accepting gifts) – the child is qualified even without permission; (3) mixed benefit and harm (sale, purchase, lease, marriage) – the child becomes qualified with permission. Before permission, such transactions are suspended (mawquf) pending the guardian's ratification, because they might be beneficial. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing this third category.

Key Terms
طفل مأذون (tifl ma'dhun): A minor child who has been granted permission by his guardian to engage in trade.
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction preventing a person from disposing of property, applied to minors, insane persons, etc.
ولاية (wilayah): Legal authority over another person, as a guardian has over a minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a child's sale valid after guardian's permission, while divorce by a child is not valid even with permission?
A) Because sale is beneficial, while divorce is harmful.
B) Because the child is older than the divorce age.
C) Because the guardian must be present during sale.
D) Because sale is a form of worship.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a discerning child's trade with guardian's permission is valid because permission removes the cause of restriction (lack of guidance) and trade is a mixed-benefit disposition.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
15 min
Hanafi Response to Shafi'i: Validity of an Authorized Minor's Transactions
Hidayah commentary refuting Shafi'i arguments on minor's transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits an authorized minor to transact, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument by distinguishing purely harmful, purely beneficial, and mixed transactions.

Core Idea
A minor's transaction is valid with guardian's permission because restriction is based on lack of skill, not inherent incapacity.

In this passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, the commentator refutes Imam al-Shafi'i's argument that a minor cannot validly buy or sell even with guardian permission. The Hanafi school distinguishes between types of transactions, arguing that minority alone does not inherently restrict capacity. Understanding this dispute clarifies a fundamental Hanafi principle: authorization removes the restriction of guardianship, allowing the minor to act with his own capacity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: an authorized minor (ma'dhun) can validly engage in mixed transactions (sale, purchase, lease, marriage) with guardian's permission. The transaction is valid but suspended (mawquf) until the guardian approves.
The child must be discerning (aqil mumayyiz) and understand that sale removes ownership and brings profit.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a minor is under guardianship (mahjur) due to minority itself, so no transaction can be valid. He analogizes to divorce and emancipation, which are invalid even with permission.
Shafi'i view: the cause (sabab) of restriction is minority inherently, not lack of knowledge.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the shafi'i proof
The Hanafi response argues that restriction is due to the child's lack of transactional knowledge (tasarruf ka dhang), not minority itself. The guardian's permission proves the child has acquired that knowledge. Also, transactions are divisible into three types.
The three types: purely harmful (divorce, emancipation, gift – invalid even with permission), purely beneficial (accepting gifts – valid without permission), and mixed (sale, purchase – valid only with permission).
4
Explain the continuation of guardian's authority
A question: if the child is now knowledgeable, why does the guardian's authority remain? Answer: for dual oversight – the child's own guidance and the guardian's supervision, and to handle changing circumstances.
Guardianship remains to protect the child's interests from all angles.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi fiqh, an authorized minor's mixed transactions are valid upon guardian approval; in Shafi'i, they are void ab initio. This affects daily commerce, estate management, and business partnerships involving minors.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and facilitates economic activity for minors with guardian oversight.

تشریح قوله صاحب الشرط الخ - شرط شرطہ کی جمع ہے اور شرط بمعنی علامت سے ہے۔ منہ اشراط الساعۃ الی علا ما تھا ، پھر شرطہ فوج کے اس پہلے دستہ کو کہتے ہیں جو میدان جنگ میں آئے۔ بقول اصمعی وجہ تسمیہ یہ ہے کہ وہ ایسی علامت لگاتے ہیں جس سے وہ پہچانے جاتے ہیں یہاں اس سے مراد امیر شہر ہے جیسے امیر بخارا، امیر سمرقند وغیرہ۔ ان کو قاضی مقرر کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا۔ بخلاف والی کے کہ اس کو یہ اختیار ہوتا ہے فكان الوالى اكبر منه قوله والشرط ان يعقل .. الخ - یعنی طفل کے ماذون ہونے میں یہ شرط ہے کہ وہ یہ سمجھتا ہو کہ فر وخت کرنے سے ملک زائل اور منفعت حاصل ہوتی ہے اور " وهو في البيع والشراء كالعبد الماذون " میں طفل مازون کو جو عبد مازون کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی ہے اس کا فائدہ یہ ہے کہ جو احکام عبد مازون کے حق میں ثابت ہوئے وہ طفل مازون کے حق میں بھی ثابت ہوں گے۔
Book of the Authorized [Child]. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary of Hidayah, Volume 12. Also because the child himself is under guardianship, meaning another person is appointed as guardian over him, to the extent that even after the guardian's permission, he has authority over his property and can also appoint him as an agent? So the child himself cannot be a guardian. Because being a guardian and being under guardianship are contradictory, since being under guardianship is a sign of incapacity and being a guardian is a sign of power. So this becomes like divorce and emancipation: the child's divorce and freeing are not valid even if the guardian permits. Similarly, permission for trade would not be valid. Contrary to fasting and prayer, which are valid from the child because their performance does not come through a guardian. Similarly, according to Imam Shafi'i's principle, a bequest is valid because their principle is that any transaction that is carried out through a guardian is not valid by the child's direct action, and a transaction that is not dependent on a guardian is valid by the child's direct action. Thus, the need for execution of the bequest from the child's side is established. As for buying and selling, the guardian is the custodian of that, so there is no need for execution from the child's side. His statement: "And we have argument that the transaction..." - Our argument is that buying and selling from the authorized child is a prescribed transaction that is issued with legal guardianship from a person who is qualified for this task because he is sane and discerning, and this transaction is in its proper place because the property is valuable, so its execution is necessary. His statement: "Minority is a cause of restriction..." - This is a response to Imam Shafi'i's statement that childhood, which is a cause of restriction, is not inherent but because the child does not have the knowledge of transaction. But by the guardian's permission, it becomes known that he has acquired this knowledge, otherwise the guardian would not have permitted him. So when the child's opinion is combined with the guardian's opinion, the child and the seller become equal, and the side of profit takes precedence over the side of loss. His statement: "And the continuation of his guardianship..." - This is a response to a question. The question is: When the child has acquired guidance in transaction by the guardian's permission, why should the guardian's guardianship not remain? The essence of the answer is that the guardian's guardianship remains so that the child's interests are fulfilled in two ways: first, the child himself has guidance; second, the guardian can also supervise, and there is a possibility that circumstances might change. So the guardianship of the guardian remains to achieve that. His statement: "Contrary to divorce..." - This is a response to Imam Shafi'i's statement that it becomes like divorce and emancipation. The explanation is that the child is deserving of consideration and protected from harm, and transactions are of three types: First, purely harmful, like divorce, emancipation, gift, charity - the child does not have ownership of such transactions even if the guardian permits. Second, purely beneficial, like acceptance of gift and charity - he has ownership of these even without the guardian's permission. Third, between profit and loss, like sale, purchase, lease, marriage - the child has ownership of these with the guardian's permission and does not have ownership without permission. So before permission, the child's buying and selling will be suspended, and with the guardian's permission it will become valid because it is possible that it occurred with benefit, along with the validity of the transaction itself. On whom does the term 'guardian' apply? And the mention of 'guardian' in the book includes the father, the grandfather in his absence, the executor, the judge, and the ruler, contrary to the 'sahib al-shart' (city governor) because he does not have the authority to appoint judges. The condition is that he understands that sale removes ownership and brings profit. The analogy with the authorized slave yields that whatever rulings are established for the slave are established for him, because permission releases the restriction, and the authorized person transacts with his own capacity, whether slave or child. So his transaction is not restricted to a specific type, and he becomes authorized by silence, as in the case of the slave. His acknowledgment of what is in his hand from his earnings is valid, and similarly regarding his inherited property in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), as the slave's acknowledgment is valid. He does not have the authority to marry off his slave nor to make a kitabah contract, as in the case of the slave. And the insane person who understands buying and selling is like the child; he becomes authorized with the permission of the father, grandfather, and executor, not others, as we have explained, and his ruling is like that of the child. And Allah knows best. Translation and the word 'wali' in the book includes the father, the grandfather in his absence, the executor, the judge, and the ruler, contrary to the 'sahib al-shart' (city governor) because he does not have the authority to appoint judges. The condition is that he understands that sale removes ownership and brings profit. The analogy of the child with the authorized slave gives the benefit that whatever rulings are established for the authorized slave are also established for the child, because permission removes restriction, and the authorized one transacts with his own capacity, whether slave or child, so his transaction will not be restricted to a specific type, and he becomes authorized by silence, as in the case of the slave. His acknowledgment of what is in his hand from his earnings is valid, and similarly regarding his inherited property in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah). He does not have the authority to marry his slave nor to make a kitabah contract, as in the case of the slave. And the insane person who understands buying and selling is like the child; he becomes authorized with the permission of the father, grandfather, and executor, not others, and his ruling is like that of the child. And Allah knows best. Explanation: His statement: 'sahib al-shart' - shart is the plural of shurtah, and shurtah is from the meaning of sign, from the signs of the Hour etc. Then shurtah is called the first battalion of an army that comes to the battlefield. According to Asma'i, the reason for naming is that they put a sign by which they are recognized. Here it refers to the city governor, like the governor of Bukhara, Samarqand, etc. They do not have the authority to appoint judges. Contrary to the ruler (wali), who has this authority, so the ruler is greater than him. His statement: 'And the condition is that he understands...' - that is, for the child to be authorized, the condition is that he understands that selling removes ownership and brings profit. And in the phrase 'He is in buying and selling like an authorized slave,' the benefit of comparing the authorized child with the authorized slave is that whatever rulings are established for the authorized slave are also established for the authorized child.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, an Urdu commentary on the Hidayah, explains the Hanafi position on the transactions of a minor who has been authorized (ma'dhun) by his guardian to trade. The text begins by addressing an objection from Imam Shafi'i, who argues that a minor cannot validly transact because he is under guardianship (mahjur) and lacks capacity, just as he cannot divorce or emancipate. The Hanafi response, attributed to the commentator, distinguishes between different types of transactions. The key argument is that the restriction (hajr) due to minority is not inherent but stems from the child's lack of knowledge of transactional mechanisms. Once the guardian permits, it indicates that the child has acquired the requisite understanding, so the transaction becomes valid. The text then responds to a further objection: if the child has gained guidance, why does the guardian's authority remain? The answer is that the guardian's continued supervision serves the child's interests, especially as circumstances may change. Next, the text categorizes transactions into three types: purely harmful (e.g., divorce, emancipation, gift) which the child cannot do even with permission; purely beneficial (e.g., accepting gifts) which he can do without permission; and mixed (e.g., sale, purchase, lease, marriage) which he can do only with guardian's permission. Import and sale are valid but suspended (mawquf) until the guardian approves. The latter part of the passage defines who qualifies as a 'wali' (guardian) for the purpose of authorization: the father, grandfather (if father absent), executor, judge, and ruler (wali), excluding the city governor (sahib al-shart) who lacks authority to appoint judges. The child is compared to an authorized slave (abd ma'dhun), meaning he can transact in any type of trade, becomes authorized by the guardian's silence, and his acknowledgment (iqrar) of earnings and inherited property is valid in the zahir al-riwayah. However, he cannot marry off his own slave or execute a kitabah contract. Finally, the insane person (ma'tuh) who understands buying and selling is treated like the child, authorized only by father, grandfather, or executor. The passage upholds the Hanafi principle that authorization removes the restriction of guardianship, allowing the minor to act with his own capacity.

Key Terms
ماذون (ma'dhun): A minor (or slave) who has been given permission (idhn) by his guardian to engage in trade, allowing him to transact with his own capacity.
حجر (hajr): Legal restriction imposed on a person due to minority or insanity, preventing them from independently handling their property.
مولی علیہ (mawla 'alayhi): A person under legal guardianship, such as a minor or insane person, for whom a wali (guardian) is appointed.
صاحب الشرط (sahib al-shart): The city governor (amir of a city like Bukhara, Samarqand) who does not have the authority to appoint judges, unlike the wali (ruler).
معتوه (ma'tuh): An insane person who still has some understanding of buying and selling; treated like a minor for authorization purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which type of transaction can an authorized minor (ma'dhun) perform with the guardian's permission?
A) Divorce and emancipation
B) Acceptance of a gift
C) Sale and purchase
D) Executing a will

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits an authorized minor to engage in mixed transactions (sale, purchase, lease, marriage) with guardian approval, because restriction is due to lack of skill, not inherent incapacity – a key principle distinguishing it from the Shafi'i position.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Who Can Authorize a Minor’s Trade? The Guardian and Conditions
Defines who qualifies as guardian and conditions for minor's trade authorization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six guardians who can authorize a minor's trade and explain the condition of the minor's understanding of profit and ownership transfer, along with the key rulings that parallel the authorized slave.

Core Idea
Only specific guardians can authorize a minor’s trade, and the minor must understand buying and selling.

In Hanafi fiqh, a minor’s transactions are invalid unless authorized by a designated guardian. This passage from Al-Hidayah specifies exactly who may grant such permission and the crucial condition that the minor must grasp the economic consequences of trade. Additionally, the text draws a powerful analogy to the authorized slave, extending many of the same rulings to the authorized minor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of guardians
Name the six persons who can authorize a minor: father, grandfather (in his absence), executor (wasī), judge (qādī), and governor (wālī). Note the order of priority.
2
Understand the exclusion
Recognize who is NOT included: the local commander (sāhib al-shart) because he lacks authority to appoint judges.
This shows the high standard for guardianship over a minor's property.
3
Recognize the key condition
The minor must intellectually understand that selling removes ownership and brings profit. Without this understanding, authorization is ineffective.
This condition is separate from the guardian’s identity.
4
Apply the analogy to the authorized slave
Memorize the parallel rulings: valid acknowledgment of earnings/inheritance, no authority to marry off a slave or make kitābah contract, and authorization by silence.
The analogy is a core Hanafi tool for deriving rulings.
5
Note the special case of the insane person (ma‘tūh)
A mentally deficient person who understands trade is treated like a minor: authorized only by father, grandfather, or executor.
This extends the same protections.

تشریح قوله صاحب الشرط الخ - شرط شرطہ کی جمع ہے اور شرط بمعنی علامت سے ہے۔ منہ اشراط الساعۃ الی علا ما تھا ، پھر شرطہ فوج کے اس پہلے دستہ کو کہتے ہیں جو میدان جنگ میں آئے۔ بقول اصمعی وجہ تسمیہ یہ ہے کہ وہ ایسی علامت لگاتے ہیں جس سے وہ پہچانے جاتے ہیں یہاں اس سے مراد امیر شہر ہے جیسے امیر بخارا، امیر سمرقند وغیرہ۔ ان کو قاضی مقرر کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا۔ بخلاف والی کے کہ اس کو یہ اختیار ہوتا ہے فكان الوالى اكبر منه قوله والشرط ان يعقل .. الخ - یعنی طفل کے ماذون ہونے میں یہ شرط ہے کہ وہ یہ سمجھتا ہو کہ فر وخت کرنے سے ملک زائل اور منفعت حاصل ہوتی ہے اور " وهو في البيع والشراء كالعبد الماذون “ میں طفل مازون کو جو عبد مازون کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی ہے اس کا فائدہ یہ ہے کہ جو احکام عبد مازون کے حق میں ثابت ہوئے وہ طفل مازون کے حق میں بھی ثابت ہوں گے۔
He mentioned the guardian in the book: it includes the father, and the grandfather in his absence, the executor, the judge, and the governor, unlike the commander of the garrison because he does not have the authority to appoint judges. The condition is that he understands that selling removes ownership and brings profit. The analogy to the authorized slave indicates that whatever rulings are established for the slave are established for him, because permission removes incapacity. The authorized person acts with his own eligibility, whether slave or minor, so his actions are not restricted to a specific type. He becomes authorized by silence, as in the case of the slave. His acknowledgment regarding what is in his possession from his earnings is valid, and likewise regarding his inherited wealth in the apparent narration, just as the slave's acknowledgment is valid. He does not have the authority to marry off his slave nor to make a kitabah contract, as in the case of the slave. The insane person who understands buying and selling is like a minor: he becomes authorized by the permission of the father, grandfather, and executor, not others, as we have explained, and his ruling is the ruling of the minor. And Allah knows best. Commentary: His saying 'sahib al-shart' etc. - 'shart' is the plural of 'shirta', and 'shart' comes from the meaning of 'sign'. Among its uses is 'ashrat al-sa'ah' (signs of the Hour) meaning its markers. Then 'shirta' refers to the first detachment of the army that enters the battlefield. According to Asma'i, the reason for the name is that they put up a sign by which they are recognized. Here it means the city governor, like the governor of Bukhara, Samarkand, etc. They do not have the authority to appoint judges. Unlike the 'wali' (governor), who has this authority, so the wali is greater than him. His saying: 'the condition is that he understands...' - meaning the condition for the minor being authorized is that he understands that selling removes ownership and brings profit. And in the phrase 'he is in buying and selling like the authorized slave', the benefit of likening the authorized minor to the authorized slave is that whatever rulings are established for the authorized slave are also established for the authorized minor.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that defines who can act as guardian (walī) for a minor to authorize him in trade, along with the conditions and effects. The text states that the term 'walī' in the book (Al-Hidayah) includes the father, then the grandfather in his absence, the executor (wasī), the judge (qādī), and the governor (wālī), but excludes the local commander (sāhib al-shart) because he lacks the authority to appoint judges. The key condition is that the minor must intellectually grasp that buying and selling removes ownership and yields profit. The minor is compared to an authorized slave (abd ma'dhūn); just as a slave's permission lifts his legal incapacity, so too for the minor. Consequently, the minor can act based on his own eligibility, unrestricted to specific types of trade. He can become authorized through silence (sukūt) like the slave. His acknowledgment (iqrār) regarding what he possesses from his earnings or inheritance is valid according to the zāhir al-riwāyah, akin to a slave's acknowledgment. However, he cannot marry off his slave or make a kitābah contract, just like a slave. The insane person (ma'tūh) who understands buying and selling is treated like a minor: he becomes authorized only by the permission of the father, grandfather, or executor, not others, and his ruling mirrors that of the minor. The commentary then elaborates on the term sāhib al-shart, explaining that 'shart' linguistically means a sign or marker, and idiomatically refers to the first vanguard of an army. Here it means the city governor (amīr) who does not have the power to appoint judges, unlike a wālī who does. It also reiterates the condition that the minor must understand the consequences of trade and the benefit of the analogy to the authorized slave.

Key Terms
ولی (walī): In this passage, walī refers to the guardian authorized to grant permission for a minor to engage in trade—specifically the father, grandfather, executor, judge, or governor.
طفل ماذون (tifl ma'dhūn): A minor who has been given permission (idhn) by his guardian to engage in trade, with the condition of understanding the nature of buying and selling.
عبد ماذون (abd ma'dhūn): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade, acting with his own legal capacity.
صاحب الشرط (sāhib al-shart): The local commander or city governor (e.g., amīr of Bukhara) who does not have the authority to appoint judges, and thus cannot grant authorization to a minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Only the father, grandfather, executor, judge, and governor may authorize a minor's trade, and the minor must understand that buying and selling involve transfer of ownership and profit.

Profit from Usurped Goods: Conditions and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains ruling on profit from usurped goods, conditions, and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit from usurped goods, identify the conditions that render the profit impure, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between the Two and Imam Abu Yusuf, including the later mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Profit from usurped property must be given in charity – ownership deficiency (no liability) renders the gain impure.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding profit earned by a usurper who trades with the usurped property. The core question: is the profit lawful (halal) for the usurper, or must it be purified by charity? We will follow the reasoning of the Two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and the later mashayikh, and contrast it with Imam Abu Yusuf's view. The passage also details how the method of payment and contractual indication affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue and the two positions
The issue: if a usurper trades with usurped property and makes a profit, is the profit lawful for him? The Two say it is impure and must be given in charity; Abu Yusuf says it is pure.
The Two refer to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Understand the Two's dalil
The transaction is not in the usurper's ownership because liability (damān) is absent. The property does not belong to him, so any profit attached to it cannot be his.
This is clearer in the case of deposit (wadi'ah) where the trustee also lacks liability.
3
Analyze the distinction between specified and unspecified property
For property specified by indication (ishārah), profit is impure only if the contract indicates the specific usurped goods AND payment is made from those goods. Otherwise, the profit is pure. For unspecified property (like currency), the later mashayikh ruled absolutely that profit is never pure.
Ishārah means contractual reference, e.g., 'with this dirham'.
4
Evaluate the later mashayikh's absolute view and its tarjih
The later scholars (mashayikh) said that regardless of before or after compensation, the profit is never pure. This is the chosen (mukhtār) view because the texts in Jami' al-Saghir, Jami' al-Kabir, and al-Mabsut are absolute.
This view overrides the earlier conditional approach of Imam al-Karkhi.
5
Note the condition for profit to appear
Profit only becomes apparent when the type (jins) is unified – i.e., when the sold item is of the same category as the purchase price, such as selling a slave-girl for dirhams after buying her with dirhams.
If the value remains the same (no surplus), no charity is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الْغَصْبِ جَمِيعًا، لِأَنَّ الرِّبْحَ إِنَّمَا يَتَبَيَّنُ عِنْدَ اتِّحَادِ الْجِنْسِ 
تَرْجَمَة: كسی نے ہزار درہم غصب کر کے ان سے ایک باندی خرید کر دو ہزار میں فروخت کردی پھر 1 ہزار میں ایک باندی خرید کر تین ہزار میں فروخت کی تو وہ پورا نفع صدقہ کر دے۔ یہ طرفین کے نزدیک ہے۔ اور اس کی اصل یہ ہے کہ جب غاصب اور مودع مغصوب یا ودیعت میں تصرف کرلے اور نفع اٹھائے تو طرفین کے نزدیک اس کے لیے نفع پاکیزہ نہیں ہوتا۔ برخلاف امام ابو یوسف کے دلائل سابق میں گزر چکے اور طرفین کا جواب ودیعت کی صورت میں ظاہر تر ہے۔ کیونکہ تصرف سے مال کی طرف ملک مستند نہیں ہوتی، سبب ضمان معدوم ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ پس تصرف اپنی ملک میں نہ ہوا پھر یہ ایسے مال میں تو ظاہر ہے جو اشارہ سے متعین ہو جاتا ہے۔ رہا وہ مال جو متعین نہیں ہوتا۔ جیسے دونوں ثمن سو کتاب میں یہ کہنا اشتری بہا اشارہ ہے اس بات کی طرف کہ صدقہ کرنا اس وقت واجب ہو گا جب ان درہموں کے عوض خریدے اور انہیں سے ثمن ادا کرے اور اگر ان کی طرف اشارہ کیا اور ثمن ان کے علاوہ سے دیا یا ثمن انہیں سے دیا اور اشارہ ان کے علاوہ کی طرف کیا یا عقد مطلق رکھا اور ثمن انہیں سے ادا کیا تو نفع اس کے لیے پاکیزہ ہوگا امام کرخی نے بھی یوں ہی کہا ہے اس لئے کہ اشارہ جب مفید تعین نہیں ہوتا تو نقد سے مؤکد ہونا ضروری ہے تاکہ خبث متحقق ہو اور ہمارے مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ ضمان سے پہلے ہو یا ضمان کے بعد بہر حال نفع پاکیزہ نہیں ہوتا اور یہی مختار ہے جامع صغیر و کبیر اور مبسوط میں جواب مطلق ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ اور اگر ہزار کے عوض ایمی باندی خریدی جو دو ہزار کے برابر ہے پھر وہ کسی کو ہبہ کر دی یا اناج خرید کر کھا لیا تو صدقہ نہ کرے اور یہ ان سب کا قول ہے۔ کیونکہ نفع اسی وقت ظاہر ہوتا ہے جب جنس متحد ہو۔
Book of Ghash: All because profit is only clear when the type is unified. Translation: Someone usurped a thousand dirhams, bought a slave-girl with them, sold her for two thousand, then bought another slave-girl for one thousand and sold her for three thousand; he should give all the profit in charity. This is according to the Two. And the basis of this is that when the usurper or trustee transacts in the usurped or deposited property and takes profit, the profit is not pure for him according to the Two. Contrary to Imam Abu Yusuf; the proofs have passed earlier. And the Two's response is more apparent in the case of deposit because the transaction does not attribute ownership to the property due to the absence of liability. So the transaction was not in his own property. Then this is apparent in property that is specified by indication. As for property that is not specified, like both? In the book, saying 'he bought with it' is an indication that charity becomes obligatory when he buys in exchange for those dirhams and pays the price from them. And if he indicated them but paid the price from other than them, or paid the price from them and indicated other than them, or made the contract absolute and paid the price from them, then the profit will be pure for him. Imam al-Karkhi said the same, because when indication is not specifying, it must be emphasized by payment so that impurity is realized. And our mashayikh said: whether before or after compensation, the profit is never pure, and this is the chosen view because in Jami' al-Saghir, Jami' al-Kabir, and al-Mabsut the ruling of profit being pure is absolute. And if he bought with the thousand a slave-girl worth one thousand, then gifted her to someone or bought grain and ate it, he should not give charity. This is the saying of all of them, because profit only appears when the type is unified.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding profit from usurped or deposited property. The issue: someone usurps a sum of money, uses it to trade, and makes profit – is the profit lawful for him? The Two (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad) say the profit is impure and must be given in charity, while Imam Abu Yusuf holds it is pure for him. The text then provides the legal reasoning: the Two argue that since the usurper does not own the property, any transaction on it is not in his ownership, so profit is impure. This is clear in deposited property because the trustee's liability is absent. The passage then distinguishes between property that is specified by indication (like specific goods) and property that is not specified (like currency). For currency, there is further detail: if the contract indicates the specific usurped coins and payment is made from those coins, the profit is impure; if the contract indicates them but payment comes from other coins, or if the contract is absolute, the profit is pure. Imam al-Karkhi held this view. However, our later scholars (mashayikh) ruled that regardless of the circumstances, the profit is never pure – this is the relied-upon (mukhtar) view because the texts of Jami' al-Saghir, Jami' al-Kabir, and al-Mabsut state it absolutely. Finally, if the usurper buys a slave-girl of equal value and then gifts or consumes her, there is no profit to give in charity because profit only appears when there is a surplus from a sale at a higher price.

Key Terms
طرفین (tarafayn): The Two: Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad b. al-Hasan al-Shaybani.
ضمان (damān): Liability to compensate; here, the usurper's obligation to return the property or its value.
اشارت (ishārah): Indication; referring to specific coins or goods in a contract, e.g., 'with this dirham'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the later Hanafi school, if a usurper trades with usurped money and makes a profit, what should he do?
A) Keep the profit as it is halal
B) Give the entire profit in charity
C) Return only the principal to the owner
D) Spend the profit on himself but avoid extravagance

Key Takeaway
Profit from usurped property is never halal to keep; charity is wajib according to the mufta bihi view.
