Sale vs. Emancipation of a Usurped Slave: Validity and Ownership Deficiency
Explaining the ruling that usurper's sale is valid but emancipation is not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between valid sale and invalid emancipation in cases of deficient ownership and explain the rationale using the analogy of the mukatib.

Core Idea
A usurper's sale of a slave is valid but emancipation is not, because deficient ownership suffices for transfer but not for liberation.

In Hanafi fiqh, the ownership of a usurper who is held liable for the price is considered deficient (nāqiṣ). This deficiency has specific legal consequences: it validates a sale but invalidates an emancipation. The passage you are about to read not only states this rule but also provides the underlying reasoning through a qiyās (analogy) with a mukātib (a slave under contract of manumission). Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how the Hanafi school treats partial ownership and its effects on different transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage states two scenarios: sale and emancipation by the usurper after the owner takes the price. State each ruling clearly: sale valid, emancipation invalid.
2
Locate the rationale
The author explains that the usurper's ownership is deficient (nāqiṣ) because it is established by recourse (istinād) or necessity (ḍarūrah). This deficiency manifests in that the usurper takes earnings but not children.
3
Trace the analogy (qiyās)
The deficient ownership of the usurper is compared to that of a mukātib. Both can sell but cannot emancipate. Identify the effective cause (ʿillah): deficiency of ownership.
4
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
The commentary mentions that Imām al-Shāfiʿī, Mālik, and in one narration Aḥmad hold the sale invalid. Contrast their reasoning (they require full ownership for a valid sale).
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider a case where a usurper sells a female slave, then the owner takes the price, but before compensation she gives birth. Determine who owns the child. Answer: the original owner, because deficient ownership does not extend to offspring.

تشريح: قوله ومن غصب عبدا.. الخ - ایک شخص نے کسی کا غلام غصب کر کے بیچ ڈالا۔ اور مالک نے اس کی قیمت کا تاوان لے لیا تو غاصب کی بیع نافذ ہو جائے گی ایک روایت میں امام احمد بھی اسی کے قائل ہیں۔ امام شافعی، امام مالک اور دوسری روایت میں امام احمد کے نزدیک بیج نافذ نہ ہوگی۔ اور اگر غاصب نے غلام کو آزاد کر دیا پھر مالک نے تاوان لیا تو عتق نافذ نہ ہوگا۔ وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ ملک غاصب ناقص ہوتی ہے کیونکہ اس کا ثبوت بطریق استناد ہوتا ہے اور جو چیز بطریق استناد ثابت ہو۔ وہ من وجہ تابلی من وجہ غیر ثابت ہوتی ہے یا اس کا ثبوت بوجہ ضرورت ہوتا ہے۔ کیونکہ از روئے دلیل غصب کے ذریعہ سے ملک ثابت نہیں ہو سکتی۔ اس لئے کہ غصب عدوان محض ہے اور ملک ایک نعمت ہے غاصب کے لئے جو ملک ثابت ہو جاتی ہے وہ قضاء بالضمان کی ضرورت کی وجہ سے ہوتی ہے۔ تا کہ ملک واحد میں بدل اور مبدل منہ دونوں جمع نہ ہو جائیں اور جو چیز ضرورۃ ثابت ہو، وہ بھی من وجہ ثابت اور من وجہ غیر ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ اسی لئے ثبوت ملک کا ظہور صرف کمائی کے حق میں ہوتا ہے نہ کہ اولاد کے حق میں۔ چنانچہ اگر باندی غصب کی اور غاصب کے اداء ضمان سے پہلے اس نے کچھ کمائی کی۔ تو کمائی غاصب کی ہوگی۔ اور اگر اداء ضمان سے پہلے اولاد ہوئی تو وہ مغصوب منہ کی ہوگی۔ بہر کیف ملک غاصب ناقص ہوتی ہے اور ملک ناقص نفاذ بیچ کے لئے کافی ہوتی ہے نفاذ عشق کے لئے کافی نہیں ہوتی۔ جیسے ملک مکاتب ہے کہ مکاتب اپنی کمائی میں خرید و فروخت کر سکتا ہے لیکن اپنی کمائی کا غلام آزاد نہیں کر سکتا۔
He said: And whoever usurps a slave and sells him, and the owner makes him liable for the price, then his sale is valid. And if he emancipates him, then becomes liable for the price, his emancipation is not valid. Because the ownership established in him is deficient, due to its being established by means of recourse (istinad) or necessity. For this reason, it appears in regard to earnings but not children. And the deficient ownership suffices for the validity of sale but not for emancipation, like the ownership of a mukatib. Translation: Someone usurped a slave and sold him, and the owner took the price as compensation, then the usurper's sale becomes valid. And if he emancipates the slave and becomes liable for the price, his emancipation is not valid. Because the ownership that is established in the slave is deficient due to its being by recourse or necessity. For this reason, it appears in the right of earnings and not in the right of children. And deficient ownership suffices for the enforcement of sale but not for the enforcement of emancipation, like the ownership of a mukatib. Explanation: His statement: 'And whoever usurps a slave...' etc. A person usurped someone's slave and sold him. And the owner took the price as compensation, then the usurper's sale becomes effective. In one narration, Imam Ahmad also holds this view. Imam al-Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and in another narration Imam Ahmad hold the sale is not effective. And if the usurper emancipated the slave then the owner took compensation, the emancipation is not effective. The reason for the difference is that the usurper's ownership is deficient because its proof is by way of recourse (istinad), and what is proved by way of recourse is established from one aspect and not from another, or its proof is by way of necessity. Because, based on proof, ownership cannot be established through usurpation. Because usurpation is pure aggression and ownership is a blessing. The ownership that is established for the usurper is due to the necessity of judicial compensation so that price and the object of price do not combine in one ownership. And what is established by necessity is also established from one aspect and not from another. For this reason, the manifestation of ownership is only in the right of earnings, not in the right of children. Thus, if a female slave is usurped and before the usurper pays compensation she earns something, the earning is the usurper's. And if before paying compensation she gives birth, the child belongs to the one from whom she was usurped. In any case, the usurper's ownership is deficient, and deficient ownership suffices for the enforcement of sale but does not suffice for the enforcement of emancipation. Like the ownership of a mukatib: he can buy and sell with his earnings but cannot free a slave from his earnings.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on the difference between sale and emancipation by a usurper. The matn states: if a usurper sells a slave he wrongfully took, and then the original owner accepts the price as compensation, the sale becomes valid. But if he emancipates the slave and then is held liable for the price, the emancipation is not valid. The reason given is that the usurper's ownership is deficient (nāqiṣ). It is established either through a legal fiction (istinād: the law treats the usurper as owner from the time of sale to validate it) or by necessity (to avoid combining the price and the property in the same ownership). This deficiency manifests in that the usurper takes the slave's earnings but not her children. A deficient ownership suffices to authorize a sale but not an emancipation, analogized to a mukātib (a slave with a contract of manumission) who can sell but cannot free. The commentary (Ashraf Ul Hadaya) explains that Imam Ahmad in one narration agrees, while al-Shafi'i, Malik, and another narration of Ahmad disagree, holding the sale invalid. The key principle: the usurper's ownership is partial, necessary only to give legal effect to the transaction and prevent double recovery.

Key Terms
ملك ناقص (milk nāqiṣ): Deficient ownership that is established by recourse or necessity, sufficient for some legal effects but not others.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability for the value of the usurped property, taken by the owner as compensation.
مستندا (mustanidan): The ownership is established by recourse to a legal fiction, treating the usurper as owner from the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a usurper's sale of a slave valid but his emancipation invalid after the owner takes the price?
A) Because the usurper owns the slave completely after paying the price.
B) Because the usurper's ownership is deficient (nāqiṣ), sufficient for sale but not emancipation.
C) Because emancipation requires the owner's consent, which is absent.
D) Because the slave is still owned by the original owner until court order.

Key Takeaway
A usurper's ownership established through compensation is deficient (nāqiṣ), rendering his sale valid but his emancipation invalid, analogous to the ownership of a mukātib.

Contrasting Rulings: Dhimmis, Wine, Riba, and Slaughter without Tasmiyah
Commentary expanding contrasts to dhimmi default rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi law treats riba differently from wine/pork in dhimmi transactions and evaluate the inter-madhhab dispute over liability for destroying meat without tasmiyah.

Core Idea
Riba is universally prohibited and interferes with dhimmi contracts; meat without tasmiyah is not property.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah expands on three contrasts to the general rule that dhimmis are free to trade in wine and pork. While those are permitted under their covenant, riba is not—because it is forbidden in their own scripture and by the hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ). Additionally, the passage discusses the case of an apostate slave owned by a dhimmi, where we do not refrain from interference. Finally, it addresses a Shafi'i-Hanafi dispute: if a Hanafi destroys an animal slaughtered without tasmiyah, is he liable? The Hanafi position is that no compensation is due because the meat is haram and thus not valid property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline rule for dhimmis
Understand that dhimmis are not prohibited from owning or dealing in wine and pork. This is the default position from their covenant.
The text states 'mazaqqah nahin' / 'la hara'.
2
Distinguish riba from wine/pork
Note that riba is an exception: the hadith 'except the usurer, there is no covenant' and the verse on previous nations show that riba is forbidden even for dhimmis. We are commanded to nullify riba contracts.
The Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'la riba baynana wa baynahum' or similar.
3
Analyze the apostate slave contrast
An apostate slave owned by a dhimmi is not left alone because that would degrade Islam. The covenant does not cover apostasy.
This is another case where we interfere with dhimmi property.
4
Examine the tasmiyah dispute
A Hanafi who intentionally destroys meat from an animal slaughtered without tasmiyah (which Shafi'is consider halal) is not liable for compensation according to Hanafi law. The verse (6:121) makes it haram, hence not property.
The Shafi'i opinion that it is halal does not bind the Hanafi judge in imposing damages.
5
Synthesize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in a Shafi'i court, a Hanafi might be sued for destroying such meat, but a Hanafi judge would not enforce liability. This illustrates the limits of inter-madhhab enforcement.
This is the mukhtalaf fih: the property status of matruk al-tasmiyah.

مضائقہ نہیں ۔ اسلئے کہ ذمی کو مر یا خنزیر کے مالک کرنے یا مالک ہونے سے ممانعت نہیں ہے۔ قوله وهذا بخلاف الربوا.... الخ - يقول سابق لان الذمی غیر ممنوع عن تملیک الخمر و تملکھا سے متعلق ہے۔ مطلب یہ ہے کہ حکم مذکور شراب اور سور وغیرہ میں ہے۔ بخلاف بیاج کے کہ یہ ان کے عہد ذمہ سے مستثنی ہے تو عقو دربا کے ابطال میں ان سے تعرض کیا جائے گا کیونکہ ہم ان کے بارے میں ترک تعرض کے مامور نہیں ہیں۔ و قد قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الا من اربي فليس بيننا و بینه عهد وجہ یہ ہے کہ بیاج لینا ان کا فسق ہے نہ کہ ترین ورنہ حرمت ربوا تو خودان کے دین میں بھی ثابت ہے۔ قال اللہ تعالى واخذهم الربوا وقد نهو عنه اور بخلاف مرتد غلام کے جو کسی ذمی کی ملک میں ہو کہ معاف نہیں کیا جائے گا کیونکہ ذمیوں سے ہمارا معاہدہ مرتد کو چھوڑ دینے کا نہیں ہے کیونکہ ایسا کرنے میں دین کی ہتک ہوتی ہے۔ قوله و بخلاف متروک التسمية .... الخ ۔ اور بخلاف اس کے کہ اگر کوئی حنفی المسلک عمد امتروک التسمیہ کو تلف کر دے ( جو شافعیہ کے نزدیک مباح ہے ) تو اس پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا ۔ اسلئے کہ شافعیہ کے زعم میں گومتروک التسمیہ حلال ہے ۔ لیکن حجت و دلیل سے قائل کرنے کی ولایت حاصل ہے۔ کیونکہ حق تعالیٰ کا ارشاد ولا تاكلوا مما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه و انه لفسق اس بارے میں صریح ہے کہ عد امتروک التسمیہ حرام ہے۔ مال نہیں ۔ پس ایجاب منمان کے بارے میں شافعیہ کا اعتقاد معتبر نہ ہوگا۔
There is no harm. Because the dhimmi is not prohibited from making wine or pork owned or from owning them. Statement: 'And this is contrary to riba... etc.' - it says earlier because the dhimmi is not prevented from giving ownership of wine and owning it. The meaning is that the mentioned ruling is about wine, pork etc. Contrary to riba, because it is excepted from their covenant of dhimma, so we will interfere with them in invalidating riba contracts, because we are not commanded to refrain from interference regarding them. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Except the usurer, there is no covenant between us and him.' The reason is that taking riba is their transgression, not disbelief; rather the prohibition of riba is established in their own religion. Allah said: 'And [for] their taking of usury, although they were forbidden it.' And contrary to the case of an apostate slave who is in the ownership of a dhimmi, because he will not be forgiven, for our covenant with dhimmis does not include leaving apostates, because such an act leads to the degradation of the religion. Statement: 'And contrary to the case of [an animal] omitted from pronouncing the name [of Allah]... etc.' - And contrary to the case that if a Hanafi intentionally destroys an animal from which pronouncing the name [at slaughter] was omitted (which according to the Shafi'is is permissible), then compensation is not obligatory upon him. This is because in the opinion of the Shafi'is, the animal from which the name is omitted is lawful. But the authority to convince by proof and evidence is present. Because the command of Allah Most High: 'And do not eat of that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned, and indeed it is grave disobedience' is explicit in this matter that omitting the name is unlawful. It is not property. Therefore, the belief of the Shafi'is regarding imposing compensation is not considered.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah comments on the Hidayah's statement 'And this is contrary to riba...' The author explains that dhimmis are not prevented from owning or dealing in wine and pork because their covenant (dhimmah) does not prohibit these. However, riba is an exception: the Prophet's hadith 'except the usurer, there is no covenant between us and him' indicates that riba contracts with dhimmis are invalid and we should interfere. Riba is forbidden in their own scriptures (Quran 4:161), so it is not merely a matter of their religion but a universal prohibition. Then another contrast: an apostate slave owned by a dhimmi is not covered by the covenant; we do not leave apostates because it degrades Islam. Finally, a third contrast: the case of an animal slaughtered without tasmiyah (pronouncing Allah's name). The Shafi'is consider such meat halal, but the Hanafi position is that it is haram and thus not valid property. If a Hanafi destroys such an animal, he is not liable for compensation in a Shafi'i court because according to Hanafi law it is not property. The Quranic verse (6:121) clearly prohibits it. The Shafi'i belief is not binding in imposing damages. This illustrates the inter-madhhab dynamic in legal liability.

Key Terms
ذمی (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic state with a covenant (dhimmah).
ربوا (riba): Usury or interest, prohibited in Islam and in previous scriptures.
متروک التسمیة (matruk al-tasmiyah): An animal slaughtered without pronouncing the name of Allah (Bismillah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best explains why riba contracts with dhimmis are subject to interference according to this passage?
A) Because riba involves money, which is always regulated by the state.
B) Because the Prophet’s hadith excludes riba from the dhimmi covenant, and riba was forbidden in their own scriptures.
C) Because dhimmis are not allowed to engage in any financial transactions.
D) Because riba is prohibited only in Islam, so we must enforce it on all subjects.

Key Takeaway
Dhimmis may deal in wine and pork freely, but riba is universally prohibited and we nullify such contracts; meat without tasmiyah is not considered property in Hanafi law, affecting inter-madhhab liability.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
7 min
Liability for Transformed Usurped Property: The Dispute on Vinegar and Tanned Leather
Intra-Hanafi dispute on daman for usurped property after transformation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for tanned leather after usurpation and identify the proof and practical consequence of each position.

Core Idea
Ownership remains after transformation; liability depends on whether the addition of value changes compensation.

This passage explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when a usurper takes wine (which becomes vinegar) or a raw hide (which becomes tanned leather) and then destroys it, is he liable? Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the two, while his two disciples hold both are guaranteed with compensation for the added value. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how property rights persist through transformation and how qiyas operates across different scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Case
The case involves a usurper who takes wine (which turns into vinegar) or a raw hide (which is tanned), then destroys the transformed item before returning it. The issue is whether the usurper is liable and how compensation should be calculated.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa rules that the usurper is liable only for the vinegar, not for the tanned leather. For vinegar, he must return its like (mithl). For leather, tanning is considered a separate permissible act that does not trigger liability for the leather itself.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that both items are guaranteed. The usurper must compensate for the tanned leather as is, but the owner must pay the increase due to tanning. This is analogous to dyed cloth.
4
Analyze the Proofs
The Sahibayn base their view on two analogies: (1) dyed cloth – if someone usurps cloth, dyes it, then destroys it, he is liable for the dyed cloth and the owner pays for the dye increase; (2) borrowed property – since returning was obligatory, destroying it substitutes its value. Abu Hanifa's proof (implied) is that tanning is an independent act that adds value, so the owner must pay more to reclaim the leather.
The passage also clarifies a nuance: the 'increase' is paid only when the compensation species differ; otherwise, it is deducted.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: Under Abu Hanifa, if the usurper destroys tanned leather, he owes nothing for the leather (only for vinegar if applicable). Under the Sahibayn, he owes the full value of tanned leather minus the tanning cost (or a separate payment if species differ).
Later Hanafi fatwa often follows the Sahibayn for fairness.

قوله وقولهما يعطى .. الخ - یعنی صاحبین نے جو یہ فرمایا ہے کہ دباغت سے جو زیادتی ہوئی وہ دے دی جائے یہ اس پر محمول ہے کہ غاصب سے جو تاوان لیا جائے وہ ایک جنس ہو اور جو دیا جائے وہ دوسری جنس ہو بایں طور کہ قاضی نے کھال کی قیمت دراہم سے اور دباغت کی قیمت دنا نیر سے لگائی ۔ حتی کہ جنس مختلف ہو گئی۔ اور اگر جنس متحد ہو تو اس کی ضرورت نہیں بلکہ غاصب کو جو دینا ہے وہ اس کے ذمہ سے کم کر کے باقی لے لے اس لئے کہ اس سے لے کر پھر اس کو واپس کرنے میں کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے۔
Book of Usurpation. The increase due to tanning is in it. If it perishes in his hand, he is not liable by consensus. As for vinegar, because it remained in the ownership of its owner and it is valuable property, he is liable for it by destruction, and its like is obligatory because it is of substances with equivalents. As for the leather, according to them (the two disciples), it remains in the ownership of its owner so that he had the right to take it, and it is valuable property, so he is liable for it as tanned by destruction, and the owner gives him the increase due to tanning, just as if one usurps a garment and dyes it then destroys it, he is liable for it and the owner gives him the increase from the dye. And because it was obligatory to return, so when he caused its loss, its value substitutes, as in a borrowed thing. By this it differs from perishing by itself. Their statement 'he gives the increase due to tanning' is interpreted as when the species differ; but when the species is the same, that amount is deducted and the remainder taken, because there is no benefit in taking and then returning. [Explanation in Urdu: The text elaborates on the dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa says only vinegar is guaranteed, not tanned leather; Sahibayn say tanned leather is guaranteed with compensation for the increase from tanning. If the leather perishes on its own, by consensus there is no guarantee. The reasoning for vinegar is that it remains in the owner's ownership as valuable property, so destruction necessitates liability, and because it is a mithli (fungible) item, its like is owed. For leather, Sahibayn argue it remains in the owner's ownership and is valuable, so the usurper is liable for it as tanned, and the owner pays for the added value. This is analogous to dyed cloth. Also, since returning it was obligatory, destroying it necessitates substituting its value, like a borrowed item. Thus it differs from perishing without the usurper's action. The phrase 'he gives the increase' applies when the two compensations are of different species (e.g., one in dirhams, the other in dinars); if the same species, the amount is simply deducted without exchange. Further, the text notes that the consensus referred to by the author of al-Hidayah is the consensus of the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), not the consensus of the entire Ummah. The views of the three Imams of other schools (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad) are also mentioned: if wine becomes vinegar by itself and remains in the hand of the usurper, he is liable; if by his action, he is not liable. Regarding tanned leather, there are two opinions: he is not obligated to return it nor liable, or he must return it and is liable.]
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the liability (daman) for usurped property that undergoes transformation. The specific case: a usurper takes wine (which becomes vinegar) or a raw hide (which becomes tanned leather) and then destroys it before returning. The central disagreement is between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (collectively known as Sahibayn).

Imam Abu Hanifa holds that if the usurper destroys these transformed items, he is liable only for the vinegar, but not for the tanned leather. The reasoning: vinegar was originally the owner's valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) and remained in his ownership throughout; even when it was impure wine, ownership did not cease. Upon becoming vinegar, it regained its value and thus destruction triggers liability. Since vinegar is a fungible good (mithli), the usurper must return its like. The tanned leather, however, does not trigger liability under Abu Hanifa's view because the act of tanning is considered a separate, permissible action that adds value, and the original ownership over the hide is not fully clear regarding the increase.

The Sahibayn argue that the tanned leather also remains in the owner's ownership and is valuable, so the usurper is liable for it as tanned. Additionally, the owner must compensate the usurper for the increase in value due to tanning. They analogize this to a dyed cloth: if someone usurps a garment, dyes it (adding value), then destroys it, he is liable for the dyed garment, and the owner pays for the increase from the dye. Furthermore, since the leather was obligatory to return (wajib al-radd), destroying it means its value must be substituted, similar to a borrowed item (musta'ar) which must be returned or its value paid.

The text then clarifies a nuance: the Sahibayn's statement that 'the owner gives the increase due to tanning' applies only when the compensations are of different species (e.g., the leather's value calculated in dirhams and the tanning increase in dinars). If they are of the same species, there is no need to physically exchange; rather, the usurper's liability is reduced by the amount of the increase, and he pays the remainder.

A key distinction is made between destruction by the usurper's act and when the item perishes on its own (e.g., the leather spoils without his action). In the latter case, there is consensus (ijma') among the Hanafi imams that no liability exists. The commentary from ashraf al-Hidayah notes that the 'consensus' here refers to the agreement of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, not the consensus of the entire Muslim community, because ijma' of all mujtahids of one era is not proven in this specific issue.

The passage also briefly mentions the views of the three other leading imams (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad): if wine becomes vinegar by itself while in the usurper's hand, he is liable; if by his action (e.g., adding salt), he is not. For tanned leather, there are two opinions: either he is not obliged to return it and not liable, or he must return it and is liable. This inter-madhhab comparison enriches the discussion but the focus remains on the Hanafi intra-madhhab difference.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; the obligation to compensate for loss or destruction of another's property.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and permissible to benefit from.
مثلي (mithli): Fungible goods that have standard equivalents in the market, such that compensation is by like (mithl) rather than value.
قيمي (qimi): Non-fungible goods that require compensation by market value (qimah) because they lack standard equivalents.
استهلاك (istihlak): Destruction or consumption of property, triggering liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa regarding liability for tanned leather if the usurper destroys it after tanning?
A) The usurper is liable for the tanned leather and must pay its value.
B) The usurper is not liable for the tanned leather, only for the vinegar.
C) The usurper is liable only if the leather tanned by itself.
D) The usurper is liable but the owner must pay for the increase due to tanning.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, usurped property remains in the owner's ownership even after transformation; liability for destruction depends on whether the transformation adds value, with differences between Abu Hanifa and his disciples on tanned leather.

Liability for Destroying Musical Instruments and Intoxicants
Commentary on a dispute from Kitab al-Ghasb of al-Hidayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi dispute on liability for destroying items used for sin, explain the proofs of both sides, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Breaking sinful items: Abu Hanifa says pay value; Sahibayn says no liability – the fatwa follows Sahibayn.

This lesson examines a classical Hanafi dispute: if you break someone's musical instrument or spill their wine, are you liable? The answer depends on whether the item retains property value (taqawwum) or is exclusively sinful. We will explore the proofs from Quran, hadith, and qiyas, and see how the later school settled on the Sahibayn's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Positions
State Abu Hanifa's position (liable) and the Sahibayn's position (not liable). Note that the three Imams and general scholars agree with Sahibayn.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab.
2
Examine the Sahibayn's Proof
Understand their main argument: these items are exclusively for sin, so taqawwum is void. Destroying them is an act of forbidding evil, supported by hadith: 'I was sent to break musical instruments' and 'Whoever sees an evil, let him change it with his hand.'
They analogize to acting with the Imam's permission.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's Proof
Understand his argument: these items have potential for lawful benefit (e.g., duff for weddings, intoxicants for industry). Corruption is from the user's choice, not the object. The hadith about changing evil by hand is for rulers, not individuals.
He uses qiyas with the singing slave girl and other dual-use objects.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Later authorities (Jami' al-Abilyasr, al-Kafi, etc.) give fatwa on Sahibayn's view: no liability. This is the operative position for fatwa today.
However, for items with clear lawful use (e.g., war drum), liability is unanimous.

دے یا اس کی سکریا منصف شراب بہادے تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک وہ ضامن ہوگا اور ان اشیاء کی بیع جائز ہے صاحبین ، ائمہ ثلاثہ اور عام علماء کے نزدیک وہ ضامن نہ ہوگا اور ان اشیاء کی بیع بھی نا جائز ہے۔ قوله وقيل الاختلاف ..... الخ - بعض علماء نے کہا ہے کہ اختلاف مذکور اس دف اور طبل میں ہے جو لہو کے لئے بجایا جاتا ہے۔ رہا غازیوں کا طبل اور نکاح کا دف سو اس کے توڑنے میں بلا خلاف ضامن ہوگا۔ لیکن ذخیرہ میں فقیہ ابواللیث کا قول منقول ہے کہ نکاح کا دف بجانا علماء کے یہاں مختلف فیہ ہے۔ بعض نے اس کو مکر وہ کہا ہے اور بعض نے مکروہ نہیں کہا۔ رہا وہ دف جو ہمارے زمانے میں صبیحات و جلا جات ( خوب صورت بچوں اور گھونگھروں ) سے بجایا جاتا ہے سو یہ مکروہ (حرام ) ہونا چاہیے اور شیخ عتابی نے شرح جامع صغیر میں کہا ہے کہ اگر حج کے شکار کایا کسی بچہ کا ڈھول ہو جو وہ گھر میں بجاتا ہے تو اس کے توڑنے سے بالا تفاق ضامن ہوگا۔ قوله وقيل الفتوى .... الخ - جامع ابوالیسر، کافی، منح الدر المنقی، زیلعی قہستانی ، بر جندی وغیرہ میں ہے کہ فتوی صاحبین کے قول پر ہے کہ بابے توڑنے والا اور سکر و منصف بہانے والا ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ قولهما ان هذه الاشياء .... الخ - صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ چیزیں معصیت کے لئے ہوتی ہیں۔ اس لئے ان کا تقوم ساقط ہے۔ نیز اس نے جو کچھ کیا ہے وہ منشاء شارع کے موافق کیا ہے۔
قال عليه الصلوة والسلام "بعثت لكسر المزامير وقتل الخنازیر اس کے ساتھ ساتھ اس نے توڑ کرامر بالمعروف ونهى عن المنکر کا حق ادا کیا ہے۔
قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم اذا رأى احدكم منكرا فليغيره بيده فان لم يستطع فبلسانه فان لم يستطع فبقلبه و ذلك اضعف الايمان وما على المحسنين سبيل
اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے اگر وہ امام یعنی نائب شرع کے حکم سے ایسا کرے تو بالا تفاق ضامن نہیں ہوتا۔ چنانچہ فتاوی صغریٰ کی کتاب الجنایات کے آخر میں ہے۔ کسردن الخمر ان کان باذن الامام لا يضمن والاضمن امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل
و لابي حنيفة أنها موال الصلاحيتها لما يحل من وجوه الانتفاع و ان صلحت لما لا يحل فصار كالامة المغنية و هذا لأن الفساد بفعل فاعل مختار فلا يوجب سقوط التقوم و جواز البيع والتضمين مرتبان على المالية والتقوم والامر بالمعروف باليد الى الامراء لقدرتهم وباللسان الى غيرهم و تجب قيمتها غير صالحة للهو كما فى الجارية المغنية والكبش النطوح والحمامة الطيارة والديك المقاتل والعبد الخصى تجب قيمة غير صالحة لهذه الأمور كذا هذا و فى السكر والمنصف تجب قيمتها و لا يجب المثل لان المسلم ممنوع عن تملیک عینه و ان كان لو فعل جاز و هذا بخلاف ما اذا اتلف على نصراني صليبا حيث يضمن قيمته صليبا لانه مقر على ذلك -
If someone gives wine or an intoxicating drink (sukr or munsif) to another, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, he is liable (damin) and the sale of these items is permissible. According to the Sahibayn, the three Imams (al-Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad), and general scholars, he is not liable and the sale of these items is not permissible. 
Statement: 'And it is said: the disagreement...' - Some scholars said that the mentioned disagreement concerns the duff and drum (tabl) that are played for amusement. As for the drum of warriors (tabl al-ghuzat) and the duff of marriage (duff al-nikah), breaking them will certainly incur liability (without disagreement). However, in al-Dhakhirah, the saying of Faqih Abu al-Layth is transmitted that playing the duff of marriage is disputed among scholars. Some considered it makruh, others did not. As for the duff played in our time by beautiful children and bells, it should be makruh (haram). Shaykh al-ʿAttabi said in Sharh al-Jamiʿ al-Saghir that if it is a drum of a child that he plays at home, breaking it will definitely incur liability. 
Statement: 'And it is said: the fatwa...' - In Jamiʿ al-Abilyasr, al-Kafi, Manh al-Durr al-Muntaqa, Zaylaʿi, Quhistani, Barjandi, etc., the fatwa is according to the saying of the Sahibayn: the one who breaks the duff and the one who destroys intoxicants is not liable. 
The proof of the Sahibayn (qawlahuma) is that these things are for sin; therefore their taqawwum (property value) is void. Moreover, what he did is in accordance with the intent of the Lawgiver. The Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'I was sent to break musical instruments and kill pigs.' Along with that, by breaking, he fulfills the duty of enjoining good and forbidding evil. The Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'When any of you sees an evil, let him change it with his hand; if he cannot, then with his tongue; if he cannot, then with his heart, and that is the weakest of faith.' And there is no blame on those who do good. This is like the case where if he does it by order of the Imam (deputy of the Shariʿa), he is definitely not liable. Indeed, at the end of Kitab al-Jinayat in Fatawa al-Sughra it is stated: 'Breaking wine if done with the permission of the Imam – he is not liable; otherwise, he is liable.' 
The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa: 'And for Abu Hanifa (that these items have potential for lawful benefit, even though they are also fit for unlawful benefit, so they become like the singing slave girl. This is because corruption occurs through the act of a free agent, so it does not cause the dropping of taqawwum. The permissibility of sale and liability are based on value (maliyyah) and taqawwum. Enjoining good with the hand is for the rulers (umara'), due to their power, and with the tongue for others. Their liability is the value (qimah) that is not suited for amusement, just as for the singing slave girl, the butting ram, the flying pigeon, the fighting rooster, and the castrated slave, their liability is the value that is not suited for these matters. Similarly, for sukr and munsif, their liability is the value (qimah), not the like (mithl), because a Muslim is prohibited from owning the substance of these items, even if it is permissible if he does it. This is contrary to the case of destroying a cross belonging to a Christian, where he guarantees its value as a cross because he is confirmed (muqarr) upon that.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya is a commentary on the Kitab al-Ghasb (Book of Usurpation) of al-Hidaya. It presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute (and broader inter-madhhab dispute) about liability when someone destroys objects typically used for sinful purposes, such as musical instruments (duff, tabla) and intoxicants (sukr, munsif). The author first states the ruling: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the destroyer is liable (damin) and the sale of such items is valid. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), the three Imams (al-Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad), and the generality of scholars, he is not liable and the sale is invalid. The commentary then explores the scope of the disagreement: some scholars said it applies only to duff and drum used for amusement; for the warrior's drum and wedding duff, liability is unanimous. However, Faqih Abu al-Layth notes that playing the wedding duff is itself disputed (some consider it makruh, others not). The duff played in the author's time with beautiful children and bells should be makruh (haram). Shaykh al-ʿAttabi said a child's drum played at home certainly incurs liability upon destruction. The passage then clarifies that the fatwa (operative legal opinion) is on the Sahibayn's view: the breaker is not liable. This is affirmed by many later sources. The Sahibayn's proof is that these items are exclusively for sin, thus they have no property value (taqawwum) in the Shariʿa. Moreover, by destroying them, one enforces the command of forbidding evil, as supported by hadith: 'I was sent to break musical instruments and kill pigs' and 'Whoever sees an evil, let him change it with his hand.' The destroyer is like one acting on the Imam's order, in which case liability is dropped. Abu Hanifa's proof is that these items still have potential for lawful benefit (e.g., a duff can be used for a lawful purpose like wedding, though that is disputed; intoxicants can be used for industrial purposes). Therefore they retain property value (taqawwum). Corruption arises from the free choice of the user, not from the object itself. So destroying them without legal authority makes one liable for their value (qimah) - but not the 'like' (mithl), because a Muslim is forbidden from owning the substance of intoxicants. This is analogous to the singing slave girl, butting ram, flying pigeon, fighting rooster, and castrated slave: liability is for the value that excludes the prohibited attribute. For a Christian's cross, full value as a cross is due because the Christian is allowed to own it. The commentary elaborates that the liable value is calculated based on the object's potential for lawful use, not its use in sin. Faqih Abu al-Layth added that for a drum or sitarngi, only the value of the carved wood is due. In summary, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the Sahibayn: no liability, because such items lack taqawwum and the destroyer is executing a religious duty.

Key Terms
ضامن (dāmin): Legally liable to pay compensation (daman) for destruction or damage of property.
تقوم (taqawwum): The quality of being regarded as property with legal value in the Shari'a; items lacking taqawwum are not compensable.
صاحبین (al-Ṣāḥibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two major disciples of Imam Abu Hanifa.
سکر (sukr): A specific intoxicating drink (possibly wine or a strong beverage).
منصف (munṣif): A type of intoxicating drink, likely a mixture that is half wine and half something else, or a specific beverage.
دف (duff): A frame drum or tambourine, often used in music.
طبل (ṭabl): A drum, larger than a duff, used in military or musical contexts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) in the text, if someone breaks a duff (tambourine) that is commonly used for amusement, is he liable for its value?
A) Yes, he must pay the full value
B) No, he is not liable because the duff has no property value (taqawwum) and he is acting to forbid evil
C) Yes, but only if the owner is a Muslim
D) No, unless he acted without the intention of forbidding evil

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school is that destroying musical instruments or intoxicants used exclusively for sin does not incur liability, because they lack property value and the destruction is an act of forbidding evil.
